  On the profit=revenue - cost example being too simple, we are essentially tying to solve the profit problem by improving operation or new revenue sources (and limited ourselves in that direction), assuming that the segment we are in can be "profitable".   In terms of hierarchy (or order of the problems), it would be something like this:

  1. Are we operating in a "profitable" market segment?  (hidden question, question not asked)

  2. Within this segment, can we improve our profit?



  Sometimes we assume the answer to Q1 is yes, and therefore we move straight to Q2. It's worth verifying this before attempting Q2 (even though Q2 is the problem assigned by the owner). He/she might not realise that the segment is no longer profitable (for example, not aware that young people nowadays spend more time online with friends than in person).



  At problem definition stage, we should ask the problem owner or ourselves: why this question, what led to this question, and what are the other questions related to it? This is similar to the first diamond in the Design Thinking approach, where at the Empathise stage, one explores before concluding on the right pain point to address.

# Concepts
## BPR
  **What is BPR?**
  Business Process Reengineering (BPR) is about throwing away the old rulebook of how work is done and starting fresh. The idea, popularized by Michael Hammer in the 1990 Harvard Business Review article *“Don’t Automate, Obliterate”*, is that most of the tasks inside companies don’t add real value to customers. Instead of just automating those tasks with technology, firms should **radically redesign the process from the ground up**.

  **Why is it radical?**
  Unlike methods like DMAIC (which focus on incremental improvements, usually <10%), BPR aims for **dramatic performance jumps** (>50%) in cost, quality, service, or speed. It’s like redesigning the road system of a city rather than just fixing potholes.

  **Why is it difficult?**

  * Processes are often invisible and spread across departments, so nobody sees the whole picture.
  * People resist change — sticking to old habits is easier.
  * Managers sometimes misuse BPR as a cover for mass layoffs, earning it a bad reputation for dehumanising workplaces.

  **The Framework (Kettinger, Teng & Guha 1997)**
  After comparing 25+ BPR methodologies, they distilled a **six-stage framework**:

  1. **Envision**: Create a vision of where the business should go.
  2. **Initiate**: Build commitment, form teams, and plan.
  3. **Diagnose**: Study current processes (as-is), benchmark, measure.
  4. **Redesign**: Generate and prototype “to-be” processes.
  5. **Reconstruct**: Implement the redesigned process with IT, training, and organizational changes.
  6. **Evaluate**: Measure outcomes, audit performance, and connect to continuous improvement.

  **Tools & Techniques mapped to each stage**:

  * **Business Planning**: Benchmarking, Competitive Analysis, Value-Chain Analysis, Cost/Benefit Analysis.
  * **Diagnosis**: Process Flowcharting, Activity Based Costing (ABC), Fishbone, Pareto, Surveys.
  * **Redesign**: Brainstorming, Affinity Diagram, Simulation, Workflow Design, Prototyping.
  * **Implementation**: Project Scheduling (PERT/CPM, Gantt), Training, Conversion techniques.
  * **Evaluation**: Audits, SPC (Statistical Process Control), QFD (Quality Function Deployment).

  **Role of IT**
  BPR is tightly linked with IT as an enabler. For example:

  * EDI (electronic data interchange) to cut out intermediaries in supply chains.
  * Groupware to connect people directly.
  * Robotics to automate physical processes.
  * AI and databases to inject information into decisions.

  **Key Takeaways**

  * BPR = **radical change** vs DMAIC = **incremental improvement**.
  * Needs **top-management support**, cross-functional teams, and heavy use of IT.
  * Success depends on **managing change and people**, not just drawing new processes.
  * Provides a **structured toolbox** (over 70 techniques, 100+ tools catalogued in the paper).

  **In short:** BPR is bold, disruptive surgery for organizations — risky, but potentially transformative.

  **中文解释**

  **什么是业务流程再造 (BPR)?**
  业务流程再造就是把公司运作的“旧规则书”扔掉，从零开始重新设计流程。1990 年，哈佛商业评论上 Michael Hammer 写了一篇著名文章《不要自动化，要彻底重构》。他认为，企业里做的大部分工作对客户没有真正价值。与其用技术去加速这些无价值的工作，不如彻底删掉，重新设计流程。

  **为什么说它“激进”?**
  和 DMAIC 这种追求小步改进（通常小于 10%）的方法不同，BPR 追求的是**大幅度的飞跃性改进**（超过 50%），比如成本、质量、服务或速度。它不是修补旧路，而是直接重建一套新的道路系统。

  **为什么难做?**

  * 流程往往横跨多个部门，谁都看不到全貌。
  * 人性本能抗拒改变，走老路最省力。
  * 有些管理层把 BPR 当作裁员的借口，导致它被批评是“新瓶装旧酒的泰勒主义”，使工作环境更不人性化。

  **六阶段框架（Kettinger, Teng & Guha, 1997 总结）**

  1. **愿景阶段**（Envision）：确立企业未来要达到的愿景和目标。
  2. **启动阶段**（Initiate）：高层表态支持，组建团队，做好项目规划。
  3. **诊断阶段**（Diagnose）：分析现有流程（As-Is），做基准比较和测量。
  4. **重设计阶段**（Redesign）：创造、原型化新的流程（To-Be）。
  5. **重建阶段**（Reconstruct）：落实新的流程，包括 IT 系统、培训、组织变革。
  6. **评估阶段**（Evaluate）：检查成果，做审计，和持续改进衔接起来。

  **信息技术的作用**
  IT 是 BPR 的关键推动力：

  * 电子数据交换 (EDI) 可以去掉中间商。
  * 协同软件 (Groupware) 直接连接人和人。
  * 机器人让生产流程自动化。
  * AI 和数据库能把大量信息引入决策中。

  **一句话总结：** BPR 是对组织的大手术，风险大，但成功了就能彻底转型。
## AEIOU observing framework
  - AEIOU is one of those deceptively simple design-thinking frameworks that helps you observe the world in a structured way so you don’t just see *what’s happening*, but also *why it matters*. It’s often used in ethnographic research, customer discovery, and innovation projects. The acronym stands for:
  * **A = Activities** What people are doing. The routines, actions, and processes you can see. Example: in a café, customers order, wait, scroll their phones, chat, sip coffee.
  * **E = Environment** The physical, digital, or social context where the activity takes place. What surrounds people? Example: is the café cozy, noisy, cramped, full of power sockets, or dominated by a big ordering counter?
  * **I = Interactions** How people engage with others, objects, or systems. Example: customer ↔ cashier, customer ↔ payment machine, friends chatting, or even customer ↔ coffee cup.
  * **O = Objects** The physical or digital artifacts in use. These can be tools, devices, or even props. Example: menus, coffee cups, mobile apps, loyalty cards, laptops.
  * **U = Users** The people you’re observing — their roles, goals, demographics, and behaviors. Example: students working on laptops, office workers grabbing takeaway, families lingering with kids.

  The magic of AEIOU is that it forces you to widen your lens. Instead of just watching “what a person does,” you also notice *the stuff, the space, the relationships, and the subtle cues*.

  Design thinkers often use AEIOU observation grids: a table with five columns (A–E–I–O–U) where you jot down what you see. Later, you can mine this grid for insights, spot unmet needs, or frame challenge statements.

  It’s a cousin of **customer journey mapping** and **service blueprinting**, but AEIOU is lighter weight — a portable notebook method for early discovery work.

  Would you like me to sketch a sample **AEIOU observation grid** (say, for a university library or a gym) so you can see how it works in practice?
## MECE
  - Mutually Exclusive Collectively Exhaustive 简称 **MECE**。这是一个常用在管理咨询、战略分析里的逻辑框架。
    * **Mutually Exclusive（互斥）**：意思是分类之间不重叠，每个元素只能属于一个类别。比如把人分为“男性”和“女性”，一个人不会同时在两个组里。
    * **Collectively Exhaustive（完全穷尽）**：意思是所有可能的情况都被覆盖了，不会漏掉。比如分类时既有“男性”也有“女性”，那就把所有人都包括进去了。

  合起来，MECE 就是要求我们在分类、拆解问题时：

  1. **不重叠**（避免重复计算/重复考虑）；
  2. **不遗漏**（避免有盲点）。

  举个例子：
  要分析一家电商的客户群体，如果分为「新用户」和「老用户」，这就是 MECE，因为：

  * 一个用户不可能同时既是新用户又是老用户（互斥）。
  * 所有用户都在新用户或老用户里（穷尽）。

  但如果你分成「学生用户」和「老用户」，就不是 MECE，因为：

  * 有人可能既是学生又是老用户（不互斥）。
  * 还可能有既不是学生也不是老用户的顾客（不穷尽）。

  这套方法常被麦肯锡、BCG 之类的咨询公司用来拆解问题。它的核心价值是**逻辑清晰、无遗漏、无重复**。
## Fishbone Diagram (Ishikawa diagram)
  - the standard main “bones” are the 6Ms:
    - Man (People) – skills, training, fatigue, motivation.
    - Machine – equipment, tools, maintenance.
    - Method – procedures, instructions, process design.
    - Material – raw materials, consumables, quality.
    - Measurement – inspection, data accuracy, metrics.
    - Mother Nature (Environment) – temperature, humidity, workspace.
***

# Lectures
## Week 2 Topic 2 lecture 08.21
  - [18:13 - 18:17] **Introduction to DMAIC and the MOA Framework**

    We’ll likely come back to creativity later in the course, but first, let’s talk about the three problem-solving methodologies in general. Any questions about the video or topics so far? You’ve probably seen some of this background already. As noted in your handout for MT 5021, DMAIC is a problem-solving methodology of the Six Sigma movement that Motorola popularized in the late 1980s, though its roots actually lie in Total Quality Management (TQM), which was developed in Japan earlier in that decade. Do you understand what these concepts mean? What does performance mean to you in this context? There’s a foundational theory I want to discuss called MOA, which stands for Motivation, Opportunity, and Ability. Imagine you’re incredibly motivated, but you are not chosen to represent Singapore at the Olympics. You simply aren’t given the opportunity, so you won't win a medal, no matter how motivated you are. Or, consider the alternative: maybe you are highly motivated and you do get the opportunity to compete, but you just don’t have the intrinsic ability—you simply can’t run fast enough. Again, the result is no performance. So, the MOA framework dictates that for true performance to happen, all three of these factors must align. If even one is missing, the end result is severely constrained. It works exactly like multiplication, not addition: Performance = Motivation x Opportunity x Ability. If you have values of 0.8 x 0.8 x 0.8, you end up with a very small outcome. One weak factor drags the entire result down.

    我们稍后可能会回到关于创造力的话题，但首先，让我们总体谈谈这三种解决问题的方法论。关于视频或目前的主题有什么问题吗？你们可能已经看过其中的一些背景资料了。正如你们在 MT 5021 的讲义中所见，DMAIC 是摩托罗拉在 20 世纪 80 年代末普及的六西格玛运动中的一种问题解决方法，尽管它的根源实际上在于 80 年代初日本开发的全面质量管理（TQM）。你们理解这些概念的含义吗？在这种背景下，绩效对你们意味着什么？我想讨论一个被称为 MOA 的基础理论，它代表动机 (Motivation)、机会 (Opportunity) 和能力 (Ability)。想象一下你非常有动力，但你没有被选中代表新加坡参加奥运会。你根本没有得到这个机会，所以无论你多么有动力，你都不会赢得奖牌。或者，考虑另一种情况：也许你非常有动力，并且你也确实得到了比赛的机会，但你就是缺乏内在的能力——你跑得不够快。同样，结果也是没有绩效。因此，MOA 框架表明，要实现真正的绩效，这三个因素必须全部对齐。如果缺少任何一个，最终结果就会受到严重限制。它的运作方式完全像乘法，而不是加法：绩效 = 动机 x 机会 x 能力。如果你有 0.8 x 0.8 x 0.8 的值，你最终会得到一个非常小的结果。一个薄弱的因素会拖垮整个结果。

  - [18:17 - 18:24] **Total Quality Management and the Power of Shop Floor Operators**

    So, what’s truly special about TQM and the DMAIC methodology? If you search online or ask an AI tool, you’ll likely come across case studies from ASQ award winners, such as Jabil Singapore and Sime Darby Malaysia. Unlike design thinking projects that might feature teams from Stanford and highly educated, elite consultants, many of the teams competing for these quality awards were comprised of everyday shop floor operators. These were people with perhaps an O-level education, and sometimes even less. By traditional societal standards, they weren't highly educated. However, because they were the ones physically running the machines across three shifts, they uniquely had the opportunity to observe problems and collect data on a daily basis. They were also intrinsically motivated; it taps into the basic human desire where, no matter the job, people take immense pride in solving problems and improving their work environment. What DMAIC provided them was the ability. It gave them a well-defined methodology and structured tools to solve problems systematically, allowing it to be taught to a wide group of people. A typical DMAIC team might only have one or two engineers, but the vast majority were the operators themselves. Solving problems by the problem owners—the workers themselves—greatly increases the likelihood of acceptance, which stands in stark contrast to externally imposed changes like Business Process Reengineering. Instead of relying on a few elite consultants, DMAIC leveraged an army of small teams, each tasked with solving 3% to 5% of the problems. Cumulatively, this grassroots approach had a massive impact on overall operations. That’s exactly why strategic alignment is so critical. However, the problems to be addressed must be aligned with the company's mission. Every small DMAIC team’s work must connect directly to the overarching corporate goals. If the company’s primary objective is achieving the lowest cost, all those small teams must focus their efforts on cost reduction. Otherwise, if some teams are trying to improve quality, others are adding features, and others are cutting costs, the collective, cumulative impact severely weakens. DMAIC ensures that alignment coupled with cumulative impact actually materializes.

    那么，TQM 和 DMAIC 方法论真正特别的地方在哪里呢？如果你在网上搜索或询问 AI 工具，你很可能会看到 ASQ 获奖者的案例研究，例如 Jabil Singapore 和 Sime Darby Malaysia。与可能由来自斯坦福大学的团队和受过高等教育的精英顾问组成的设计思维项目不同，许多竞争这些质量奖项的团队是由普通的车间操作员组成的。这些人可能只有 O 水准的教育程度，甚至更低。按照传统的社会标准，他们并没有受过高等教育。然而，因为他们是每天三班倒实际操作机器的人，他们独特地拥有每天观察问题和收集数据的机会。他们也有内在的动力；它触及了人类的基本愿望，即无论从事什么工作，人们都对解决问题和改善工作环境感到无比自豪。DMAIC 提供给他们的是能力。它给了他们一个明确的方法论和结构化的工具来系统地解决问题，使其能够教给广泛的人群。一个典型的 DMAIC 团队可能只有一两名工程师，但绝大多数是操作员自己。由问题所有者（即工人自己）解决问题大大增加了被接受的可能性，这与业务流程重组等外部强加的变化形成鲜明对比。DMAIC 没有依赖少数精英顾问，而是利用了一支由小型团队组成的大军，每个团队的任务是解决 3% 到 5% 的问题。累积起来，这种草根方法对整体运营产生了巨大的影响。这正是战略对齐如此关键的原因。然而，要解决的问题必须与公司的使命保持一致。每个小型 DMAIC 团队的工作必须直接与公司的总体目标联系起来。如果公司的首要目标是实现最低成本，那么所有这些小团队都必须将精力集中在降低成本上。否则，如果一些团队试图提高质量，另一些团队在增加功能，还有一些团队在削减成本，那么集体的、累积的影响就会严重减弱。DMAIC 确保了对齐加上累积影响真正得以实现。

  - [18:24 - 18:29] **The Define Stage and Crafting Problem Statements**

    Moving into the specific steps, the first stage in DMAIC is Define. In any organization, there are often many possible projects you could tackle, so the immediate challenge is deciding which one to focus on. To do this, teams typically use a project ranking matrix to evaluate options based on factors like low CapEx requirements, high Return on Investment (ROI), and short implementation duration. Once a specific project is chosen, the team must zoom in. For example, if the broad goal is reducing chemical costs, they need to identify exactly which material is driving the biggest portion of that cost and then target it. Sometimes, the problem is explicitly pre-determined by management, such as a directive to "reduce cost for this specific machine." Other times, it’s much more open-ended, like a vague instruction to "improve efficiency somewhere in the plant." The level of ambiguity in the problem definition really matters here: more freedom means more searching is required to uncover the true underlying problem. This is exactly why defining the problem is often considered the hardest skill to master. A truly good consultant is someone who can take vague client requests and reframe them into precise, impactful problem statements. Good problem statements must be SMART: Specific, Measurable, Achievable, Realistic, and Time-bound. Without this rigorous definition, teams and managers will inevitably end up arguing down the line about whether the problem was actually "solved" or not.

    进入具体步骤，DMAIC 的第一阶段是定义 (Define)。在任何组织中，通常有许多可能的项目可以解决，因此眼前的挑战是决定关注哪一个。为此，团队通常使用项目排名矩阵，根据低资本支出要求、高投资回报率 (ROI) 和短实施周期等因素来评估选项。一旦选定了特定项目，团队就必须放大细节。例如，如果宏观目标是降低化学品成本，他们需要准确找出哪种材料是导致该成本最大部分的原因，然后将其作为目标。有时，问题是由管理层明确预先确定的，比如指示“降低这台特定机器的成本”。其他时候，它更加开放，比如一个模糊的指令“提高工厂某处的效率”。问题定义中的模糊程度在这里非常重要：更多的自由意味着需要更多的搜索来发现真正潜在的问题。这正是为什么定义问题通常被认为是最难掌握的技能。一个真正优秀的顾问是能够接受模糊的客户要求并将其重新构建为精确、有影响力的陈述的人。好的问题陈述必须是 SMART 的：具体的 (Specific)、可衡量的 (Measurable)、可实现的 (Achievable)、现实的 (Realistic) 和有时限的 (Time-bound)。如果没有这种严格的定义，团队和管理层不可避免地会在以后就是否真正“解决”了问题而争论不休。

  - [18:29 - 18:45] **Measure and Analyze Stages using Factory-Born Tools**

    After you firmly Define the problem, the next stage is Measure. This is where you establish the current baseline and rigorously quantify the performance gaps. Following that is the Analyze stage. This relies heavily on fact-based decision making, which is one of the eight key principles of TQM. Here, teams use specific analytical tools to uncover root causes. A classic example is the Fishbone diagram, also known as the Ishikawa diagram, which categorizes potential causes into Man, Machine, Material, and Method—often extended to include things like Measurement and Mother Nature. Other critical tools include Cause-effect matrices and FMEA, which stands for Failure Mode and Effect Analysis. FMEA is a highly systematic approach where you calculate a risk priority number by scoring Severity x Frequency x Detectability. The goal is to reduce overall risk by implementing changes that lower any of those three factors. It is vital to remember that these are inherently factory-born tools, meaning they map exceptionally well to manufacturing settings and physical production environments. While you certainly can adapt them for service industries or software, context matters immensely, and they might feel rigid compared to methods designed for emotional or user-experience problems.

    在你坚定地定义了问题之后，下一个阶段是衡量 (Measure)。在这里你要建立当前的基线并严格量化绩效差距。紧随其后的是分析 (Analyze) 阶段。这严重依赖于基于事实的决策，这是 TQM 的八个关键原则之一。在这里，团队使用特定的分析工具来发现根本原因。一个经典的例子是鱼骨图，也称为石川图，它将潜在原因分类为人 (Man)、机 (Machine)、料 (Material) 和法 (Method)——通常扩展到包括测量 (Measurement) 和环境 (Mother Nature) 等。其他关键工具包括因果矩阵和 FMEA，它代表失效模式与影响分析。FMEA 是一种高度系统化的方法，你可以通过评估严重度 x 频度 x 可探测度来计算风险优先级数。目标是通过实施降低这三个因素中任何一个的改变来降低整体风险。至关重要的是要记住，这些本质上是诞生于工厂的工具，这意味着它们非常适合制造业和实体生产环境。虽然你当然可以对它们进行调整以适应服务业或软件业，但背景环境非常重要，并且与专为情感或用户体验问题设计的方法相比，它们可能会显得有些僵化。

  - [18:45 - 18:55] **The Improve Stage and Prioritization**

    Moving on to the Improve stage, this is where teams actively brainstorm potential solutions. They might use affinity diagrams or solution trees to organize their ideas. Once they have a list of potential fixes, they must prioritize them by assessing the required effort versus the expected return. We can break this down into a simple matrix: if a solution requires low effort but yields a high return, you absolutely do that first. If a proposed solution requires high effort but only offers a low return, you should avoid it entirely. For ideas that demand high effort but also promise high returns, whether you proceed depends entirely on your available resources and project timeline. Finally, you have the low effort, low return tasks. You might think these should be ignored, but they are often pursued for securing small, quick wins to boost team morale, or because they are necessary for strict regulatory compliance. Getting those quick wins really matters because they build team momentum and confidence. However, compliance tasks, such as meeting new safety regulations, simply must be done regardless of whether the ROI is low or high.

    接下来是改进 (Improve) 阶段，团队在这里积极集思广益，寻找潜在的解决方案。他们可能会使用亲和图或解决方案树来组织他们的想法。一旦他们有了一个潜在修复方案的列表，他们就必须通过评估所需的努力与预期的回报来对它们进行优先级排序。我们可以将其分解为一个简单的矩阵：如果一个解决方案需要较少的努力但能产生高回报，你绝对要首先做这件事。如果提议的解决方案需要付出巨大努力但只提供很低的回报，你应该完全避免它。对于那些需要付出巨大努力但也承诺高回报的想法，你是否继续完全取决于你的可用资源和项目时间表。最后，你还有低努力、低回报的任务。你可能认为这些应该被忽略，但追求它们通常是为了获得小而快的胜利以提高团队士气，或者因为它们对于严格的合规性是必要的。获得那些快速的胜利真的很重要，因为它们建立了团队的动力和信心。然而，合规性任务，如满足新的安全法规，不管投资回报率是低还是高，都必须完成。

  - [18:55 - 19:49] **The Control Stage and Institutionalizing Change**

    The final and arguably most critical stage is Control. This is entirely about sustaining your achieved results over time and institutionalizing the change. This stage is particularly crucial in complex factory environments that run around the clock with three shifts and multiple different crews. Without rigorous updates to Standard Operating Procedures (SOP) and mandatory retraining, all those hard-fought improvements will simply vanish as people inevitably revert to their old, comfortable habits. The tools utilized in this stage include rolling out SOP updates and conducting comprehensive training across all shifts so everyone is aligned. Another very powerful concept here is Pokayoke, or foolproofing. This involves physically designing processes or products so that it is impossible to make an error. Excellent examples of Pokayoke include heavy machinery that requires two-hand operation—meaning you literally have to press two buttons simultaneously, keeping both hands out of the danger zone—or something as everyday as a USB-C connector that fits smoothly no matter which way you plug it in. These intelligent design choices proactively prevent errors and physically enforce new habits. Ultimately, DMAIC heavily emphasizes this institutionalization—making the change a permanent fixture of the system, not just a temporary, one-off fix.

    最后也是可以说最关键的阶段是控制 (Control)。这完全是为了随着时间的推移维持你所取得的成果并将变革制度化。这个阶段在复杂多变的工厂环境中尤为重要，这些工厂通常是三班倒，有多个不同的班组全天候运转。如果没有对标准操作程序 (SOP) 进行严格的更新和强制性的重新培训，所有那些来之不易的改进都会随着人们不可避免地恢复到他们舒适的旧习惯而消失。这个阶段使用的工具包括推出 SOP 更新并在所有班组进行全面培训，以便每个人都保持一致。这里另一个非常强大的概念是 Pokayoke，即防呆设计。这涉及在物理上设计流程或产品，使其不可能犯错。防呆设计的优秀例子包括需要双手操作的重型机械——这意味着你必须同时按下两个按钮，从而使双手都远离危险区域——或者像 USB-C 连接器这样日常的东西，无论你以哪种方式插入它都能顺利连接。这些智能的设计选择主动防止了错误并在物理上强制执行了新的习惯。最终，DMAIC 非常强调这种制度化——使变革成为系统的永久固定部分，而不仅仅是暂时的、一次性的修复。

  - [19:49 - 20:21] **Fostering Creativity through Atomic Habits**

    I want to pivot slightly and connect this systemic problem-solving to personal development, specifically James Clear’s concepts from his book *Atomic Habits*. We've talked about how continuous improvement requires a steady flow of ideas. Habits truly stick when they are deeply tied to several core principles. First is Identity: if you tell yourself, "I am a reader," you are fundamentally more likely to read than if you just say "I want to read a book." Second is Environment cues: placing visible books on your desk or creating pleasant reading spaces naturally prompts the behavior. Third is the power of Small wins, like employing the 2-minute rule—committing to floss just one tooth or read just one single page to overcome inertia. Fourth is Rewards: human brains crave immediate satisfaction, not just a distant long-term gain, so you need to build in instant gratification. Finally, Belonging: joining reading clubs or peer groups provides crucial reinforcement. So, how does this relate to problem-solving? Creativity isn't magic; it comes from systematically collecting diverse "Lego bricks" of knowledge by reading across various domains. Later, when you are facing complex problems during an Improve stage, your brain can pull from this repository and recombine them into novel solutions. Cultivating habits like reading widely, allowing time for daydreaming, traveling to new places, and deep reflection are what build these invaluable bricks.

    我想稍微转移一下话题，将这种系统解决问题的方法与个人发展联系起来，特别是詹姆斯·克利尔在其著作《原子习惯》中的概念。我们讨论过持续改进需要源源不断的想法。当习惯与几个核心原则紧密相连时，它们才能真正坚持下去。首先是身份认同：如果你告诉自己，“我是一个阅读者”，你从根本上比你仅仅说“我想读一本书”更有可能去阅读。其次是环境提示：把显眼的书放在桌子上或创造舒适的阅读空间会自然而然地促发这种行为。第三是小胜利的力量，比如使用两分钟法则——承诺只用牙线清洁一颗牙齿或只读一页书来克服惰性。第四是奖励：人类的大脑渴望立即的满足感，而不仅仅是遥远的长期利益，所以你需要建立即时的满足感。最后是归属感：加入读书俱乐部或同伴群体提供了关键的强化。那么，这与解决问题有什么关系呢？创造力不是魔法；它来自于通过跨领域的阅读系统地收集各种知识的“乐高积木”。之后，当你在改进阶段面临复杂问题时，你的大脑可以从这个库中提取并在新颖的解决方案中重新组合它们。培养广泛阅读、留出时间做白日梦、去新地方旅行和深刻反思的习惯，正是构建这些无价积木的方法。

  - [20:21 - End] **Closing Remarks and Course Logistics**

    To close out today's session, I want to leave you with a few reminders. Please ensure you watch the Week 3 video ahead of time, which will focus heavily on business process improvement. Also, remember that we have a group exercise based on the ASQ slides you studied. You need to answer four specific DMAIC-related questions: What kind of problems are DMAIC suitable for? What are the specific steps and why are the stages named that way? What defines a successful outcome and what pitfalls should be avoided at each stage? And finally, what are the typical tools used, and how do they assist the method? Be prepared to present these in class. Lastly, as you work on these assignments, please use Generative AI critically. It can be a powerful tool, but you must double-check all references and do not blindly trust its hallucinated outputs. See you in class!

    在结束今天的课程之际，我想给大家留几个提醒。请确保你们提前观看第三周的视频，该视频将主要关注业务流程改进。此外，请记住，我们有一个基于你们学习的 ASQ 幻灯片的小组练习。你们需要回答四个与 DMAIC 相关的具体问题：DMAIC 适合什么样的问题？具体的步骤是什么，为什么这些阶段要这样命名？在每个阶段，什么定义了成功的结果，应该避免哪些陷阱？最后，通常使用哪些工具，它们是如何辅助该方法的？准备好在课堂上展示这些。最后，当你们完成这些作业时，请批判性地使用生成式 AI。它可以是一个强大的工具，但你们必须仔细检查所有参考文献，不要盲目相信它幻觉出来的输出。我们课堂上见！
## Week 3 Topic 3 lecture 08.28
  - [18:07 - 18:13] **Project Problem Statements and Issue Trees**

    A systematic approach allows you to check your problem comprehensively. You assess the significance of the possible causes contributing to the issue, and then you decide which one to tackle. If you look at your analysis, you might find that certain major culprits account for 48 percent or 47 percent of the problem. You need to work on these, because improving these key areas will yield a significant overall improvement. On the other hand, if you focus on a minor factor, even if you improve it by 100 percent, the overall impact won't be much. So, it is about tackling everything strategically. You also want to make sure you have the right competencies in your team to execute the solution. Importantly, you need to establish your baseline and clearly define how each of your proposed solutions will meet your objectives. For example, let's consider tax abandonment, or when people abandon a certain path. It is actually an interesting exercise to come up with an issue tree for this. When people abandon a path, do they do it willingly or are they forced into it? In the Singapore context, it turns out that people are often forced to move. If they suddenly have no money or lose their job, they have to move house due to housing restrictions. Some of these are financial reasons, some are non-financial reasons, and some people just change their minds because they want freedom to travel, so it's not forced. Another hypothetical problem statement could be: how do we reduce food waste in a hotel? You need to state the current situation, where you want to be, and your quantifiable objectives. Then you must check whether your data actually matches what you claim.

    系统性的方法能让你全面地审视问题。你需要评估导致该问题的可能原因的重要性，然后决定要着手解决哪一个。如果你看一下你的分析，你可能会发现某些主要元凶占了问题的 48% 或 47%。你需要致力于解决这些问题，因为改善这些关键领域将带来显著的整体提升。另一方面，如果你专注于一个次要因素，即使你将其改善了 100%，整体的影响也不会很大。所以，这关乎战略性地处理一切。你还需要确保你的团队具备执行解决方案的合适能力。重要的是，你需要建立基准，并清楚地界定你提出的每个解决方案将如何达成你的目标。例如，让我们考虑一下税务放弃，或者人们放弃某条路径的情况。为此画一个问题树实际上是一个很有趣的练习。当人们放弃一条路径时，他们是自愿的还是被迫的？在新加坡的背景下，结果发现人们通常是被迫搬家的。如果他们突然没钱或失业了，由于住房限制，他们不得不搬家。这其中有些是财务原因，有些是非财务原因，还有些人只是改变了主意，因为他们想要旅行的自由，所以这不是被迫的。另一个假设的问题陈述可能是：我们如何减少酒店的食物浪费？你需要陈述当前的情况、你想要达到的目标，以及你的可量化目标。然后你必须检查你的数据是否真的与你的主张相符。

  - [18:13 - 18:16] **Class Activity / Gap in Recording**

    (The class is engaged in an off-mic activity or reviewing process examples.)

    （全班正在进行非麦克风活动或回顾流程示例。）

  - [18:16 - 18:24] **Methodologies and Group Project Expectations**

    You can follow different frameworks. You can follow the generic DMAIC method, you can follow design thinking, or you can see other generic methods. It is entirely up to you. As long as I see a logical flow, I am fine with that. In fact, after this class and after a few rounds of practice, you should generally be able to apply problem-solving methodology without always having to refer to a very rigid, step-by-step framework. The first stage is always coming up with a good problem statement and defining the problem properly. The next stage is finding a way to analyze it, and during the analysis stage, you should avoid jumping to conclusions. Then, in the brainstorming stage, your focus is to come up with many solutions. When looking for a proxy to solve problems, you have to be clever about it. In your projects, you might look at background data to understand user behavior, like where people go and what they do at home. This gives you a sense of their career paths or the type of background you might attract. It's a very indirect way of checking, but it is relatively fast because you are going to explain it, and it is probably quite useful for this sort of applied research. Figure out the proxy to solve the problems.

    你们可以遵循不同的框架。你们可以遵循通用的 DMAIC 方法，可以遵循设计思维，或者看看其他通用的方法。这完全取决于你们。只要我看到逻辑流畅，我就觉得没问题。事实上，上完这门课并经过几轮练习后，你们通常应该能够应用解决问题的方法论，而不必总是参考非常死板、按部就班的框架。第一阶段始终是提出一个好的问题陈述并正确界定问题。下一阶段是找到分析它的方法，在分析阶段，你应该避免妄下结论。然后，在头脑风暴阶段，你们的重点是想出许多解决方案。在寻找代理指标来解决问题时，你必须聪明一点。在你们的项目中，你们可能会查看背景数据来了解用户行为，比如人们去哪里以及他们在家里做什么。这能让你了解他们的职业发展路径或你可能吸引的背景类型。这是一种非常间接的检查方式，但它相对较快，因为你们要去解释它，而且对于这种应用研究来说可能非常有用。找出解决问题的代理指标。

  - [18:24 - 18:26] **Selecting a Company Problem for the Project**

    The reason we emphasize experience is that as evaluators, we have seen and witnessed a lot more presentations than some of the students. So, I would expect a professional standard. What I will also do is give you resources; on the Internet, there are many examples of professional, very logical slides that you can use as a very good reference. I will pick out one website that has a lot of these excellent examples. For your group project, I suggest that you quickly look for a problem within your group that all of you agree on. Ideally, it should be a problem that one of you is currently facing at work. Pick a challenge that one of you is struggling with in your company, so that at the end of the semester, you not only solve the problem for your group project, but you can also present it back to your company. These days, companies are sometimes hesitant to share too much confidential data, but occasionally they are open to it. If your company is not comfortable sharing, then just don't name the company and don't use actual sensitive data. Just focus on the methodology and how you apply it.

    我们强调经验的原因是，作为评估者，我们看过和见证的演讲比一些学生多得多。所以，我期望能达到专业的标准。我还会给你们提供资源；在互联网上，有许多专业、非常有逻辑的幻灯片示例，你们可以将其作为很好的参考。我会挑选一个有很多这类优秀示例的网站。对于你们的小组项目，我建议你们在小组内尽快寻找一个大家都同意的问题。理想情况下，它应该是你们中某个人目前在工作中面临的问题。选择一个你们中某个人在公司里正在挣扎的挑战，这样在学期末，你们不仅为小组项目解决了问题，还可以把它展示给你们的公司。如今，公司有时不太愿意分享太多机密数据，但偶尔他们也会持开放态度。如果你的公司不方便分享，那就不要提公司的名字，也不要使用实际的敏感数据。只要专注于方法论以及你们如何应用它即可。

  - [18:26 - 18:30] **Introduction to Business Process Re-engineering (BPR) and Legacy Habits**

    Today we are going to talk about Business Process Re-engineering, or BPR. I hope you have seen the introductory video. By the way, how many of you have started to form good habits regarding the course readings? Have you started? Maybe on a Saturday morning, move to a corner, order a coffee, and just take a look at a few pieces of the material. After a few weeks, you actually may start to read them a bit more seriously. Forming habits takes time, and if you can cultivate them, habits can be very powerful in the long run. In some sense, BPR is actually about addressing the negative side of habits. It is about challenging the "past legacy." The way you should look at this is that everything we do—our behavior, our organizational structures—is influenced by something in the past. There is a reason why we might be scared of a certain small room; maybe something bad happened there before, or you suffered something there, so you remember it and try to avoid it. At the company level, a lot of things are also deeply rooted in past logic. The original reason may have been valid back then, but it may no longer be credible today. The quick story I shared in the video is about a World War II British artillery gun crew. It might seem funny, but it is a serious case of process legacy. During a time and motion study to speed up the firing process, they noticed a peculiar step: just before firing, two soldiers would simply stand at attention for three seconds, not doing anything. People were puzzled. The soldiers themselves didn't know why; nobody knew why. It was just how they were trained in gunnery school. It didn't seem logical—why stand there for three seconds? Eventually, an old, retired colonel looked at the pictures and realized: the two men were holding the horses. But the horses were long gone; the military had moved on to motorized artillery. Yet, the ritual—the steps of having two soldiers pause to hold the non-existent horses—remained. It just shows you how deep-rooted and rigid our organizational behaviors can be.

    今天我们要讨论的是业务流程重组，或者叫 BPR。希望你们已经看过介绍视频了。顺便问一下，你们中有多少人已经开始养成关于课程阅读的好习惯了？你们开始了吗？也许在周六早上，找个角落，点杯咖啡，就看看几篇材料。几周后，你可能实际上会开始更认真地阅读它们。养成习惯需要时间，如果你能培养它们，从长远来看，习惯会产生非常强大的力量。在某种意义上，BPR 实际上是关于解决习惯的负面影响。它是关于挑战“历史遗留问题”。你应该这样看待这个问题：我们做的每一件事——我们的行为、我们的组织结构——都受到过去某些事情的影响。我们可能会害怕某个小房间，这是有原因的；也许以前那里发生过什么不好的事，或者你在那里受过伤，所以你记住了它并试图避开它。在公司层面上，许多事情也深深植根于过去的逻辑中。最初的原因在当时可能是合理的，但今天可能不再可信。我在视频中分享的简短故事是关于二战时期英国炮兵连的。这可能看起来很有趣，但这是一个严重的流程遗留问题的案例。在进行时间与动作研究以加快射击流程时，他们注意到了一个奇怪的步骤：就在开火前，两名士兵会立正站立三秒钟，什么也不做。人们感到很困惑。士兵们自己也不知道为什么；没有人知道为什么。他们在炮兵学校就是这样受训的。这似乎不合逻辑——为什么要站在那里三秒钟？最终，一位退休的老上校看了照片才恍然大悟：那两个人是在拉马。但马早就没有了；军队已经改用机动火炮了。然而，这个仪式——让两名士兵停下来拉不存在的马的步骤——却保留了下来。这恰恰向你展示了我们的组织行为可能有多么根深蒂固和僵化。

  - [18:30 - 18:33] **The Pomelo Leaves Anecdote: Superstition vs. Outdated Science**

    Let me share a personal story. Many years ago, when I was a kid, we had a house with a *Citrus maxima* tree—a pomelo tree. My mother divergently insisted that when we bathed a baby, we had to use those pomelo leaves. She would insistently go and use the fine leaves, put them in water, and bathe my daughter. I think in Singapore you can only find pomelo leaves during certain times, but she was adamant. We argued and argued. I told her it wasn't necessary, but she said it was very important. Why do you think she said it was so important? A student might say it's an ingrained superstition. Yes, it has become a tradition, but why did that tradition start in the first place? As someone suggested, it was likely used to cleanse the water. A long time ago, when we didn't have clean piped water, this was one way to cleanse the water. Maybe because of the chemical properties in the *Citrus maxima* leaves, when you put them in the water, it acts as a lipid or has some antibacterial effect to kill germs—similar to what hand lotions or sanitizers do today. But of course, today our tap water is super clean. The need for that chemical cleansing is no longer there. But the thinking—the absolute need to use *Citrus maxima* or pomelo leaves water for the baby—carries on. You can call it superstitious now, but at one point, it actually had a scientific reason for existing. It's an outdated process. Perhaps other leaves had similar effects, but those days, most houses had these trees, making it a convenient solution.

    让我分享一个个人故事。很多年前，当我还是个孩子的时候，我们的房子里有一棵柚子树（Citrus maxima）。我母亲坚持认为，当我们给婴儿洗澡时，必须用那些柚子叶。她会坚持去摘那些细嫩的叶子，把它们放在水里，然后给我女儿洗澡。我想在新加坡你只能在特定时期找到柚子叶，但她非常固执。我们争论了又争论。我告诉她没有必要，但她说这非常重要。你认为她为什么说这如此重要？可能会有学生说这是一种根深蒂固的迷信。是的，它已经成为一种传统，但这个传统最初是如何开始的呢？正如有人建议的那样，它很可能是用来净化水的。很久以前，当我们没有干净的自来水时，这是净化水的一种方法。也许因为柚子叶中的化学特性，当你把它们放入水中时，它会起脂质作用或具有某种抗菌杀菌的作用——类似于今天的护手霜或洗手液的作用。但当然，今天我们的自来水非常干净。不再需要那种化学净化了。但是那种想法——给婴儿洗澡绝对需要用柚子叶水的想法——却延续了下来。你现在可以说它是迷信，但在某个时期，它的存在实际上是有科学依据的。这是一个过时的流程。也许其他叶子也有类似的效果，但在那个年代，大多数房子里都有这种树，这使得它成为一个方便的解决方案。

  - [18:33 - 18:36] **Legacy IT Systems and Organizational Structures**

    You find things like this happening in business all the time. The tricky part is that sometimes we do not know whether these established rules and processes remain relevant. All our processes are, one way or another, heavily influenced by Information Technology. If you go to major banks today, they have a massive amount of legacy systems. They are still using systems built in the 1980s and 1990s. Actually, a lot of those systems cause big problems now. It's very funny—those legacy systems were coded using old computer programming languages like COBOL or Pascal. Because they are still running those old core banking systems, banks have to hire very expensive, very rare engineers who still know how to read and write those outdated languages. Similarly, organizational structures can be outdated. At one point, a company might have been structured geographically by country. They looked into distinct country markets because physical presence was required. But today, because of the Internet and cloud infrastructure, you actually do not need to have a separate physical setup for different markets or countries to capture the opportunity. Yet, the old organizational structure might still remain. Or perhaps there was a specific management style when the company first started. The founding CEO might have been somebody very powerful, very old-fashioned, who wanted to check everything manually and look at things face-to-face. So everything was designed to be paper-based and manual. But even after he has long gone, maybe even into the third generation of leadership, for some reason, the staff still process things manually. Everything we do is embedded with these external and internal factors. For example, if you run a food delivery system today, market pressure dictates you have to be fast. If someone orders lunch, you have to deliver it around lunchtime. In certain countries, customers expect a strict two-hour delivery window, and this imposes heavy requirements on your technology, the way you charge customers, and how you compensate them if there is damage or delay. The challenge for us as managers is: how do we systematically identify what things are no longer relevant and therefore should be obliterated?

    你会发现类似的事情在商业中经常发生。棘手的部分在于，有时我们不知道这些既定的规则和流程是否仍然具有现实意义。我们的所有流程在某种程度上都深受信息技术的影响。如果你今天去大型银行，他们有大量的遗留系统。他们仍在使用建于 20 世纪 80 年代和 90 年代的系统。实际上，现在很多这些系统都造成了很大的问题。这很有趣——这些遗留系统是用 COBOL 或 Pascal 等旧计算机编程语言编写的。因为他们仍在运行这些旧的核心银行系统，所以银行必须聘请非常昂贵、非常稀缺的工程师，这些工程师仍然懂得如何读写那些过时的语言。同样地，组织结构也可能过时。在某个时期，一家公司可能是按国家在地理上进行构建的。他们研究不同的国家市场，因为那时需要实体存在。但今天，由于互联网和云基础设施，你实际上不需要为不同的市场或国家设立单独的物理机构来抓住机遇。然而，旧的组织结构可能仍然保留着。或者，也许在公司刚起步时有一种特定的管理风格。创始 CEO 可能是一个非常有权势、非常守旧的人，他想手动检查每一件事，并面对面地查看事物。所以一切都被设计成基于纸张和手动的。但即使他早就不在了，甚至到了第三代领导层，出于某种原因，员工仍然在手动处理事务。我们所做的一切都植根于这些内部和外部因素。例如，如果你今天经营一个食品外卖系统，市场压力要求你必须快。如果有人点午餐，你必须在午餐时间左右送达。在某些国家，客户期望有严格的两小时送达窗口，这对你的技术、你向客户收费的方式以及在出现损坏或延误时你如何赔偿他们提出了很高的要求。作为管理者，我们面临的挑战是：我们如何系统地识别出哪些东西不再具有现实意义，并因此应该被彻底消除？

  - [18:36 - 18:41] **The Process Perspective: DMAIC vs. BPR and Customer Journey**

    This brings us to BPR, which is a very specialized approach to problem-solving. DMAIC is far more general and focuses on incremental improvements. BPI or BPR is basically saying that we must look at problems from a radical process model perspective. Meaning, we see things exclusively from the customer's point of view—how the customer goes through selecting the product, buying the product, and consuming the product. We do not look at our processes from the viewpoint of Internal Function A, Function B, or Function C. Because the customer is the one who pays, their perspective is the only one that truly matters. Customers are never bothered by your internal silos. For example, you are an NUS customer. Are you bothered by how the NUS schools are structured or how the internal departments are organized? You don't care. To you, as a student, you just want to register properly, attend your classes properly, and receive a good education. Whether a class is handled by Department A, Department B, or jointly, doesn't matter to you. If there is a breakdown in service, we cannot tell you, "Oh, you have this problem because it involves cross-departmental communication between A and B." You would say, "I don't care, that's your internal problem." That excuse will not fly. This is why we have to look at everything from an end-to-end process point of view. This process perspective is not unique to BPR. Those who study design thinking will know concepts like the Customer Journey Map or User Journey Map. A Customer Journey Map charts out the process steps the user takes, capturing both their functional actions and their emotional responses at various stages. In service innovation, they use the idea of a Service Blueprint. A Service Blueprint is essentially a detailed process map. As seen in the hotel overnight stay example, it maps the physical evidence for each step, customer actions, and whether the customer interacts with a front-stage human touchpoint or a backstage support system. For instance, a customer might make a reservation via the internet; so the website is the physical evidence guiding that stage. When they arrive at the hotel, the physical evidence is the exterior parking sign, the lobby, the elevators, and the signage guiding the person to the check-in counter. When you design a service process, mapping both the front-end touchpoints and the back-stage actions is crucial because a failure in the backstage directly influences the success or failure of the front-stage customer experience.

    这就引出了 BPR，这是一种非常专门的解决问题的方法。DMAIC 要普遍得多，并且侧重于渐进式改进。BPI 或 BPR 基本上是说，我们必须从彻底的流程模型视角来看待问题。意思是，我们完全从客户的观点来看待事物——客户如何经历选择产品、购买产品和消费产品的过程。我们不从内部职能部门 A、职能部门 B 或职能部门 C 的视角来看待我们的流程。因为客户才是付款的人，他们的视角才是唯一真正重要的。客户从不关心你们内部的各自为政。例如，你是新加坡国立大学的客户。你会关心国大的学院是如何设置的，或者内部院系是如何组织的吗？你不在乎。对你这个学生来说，你只想顺利注册、正常上课，并获得良好的教育。一门课是由 A 系、B 系负责，还是联合负责，对你来说都无所谓。如果在服务中出现问题，我们不能对你说：“哦，你遇到这个问题是因为它涉及到 A 和 B 之间的跨部门沟通。”你会说：“我不在乎，那是你们内部的问题。”那个借口是行不通的。这就是为什么我们必须从端到端的流程视角来看待一切。这种流程视角并非 BPR 独有。学习设计思维的人会知道诸如客户旅程地图或用户旅程地图等概念。客户旅程地图绘制了用户采取的流程步骤，记录了他们在各个阶段的功能操作和情感反应。在服务创新中，他们使用服务蓝图的概念。服务蓝图本质上是一个详细的流程图。正如在酒店过夜住宿的例子中所见，它绘制了每个步骤的有形展示、客户行为，以及客户是与前台的人工接触点互动还是与后台支持系统互动。例如，客户可能通过互联网进行预订；那么网站就是指导该阶段的有形展示。当他们到达酒店时，有形展示就是外部的停车标志、大堂、电梯，以及引导客人前往登记柜台的指示牌。当你设计一个服务流程时，绘制前端接触点和后台操作是至关重要的，因为后台的失败会直接影响前台客户体验的成败。

  - [18:41 - 18:45] **Process Design in Action: Subway and Airport Examples**

    The best way to experience this physical process design is when you go to Subway. Who buys from Subway regularly? Nobody? Okay, what is the first step when you order at Subway? First, you choose your bread. Then second, you choose your cheese. Then you see what meat you want. You choose your veggies, you pick your sauces, and finally, they ask if you are going to make it into a meal and if you are having it to take away. Yes, and then you pay and go. Subway perfectly guides you. Typically, when you enter, they explicitly tell you the steps: 1, 2, 3, 4. They are physically guiding you along the counter. Also, notice that they make sure the display is covered by clear glass, so you can clearly see the ingredients. Why is it important to see the ingredients through the glass broadcast? A student might say "quality." Yes, quality and transparency. But from a process perspective, the physical design corresponding to that particular stage minimizes the errors that the customers will make. Because they can point and see, there are fewer communication mistakes. Because of the transparent design of the glass, the queue will move much faster. Everything we encounter in a physical retail space has been carefully designed based on the process steps involved. That is why you can see that most modern airports are designed in a specific way. What is the most important thing when you arrive at the airport departure hall? You need to know which row, which counter, and how to navigate. So they employ very bright lighting and massive, clear signage so you can see from very far away exactly which queue to go to. If they did not design it this way and make the process obvious and self-service, what would happen? Everyone would congregate at the customer service counter asking, "Where is my check-in row? I cannot see it." By making the process visually intuitive, the customer service counter does not need to have many staff, and passengers are not frustrated. The physical evidence streamlines the small steps involved.

    体验这种实体流程设计的最佳方式就是你去赛百味（Subway）的时候。谁经常在赛百味买东西？没有人吗？好吧，当你在赛百味点餐时，第一步是什么？首先，你选择面包。然后第二步，你选择奶酪。然后看你想要什么肉。你选择蔬菜，挑选酱料，最后，他们会问你是否要升级为套餐，以及是否要外带。是的，然后你付款离开。赛百味完美地引导了你。通常，当你走进去时，他们会明确地告诉你步骤：1、2、3、4。他们沿着柜台对你进行物理引导。此外，请注意，他们确保展示台被透明玻璃覆盖，以便你能清楚地看到食材。通过玻璃展示看到食材为什么很重要？可能会有学生说“质量”。是的，质量和透明度。但从流程的角度来看，与该特定阶段相对应的物理设计最大限度地减少了客户可能犯的错误。因为他们可以指着看，沟通失误就少了。由于玻璃的透明设计，队伍移动的速度会快得多。我们在实体零售空间遇到的每一件事都是根据所涉及的流程步骤精心设计的。这就是为什么你会看到大多数现代机场都是以特定方式设计的。当你到达机场出发大厅时，最重要的是什么？你需要知道是哪一排，哪个柜台，以及如何走。所以他们采用了非常明亮的照明和巨大、清晰的指示牌，让你从很远的地方就能准确地看到该去哪个队伍。如果他们不这样设计，不让流程变得明显和自助化，会发生什么？每个人都会聚集在客户服务柜台问：“我的值机柜台在哪里？我找不到。”通过使流程在视觉上直观，客户服务柜台不需要配备很多员工，乘客也不会感到沮丧。有形展示简化了所涉及的小步骤。

  - [18:45 - 18:50] **Stages of BPR and Swimlane Process Mapping**

    This brings us to the paper I asked you all to read by Guha, Kettinger, and Teng (1993) from MISQ. It is actually a very good paper that I came across many years ago when I was doing my PhD. It comprehensively explains the methodology and studies the 6 stages of the Process Reengineering Life Cycle. It also has an appendix that explains the different toolkits available. You will find that at the base level, many of these toolkits are common, generic problem-solving tools; it is just a matter of where you apply them and to what kind of problems. For simplicity, we often group those six stages into four main phases: Identify the process, map the As-Is state, design the To-Be state, and Implement. Because BPR is a process mapping approach, what you usually do in a company is map out the workflow using a cross-functional swimlane flowchart. For example, look at a credit card payment process. On the vertical axis, you put the stakeholders or the people handling the steps: the Card Holder, the Merchant, the Payment Gateway, the Acquiring Bank's Processor, and the Customer's Credit Card Issuer. The cardholder makes a purchase, the merchant forwards the details to the payment gateway, the payment gateway authorizes the payment and forwards the secure details to the processor, and so on. There is a specific process involving everyone. The key here is that time flows in one horizontal direction. We have these swimlanes, and time only moves forward from left to right. Steps rarely go backward because we cannot reverse time. Today we bought the item, tomorrow we check, and then we verify the transaction. This first map of the current process is what we call the "As-Is" state. At this point, you do not attempt to improve anything; you just try to map out the current situation to establish a baseline. Then, you start to brainstorm: What can we do to reduce the number of steps? What can we do to collapse or even eliminate some of these stakeholders involved? I have an example here from the NUS BPR team. Their original As-Is process was very long, moving up and down across the lanes in one direction, involving 9 different stakeholders touching the process. Then, they mapped a "To-Be" state using Microsoft Power Apps. With Power Apps, the overall process length is drastically reduced. While the beginning steps remain similar, the core transaction becomes internal and automated within Power Apps, making it transparent and requiring minimal human intervention. They utilized new technology to fundamentally reduce the time taken and stakeholders involved.

    这就引出了我让大家阅读的 Guha、Kettinger 和 Teng（1993）发表在 MISQ 上的那篇论文。这实际上是一篇非常好的论文，是我很多年前读博士时偶然发现的。它全面地解释了方法论，并研究了流程重组生命周期的 6 个阶段。它还有一个附录，解释了可用的不同工具包。你会发现，在基础层面上，许多这些工具包都是常见的、通用的问题解决工具；这仅仅取决于你将它们应用在哪里以及应用于什么样的问题。为了简单起见，我们通常将这六个阶段分为四个主要阶段：识别流程、绘制“现状（As-Is）”状态、设计“未来（To-Be）”状态以及实施。因为 BPR 是一种流程图解方法，所以在公司里，你通常要做的是使用跨职能的泳道流程图来绘制工作流。例如，看看信用卡支付流程。在纵轴上，你放置利益相关者或处理步骤的人员：持卡人、商户、支付网关、收单银行处理器和客户的信用卡发卡机构。持卡人进行购买，商户将详细信息转发给支付网关，支付网关授权支付并将安全细节转发给处理器，依此类推。有一个涉及所有人的具体流程。这里的关键是时间沿水平单向流动。我们有这些泳道，时间只从左向右前进。步骤很少会倒退，因为我们无法让时间倒流。今天我们买了东西，明天我们检查，然后验证交易。这第一张当前流程的地图就是我们所说的“现状（As-Is）”状态。在这一点上，你不要试图去改进任何东西；你只是努力绘制出当前的情况以建立一个基准。然后，你开始头脑风暴：我们能做些什么来减少步骤的数量？我们能做些什么来合并甚至消除其中一些涉及的利益相关者？我这里有一个来自国大 BPR 团队的例子。他们最初的现状流程非常长，在一个方向上跨越泳道上下移动，有 9 个不同的利益相关者参与到该流程中。然后，他们使用 Microsoft Power Apps 绘制了一个“未来（To-Be）”状态。通过使用 Power Apps，整个流程的长度大幅缩短。虽然开始的步骤保持相似，但核心交易变成了 Power Apps 内部的自动化操作，使其变得透明且需要最少的人工干预。他们利用新技术从根本上减少了所花费的时间和涉及的利益相关者。

  - [18:50 - 18:54] **Exercise: Re-engineering the Haircut Process**

    Now, we are going to do an interesting exercise. Your job is to map the current "As-Is" process of getting a haircut. After that, your challenge is to design a "To-Be" process that reduces the entire experience to just 20 minutes. Here is the problem statement context: Many years ago, people would easily spend an hour or more for a haircut. First, you have to call to make an appointment (maybe taking 1 minute). On the day of your appointment, you have to take public transport, maybe taking 30 minutes to travel to the salon. Then, you arrive and you probably have to wait for 15 minutes. When it is finally your turn, they wash your hair, they cut it, they dry it, and then you pay. So, map out these blocks of time on a piece of paper or Excel. Activity one is booking. Activity two is travel time (30 minutes). Activity three is waiting. Activity four is washing, then cutting, and so on. Map out this entire hour-and-a-half experience. Once you have your baseline, you need to brainstorm. Ask yourselves: what new technologies are available, and what activities can we fundamentally change or eliminate? My only criteria for your solution is that you must collapse this entire timeframe down to 20 minutes. I do not care what you put in the steps, but minimally, a physical haircut must occur—you cannot just say "I go there and do a virtual haircut." The fundamental action of cutting hair must happen. So go ahead, draw it out on your tables.

    现在，我们要进行一个有趣的练习。你们的任务是绘制目前理发的“现状（As-Is）”流程。之后，你们面临的挑战是设计一个“未来（To-Be）”流程，将整个体验缩短到只有 20 分钟。这是问题陈述的背景：很多年前，人们很容易花一个小时或更长时间理发。首先，你必须打电话预约（可能需要 1 分钟）。在你预约的那天，你必须乘坐公共交通工具，可能需要 30 分钟才能到达理发店。然后，你到了那里，可能还需要等 15 分钟。好不容易轮到你了，他们给你洗头、剪发、吹干，然后你付款。所以，在一张纸或 Excel 上画出这些时间块。活动一是预约。活动二是交通时间（30 分钟）。活动三是等待。活动四是洗头，然后剪头，等等。画出这整个一个半小时的体验。一旦你有了基准，你就需要进行头脑风暴。问问你们自己：现在有什么新技术可用，我们能从根本上改变或消除哪些活动？我对你们的解决方案的唯一标准是，你们必须将这整个时间范围压缩到 20 分钟以内。我不在乎你们在步骤里写什么，但至少，必须发生实质性的理发行为——你不能仅仅说“我去那里做个虚拟理发”。剪头发这一基本动作必须发生。所以，开始吧，在你们的桌子上画出来。

  - [18:54 - 19:32] **Group Activity / Gap in Recording**

    (The students spend this time working in their groups, mapping out the haircut process and brainstorming ways to reduce the total time to 20 minutes.)

    （学生们利用这段时间在小组中进行讨论，绘制理发流程图，并集思广益寻找将总时间缩短到 20 分钟的方法。）

  - [19:32 - 19:43] **The QB House Case Study**

    If you read the book *Blue Ocean Strategy*, they talk about the QB House case. Traditionally in Japan, going for a haircut was a big deal. Japan used to be an agricultural society where people lived in villages, so getting a haircut once a month was a social event. You would dress up, look your best, and go to the salon for a joint experience. That's why traditional salons make you wait, serve you green tea, give you a shoulder massage, and spend a lot of time on you. The whole process would take up half a day on a Saturday morning. However, the bigger societal trend in Japan shifted. Fewer and fewer people lived that agricultural lifestyle. Everyone moved to big cities, working busy corporate jobs from 8:00 AM to 6:00 PM, Monday through Friday. On Saturday mornings, nobody wants to spend half their day at a salon. The pressure to keep the process as short and simple as possible became critical.

    The founder of QB House observed this increasing trend of busy professionals who were no longer willing to spend hours at a salon. The value proposition of his entire operation was simple: "I save time for you." It is not about providing green tea or the most beautiful, intricate haircut; it is about providing a decent, highly efficient haircut. How did they achieve this dramatic time reduction? As you correctly identified in your exercise, the longest time sink is travel. But keeping in mind that QB House started in Japan in the mid-1990s—before smartphones, QR codes, or apps—how did they eliminate travel time? The founder figured out that he needed to locate his barber shops in high-traffic commuter areas. By placing the shop on your daily commute to work, at bus interchanges, or right inside MRT stations, you are already there. Therefore, your traveling time is strictly zero. But placing shops in shopping malls and transit hubs means the rental cost is extraordinarily high. How do you mitigate the expensive rent? You make the shop size as small as physically possible. And how do you do that? You completely eliminate the waiting area. There are no magazines, no comfortable sofas. It is as bare-bones as sitting in an MRT train. Will customers complain that the waiting area is uncomfortable? No, because the entire process is designed so they don't have to wait. If you have to wait a long time, comfort matters. If the wait is negligible, comfort doesn't matter.

    Furthermore, QB House implemented a brilliant physical indicator: a traffic light system outside the shop. They placed weight sensors in the few basic waiting seats. If three people are sitting, the red light flashes outside, indicating a 15-minute wait. If two people are waiting, it shows a yellow light for a 10-minute wait. If nobody is sitting, it's a green light for 0 minutes. This means from far away, a commuter can instantly see the required queue time. On top of that, because there were no QR codes, they used an automated ticket machine. You insert your money, get a card, and hand it to the barber. There is zero manual exchange of cash, streamlining the transaction. Finally, they radically changed the cleanup process. Instead of spending 15 minutes washing and drying a customer's hair, they implemented a proprietary ceiling-mounted vacuum cleaner—a proven technology—to quickly suck up all the loose hair. This ensures you are decently clean so you can go straight to work or dinner without a messy head.

    Imagine it is a Friday night. You are at a mall meeting a friend for dinner. Your friend calls to say the MRT has broken down and he will be 20 minutes late. What do you do? You look around and see a QB House. The traffic light is yellow, meaning a 10-minute wait. The haircut itself takes 10 minutes. So, wait time plus haircut equals 20 minutes. By the time your friend arrives, you are done. Everything aligns perfectly. This is a societal trend that is prevalent in busy Asian cities. What sells in Tokyo will probably sell in Hong Kong, in big cities in China, and in Singapore.

    如果你读过《蓝海战略》这本书，他们谈到了 QB House（快剪）的案例。传统上在日本，理发是一件大事。日本曾经是一个农业社会，人们住在村庄里，所以每个月理发一次是一项社交活动。你会盛装打扮，以最好的面貌去理发店体验。这就是为什么传统理发店会让你等待，给你端上绿茶，给你做肩部按摩，并在你身上花很多时间。整个过程会占用周六早上大半天的时间。然而，日本更大的社会趋势发生了转变。越来越少的人过着那种农业生活方式。每个人都搬到了大城市，周一到周五从早上 8 点到下午 6 点忙于公司的工作。在周六早上，没人想在理发店耗费半天时间。尽可能保持流程简短成为至关重要的需求。

    QB House 的创始人观察到了这一日益增长的趋势，即忙碌的专业人士不再愿意在理发店耗费数小时。他整个运营的价值主张很简单：“我为你节省时间。”这不在于提供绿茶或最漂亮、最复杂的发型；而在于提供体面、高效的理发服务。他们是如何实现这一时间的大幅缩减的？正如你们在练习中正确指出的那样，最耗时的环节是交通。但要记住，QB House 是在 20 世纪 90 年代中期在日本起步的——在智能手机、二维码或应用程序出现之前——他们是如何消除交通时间的？创始人意识到他需要将理发店设在人流量大的通勤区域。通过将店铺设在你每天上班的通勤路线上、公交枢纽或直接设在地铁站内，你刚好就在那里。因此，你的交通时间严格来说就是零。但是把店铺开在购物中心和交通枢纽意味着租金成本异常高昂。你如何减轻高昂的租金压力？你要让店铺面积尽可能小。那又该怎么做呢？你彻底取消等候区。没有杂志，没有舒适的沙发。它就像坐在地铁车厢里一样简陋。顾客会抱怨等候区不舒服吗？不会，因为整个流程的设计使得他们不需要等待。如果你必须等很长时间，舒适度就很重要。如果等待时间可以忽略不计，舒适度就无关紧要了。

    此外，QB House 还实施了一个绝妙的物理指示器：店外的交通灯系统。他们在仅有的几个基本等候座位上安装了重力传感器。如果有三个人坐着，外面就会闪烁红灯，表示需要等待 15 分钟。如果有两个人等着，就会显示黄灯，表示等待 10 分钟。如果没人坐，就是绿灯，表示 0 分钟。这意味着通勤者从很远的地方就能立刻看到所需的排队时间。最重要的是，因为那时没有二维码，他们使用了自动售票机。你把钱塞进去，拿到一张卡，然后把它交给理发师。完全没有手动现金交易，简化了交易流程。最后，他们从根本上改变了清理流程。他们没有花 15 分钟给顾客洗头和吹干，而是采用了一种专有的安装在天花板上的吸尘器——一项成熟的技术——来快速吸走所有碎发。这确保你相当干净，所以你可以直接去上班或赴宴，而不会弄得满头碎发。

    想象一下这是一个周五的晚上。你在购物中心等朋友吃晚饭。你的朋友打电话说地铁故障了，他要迟到 20 分钟。你该怎么办？你环顾四周，看到了一家 QB House。交通灯是黄色的，意味着需要等待 10 分钟。理发本身需要 10 分钟。所以，等待时间加上理发时间刚好 20 分钟。当你的朋友到达时，你已经剪好了。一切都配合得完美无缺。这是一种在繁忙的亚洲城市普遍存在的社会趋势。在东京畅销的模式，在香港、中国的大城市以及新加坡可能也会畅销。

  - [19:43 - 19:44] **Radical Changes: Internal and Business Model Re-engineering**

    So you can see the difference between the As-Is and the To-Be state. The To-Be stage is where you completely slay all the sacred cows. You think out-of-the-box, trying to radically collapse a one-hour process into 5 to 10 minutes. Typically, in an organizational BPR project, radical changes involve collapsing two departments into one, or taking a task that used to be handed off between two people and training one person to do it entirely. By doing that within the organization, you reduce the number of stakeholders involved and collapse the time. QB House is an exceptional case because it involves radically changing the entire consumer business model to achieve that speed.

    所以你们可以看到“现状（As-Is）”和“未来（To-Be）”状态之间的区别。“未来（To-Be）”阶段是你们彻底打破所有条条框框的阶段。你们打破常规思考，试图从根本上将一个小时的流程压缩到 5 到 10 分钟。通常，在一个组织的 BPR 项目中，根本性的改变包括将两个部门合并为一个部门，或者将过去在两个人之间交接的任务交由一个人负责并培训其独立完成。通过在组织内部这样做，你们减少了涉及的利益相关者数量并压缩了时间。QB House 是一个特例，因为它涉及从根本上改变整个消费者商业模式以达到这种速度。

  - [19:44 - 19:54] **Protecting Innovation: QB House Lawsuit in Singapore**

    Since we are on the topic of QB House, I want to show you a very interesting real-world study regarding how you protect your business innovation. I cover this in depth in my innovation class. This actually happened here in Singapore, and it is a documented case in the High Court of Singapore. Around the mid-2000s, a Japanese man living in Singapore went back to Japan to visit family and chanced upon QB House. He realized that this lifestyle trend would work perfectly in Singapore. So, he obtained a franchise license and opened the very first QB House in Singapore in October 2002, paying licensing fees and profit-sharing back to QB House Japan. Eventually, their relationship soured. Then, out of nowhere, a competitor called EC House appeared. QB House Japan investigated and found out that their franchisee was secretly acting as a consultant to EC House, utilizing everything he learned. QB House Japan got very angry, came to Singapore, and sued him in the High Court, claiming he violated the agreement and stole their intellectual property and business model.

    However, QB House actually lost the case. Why? It largely came down to the core philosophy of their process design. The way QB House operates requires a big transparent window so people outside can clearly see the traffic light system and how many people are waiting inside. It is completely transparent. Because of this, the judge ruled that anyone walking past could observe and easily copy the process. There was no real trade secret because the operations were entirely visible to the public. QB House Japan does hold patents for the unique angle of the chair and the ultra-compact spatial design of the cabinet, but patents are very difficult to enforce and easy to design around in this context. A competitor can easily just make their cabinet slightly wider and avoid patent infringement. So, if patents and non-compete clauses fail, how do you protect an innovation like this?

    To protect your innovation, you must look at your entire value delivery chain. Your value proposition is "time saving." The most critical component of that time saving is the convenient commuter location. So, the ultimate way to block competitors is to sign exclusive leasing agreements with all the major MRT stations, shopping malls, and bus interchanges. If you lock up the prime real estate, your competitor cannot deliver the "time saving" value proposition, even if they copy your vacuum and chairs. We also see newer competitors like kcuts (the Korean style variant). Interestingly, kcuts did not copy the physical traffic light or the sensor seats, meaning they missed some of the original design spirit. But today, they utilize a mobile app ticketing system. You take a digital queue number and you can go shopping while you wait, returning only when it's your turn. In this regard, QB House has actually fallen behind. They pioneered the 1990s technology, but they stayed there. A new startup like kcuts has no legacy baggage and can instantly leverage modern technology. Every time you map your processes, you have to constantly ask yourself: Can I make use of new technology to continuously update and shorten my process?

    既然我们谈到了 QB House，我想向你们展示一个关于如何保护你的商业创新的非常有趣的真实案例研究。我在我的创新课上对此进行了深入讲解。这实际上就发生在新加坡，这是新加坡高等法院记录在案的案件。在 2000 年代中期左右，一位居住在新加坡的日本人回日本探亲时，偶然发现了 QB House。他意识到这种生活方式的趋势在新加坡会非常完美。因此，他获得了特许经营许可，并于 2002 年 10 月在新加坡开设了第一家 QB House，向日本 QB House 支付许可费和利润分成。最终，他们的关系恶化了。然后，一个叫 EC House（剪发屋）的竞争对手不知从哪里冒了出来。日本 QB House 进行了调查，发现他们的加盟商秘密担任 EC House 的顾问，利用了他学到的一切。日本 QB House 非常生气，来到新加坡，在高等法院起诉了他，声称他违反了协议并窃取了他们的知识产权和商业模式。

    然而，QB House 实际上输了这场官司。为什么？这在很大程度上归结于其流程设计的核心理念。QB House 的运营方式需要一个透明的大窗户，以便外面的人可以清楚地看到交通灯系统以及里面有多少人在等待。它是完全透明的。正因为如此，法官裁定，任何路过的人都可以观察并轻易地复制这个流程。不存在真正的商业机密，因为这些操作对公众是完全可见的。日本 QB House 确实拥有关于椅子独特角度和储物柜超紧凑空间设计的专利，但在这种背景下，专利极难执行，而且很容易被绕过。竞争对手可以轻易地把他们的柜子做宽一点，从而避免专利侵权。所以，如果专利和竞业禁止条款都失效了，你如何保护这样的创新呢？

    为了保护你的创新，你必须审视你整个的价值交付链。你的价值主张是“节省时间”。节省时间最关键的组成部分是方便的通勤地点。因此，阻止竞争对手的终极方法是与所有主要的地铁站、购物中心和公交枢纽签署独家租赁协议。如果你锁定了黄金地段，你的竞争对手即使抄袭了你的吸尘器和椅子，也无法兑现“节省时间”的价值主张。我们也看到了像 kcuts（韩式变体）这样较新的竞争对手。有趣的是，kcuts 并没有抄袭物理交通灯或传感器座椅，这意味着他们错过了一些原始的设计精神。但今天，他们使用了移动应用程序排队系统。你拿一个数字排队号，可以在等待时去购物，只在轮到你时才回去。在这方面，QB House 实际上已经落后了。他们开创了 20 世纪 90 年代的技术，但他们却停滞不前。像 kcuts 这样的新初创公司没有历史包袱，可以立即利用现代技术。每次你绘制流程图时，你必须不断问自己：我能否利用新技术来不断更新和缩短我的流程？

  - [19:54 - 19:56] **Wrap Up and Homework**

    Alright, any questions about this? Good. So, what I want you to do is go home and perform an analysis similar to what we did last week, but this time apply it to BPR. Analyze how many stages are involved, what the specific purposes of each stage are, the pros and cons, and what specific tools and techniques you can use at each phase. In fact, most of the answers regarding the stages, tools, and techniques can be directly found in the Guha paper I provided. So that completes the second methodology. We previously analyzed DMAIC, today we covered BPR, and next week we are going to look at Design Thinking. We will do the same rigorous analysis across all three methodologies. If there are no further questions, anyone who has not signed the attendance sheet, please go to Jules at the back and make sure you sign it. We will end here today. Please form your groups and start thinking seriously about your group projects. Thank you.

    好了，关于这个有什么问题吗？很好。那么，我要你们做的是回家后进行与我们上周所做类似的分析，但这次将其应用于 BPR。分析涉及多少个阶段，每个阶段的具体目的是什么，优缺点是什么，以及在每个阶段可以使用哪些具体的工具和技术。事实上，关于阶段、工具和技术的大部分答案都可以在我提供的 Guha 论文中直接找到。这就完成了第二种方法论。我们之前分析了 DMAIC，今天讲了 BPR，下周我们要看看设计思维。我们将对所有三种方法论进行同样严格的分析。如果没有其他问题，任何没有在考勤表上签字的人，请到后面找 Jules，并确保你签字。我们今天就到这里。请组建好你们的小组，并开始认真考虑你们的小组项目。谢谢。
## Week 4 Topic 4 & 5 lecture Design Thinking 09.04
  - [18:14 - 18:18] **Time Blocking, Online Learning, and Quiz Feedback**

    Perhaps you need some sort of environment where you are forced to learn? For example, a situation where you are forced to sit down and actually run through the material. The reason I bring this up is that I recently signed up for masterclass.com. As you might know, MasterClass is an online learning portal featuring supposedly big gurus—people like Bill Clinton—teaching how they do certain things. I signed up, paid the subscription fee, and then quickly realized that I actually don't watch the videos. Right now, MasterClass is offering huge discounts, something like fifty percent off, dropping the price to around ninety-something dollars. But even with the discount, I realized I still wasn't watching the content. This made me reflect that learning, in certain contexts, sometimes requires a situation where you are physically or mentally locked into a specific schedule. In time management, this concept is called "time blocking." People used to rely heavily on standard to-do lists, but they often never get done. A much better approach to actually execute your tasks is to avoid just writing down a massive list that you passively check at the end of the day. Instead, you need to block the time directly in your calendar. You explicitly state, "For this half an hour, I am going to do exactly this task," and for the next half hour, another specific task. You are immediately translating your to-do list into calendar slots, which means you have already dedicated a specific time to accomplish it. Applying this to our pre-recorded lecture videos: you should find a time where you are consistently time-blocking yourself. For example, you might decide, "Every Wednesday afternoon during lunchtime, I am going to watch the class video." Alternatively, we could all just sit in this room and watch the video together, but that seems a bit silly and defeats the purpose of flexibility. However, I must warn you that the videos will get longer from now on, partly because of the sheer volume of content we need to cover. Because the content becomes very long, you really need to set aside dedicated time to watch it. What I might try moving forward is uploading my original, unedited lecture slides before the production team modifies them into something fancier. I might also incorporate a bit more classic, old-fashioned lecturing in class. This way, for those of you who genuinely didn't have the time to watch the pre-recorded videos, you still get a feel for the core concepts. For those who did watch the videos, this live session acts as a refresher before we dive into our group exercises. Doing it this way means we don't have to end the class so early, and we can place a bit more emphasis on the actual content. I want to make sure this works for you. Do you prefer this approach, or do you prefer what we have done so far—where we just come in and focus purely on the exercises so we don't spend the full three hours here in the evening? I worry that some of you might be extremely tired toward the end of a three-hour session. One of the physiological truths you learn very quickly is that when your brain is tired, you simply do not have the processing capacity to absorb more information. For our brain to function and process new concepts, it requires a massive amount of physical energy. If you are exhausted from a long day at work, your brain literally lacks the energy to learn. It becomes incredibly difficult for your brain to mobilize the necessary resources to absorb new material. That is why when you are tired, you don't actually learn—even if you force yourself to read a book, you won't remember a single thing. So, that's the cognitive side of our scheduling. I also took a look at the quiz results. When I first checked, I thought, "Oh my God, someone barely passed," but overall, it wasn't too bad. I hope you all are being honest with your feedback and not just trying to please me. I don't know your names on the feedback forms, so I'm obviously not going to give you high marks on next week's quiz just for writing nice things. The software we are using doesn't reflect the analytics perfectly, but I will go back, read through all your comments, and try my absolute best to address them.

    也许你需要某种强制学习的环境？例如，一种你被迫坐下来真正过一遍材料的情境。我之所以提到这个，是因为我最近注册了 masterclass.com。你们可能知道，MasterClass 是一个在线学习门户网站，上面据说有很多业界大咖——比如比尔·克林顿这样的人——在教授他们如何做某些事情。我注册了，付了订阅费，然后很快发现我其实根本没看那些视频。现在 MasterClass 在搞大幅折扣，大概有五折，价格降到了九十多美元。但即使有折扣，我发现我还是没看。这让我反思，在某些情况下，学习有时需要一种你被生理或心理上锁定在特定日程中的情境。在时间管理中，这个概念被称为“时间块”（time blocking）。人们过去常常严重依赖标准的待办事项清单（To-Do List），但这些事情往往永远做不完。要真正执行你的任务，一个更好的方法是不要只写一个庞大的清单然后等到一天结束时才被动地去检查它。相反，你需要直接在日历中预留时间。你明确地规定，“在这半个小时里，我要准确地做这个任务”，而在接下来的半个小时里，做另一个特定任务。你是在把你的待办事项立即转化为日历时间段，这意味着你已经为完成它分配了专门的时间。将这一点应用到我们的预录讲座视频中：你应该找一个你能持续进行时间块管理的时间。例如，你可能会决定，“每个星期三下午的午餐时间，我都要看课程视频。”或者，我们也可以都坐在这个教室里一起看视频，但这似乎有点傻，也违背了灵活性的初衷。不过，我必须提醒你们，从现在开始，视频会变得越来越长，部分原因是我们必须涵盖的内容量非常大。因为内容变得非常长，你真的需要留出专门的时间来看。我接下来可能会尝试的是，在制作团队将我的原始讲座幻灯片修改得更花哨之前，先把它们上传。我也可能会在课堂上加入更多经典的、传统式的讲授。这样一来，对于那些真的没有时间看预录视频的同学来说，你们仍然能了解核心概念。对于那些看了视频的同学，这个现场环节就在我们投入小组练习之前起到了复习的作用。这样做意味着我们不必那么早下课，我们可以把更多的重点放在实际内容上。我想确保这对你们有效。你们是更喜欢这种方法，还是更喜欢我们迄今为止所做的方式——也就是我们直接来上课，纯粹专注于练习，这样我们就不必在晚上花满整整三个小时？我担心你们中的一些人在三个小时的课程结束时会非常累。你很快就会学到的一个生理学真相是，当你的大脑疲劳时，你根本没有处理能力去吸收更多的信息。为了让大脑运作和处理新概念，它需要大量的体力能量。如果你因为漫长的一天工作而筋疲力尽，你的大脑实际上缺乏学习的能量。你的大脑将极难调动必要的资源来吸收新材料。这就是为什么当你累的时候，你其实学不到东西——即使你强迫自己看书，你也什么都记不住。所以，这是我们日程安排认知方面的问题。我也看了一下测验结果。我刚看的时候心想，“天哪，有人差点没及格”，但总体来说，还算不错。我希望你们在反馈时都是诚实的，而不是仅仅为了讨好我。我不知道反馈表上你们的名字，所以我显然不会因为你们写了好话就在下周的测验中给你们高分。我们使用的软件在分析反映上不是非常完美，但我会回去仔细阅读你们所有的评论，并尽我最大努力去解决它们。

  - [18:19 - 18:26] **The Double Diamond Model and Divergent Thinking**

    Today is week four. I am sure we will finish all the material on design analysis today. If we have a bit of time left, I want to discuss how to compare and contrast the three problem-solving methodologies we've been learning, which is technically next week's content. The reason I am trying to bring some of this material forward is that in week six, I have slotted in a guest speaker, which wasn't on the original schedule. I think by now you roughly get where I am heading with the course structure. So, let's talk about Design Thinking. Most of you already know Design Thinking, right? I remember at the start of the semester, many of you raised your hands when asked if you had done Design Thinking before. Let me see a show of hands again—how many have experience with it? Right, okay. For those who haven't done it at all, that's perfectly fine. You will likely recognize the classic, five-stage model: Empathize, Define, Ideate, Prototype, and Test. This is the IDEO or standard Stanford d.school way of defining Design Thinking. But the truth is, other researchers and institutions define the process slightly differently. For instance, Lakhani and his colleagues in a 2013 Harvard Business School case study structured it into three broader phases: Inspiration, Ideation, and Implementation. This specific framework was notably used in the Air New Zealand "Skycouch" and "Skynest" examples we discussed, where they overhauled the long-haul flight experience. Another variation uses Understand, Observe, Visualize, Evaluate and Refine, and Implement. Regardless of the specific terminology, what is fascinating to note is that Design Thinking is one of the very few methodologies built around the "Double Diamond" model. In this model, you start with Empathize and Define in the first diamond, and then move to Ideate, Prototype, and Test in the second. The unique aspect here is how it handles divergent and convergent thinking. Typically, in traditional problem-solving, we associate divergent thinking exclusively with brainstorming solutions—generating many ideas. Then, choosing a solution is convergent thinking. But the Double Diamond model emphasizes that when you are initially given a problem, your first step must be divergent thinking regarding the *problem itself*. You must take the time to explore what the true problem actually is. So, let me ask you: why does Design Thinking specifically incorporate a stage of divergent thinking just to define the problem, whereas other methodologies like DMAIC or Business Process Re-engineering (BPR) do not? A student correctly pointed out that it's because, initially, you often don't truly understand the problem. You might only be looking at superficial symptoms rather than the root cause. While searching for root causes is a common concept, in Design Thinking, the process goes deeper: you might start with one assumed problem, but after going through the Empathize and Define stages, you arrive at a completely different, reframed problem statement. You are literally changing the problem you are solving. Why is this necessary? Because Design Thinking is heavily applied to human-centered, emotional problems. When humans are central to the system, it is incredibly difficult to accurately describe pain points or emotions. People might not be willing to tell you the truth, they might not know how to articulate it, or they simply might not consciously know what their real problem is. What you observe at first is often just a symptom. You see this ambiguity frequently in service design. You find this ambiguity much less in methodologies like BPR, which focuses strictly on operational efficiency and reducing processing times, or DMAIC in Six Sigma, which is highly tangible and focused on reducing manufacturing costs or improving production yields. In those methods, the problem is usually unambiguous and well-defined from the start. But in Design Thinking, the nature of human problems requires the process itself to be modifiable and heavily exploratory at the beginning.

    今天是第四周。我相信我们今天会完成所有关于设计分析的材料。如果还有点时间，我想讨论一下如何比较和对比我们一直在学习的三种问题解决方**，这在技术上是下周的内容。我之所以试图将这些内容提前，是因为在第六周，我安排了一位客座演讲者，这原本不在计划表上。我想你们现在大概明白了我对这门课程结构的安排。那么，我们来谈谈设计思维（Design Thinking）。你们大多数人已经了解设计思维，对吧？我记得在学期初，当问及以前是否做过设计思维时，你们很多人都举手了。让我再看一次举手——有多少人有这方面的经验？好的。对于那些完全没做过的人，这完全没关系。你可能会认出经典的五阶段模型：共情（Empathize）、定义（Define）、构思（Ideate）、原型（Prototype）和测试（Test）。这是 IDEO 或标准的斯坦福 d.school 定义设计思维的方式。但事实是，其他研究人员和机构对这个过程的定义略有不同。例如，Lakhani 及其同事在 2013 年哈佛商学院的一个案例研究中，将其构建为三个更广泛的阶段：灵感（Inspiration）、构思（Ideation）和实施（Implementation）。我们在讨论新西兰航空的“空中沙发”（Skycouch）和“空中睡眠舱”（Skynest）彻底改变长途飞行体验的例子中，特别使用了这个具体框架。另一种变体使用理解、观察、可视化、评估与完善以及实施。不管具体术语是什么，值得注意的是，设计思维是极少数围绕“双钻”（Double Diamond）模型构建的方法论之一。在这个模型中，你在第一个钻石中从共情和定义开始，然后移动到第二个钻石中的构思、原型和测试。这里的独特之处在于它如何处理发散思维和收敛思维。通常，在传统的问题解决中，我们将发散思维仅仅与头脑风暴解决方案（产生许多想法）联系起来。然后，选择一个解决方案是收敛思维。但是，双钻模型强调，当你最初被赋予一个问题时，你的第一步必须是关于*问题本身*的发散思维。你必须花时间去探索真正的问题到底是什么。那么，我问你们：为什么设计思维特别包含一个发散思维阶段仅仅是为了定义问题，而像 DMAIC 或业务流程重组（BPR）这样的其他方法论却没有？一位学生正确地指出，这是因为，在最初，你通常并没有真正理解问题。你可能只是在看表面的症状，而不是根本原因。虽然寻找根本原因是一个普遍的概念，但在设计思维中，这个过程要深入得多：你可能从一个假设的问题开始，但在经历了共情和定义阶段后，你得出了一个完全不同的、重新构建的问题陈述。你实际上是在改变你要解决的问题。为什么这是必要的？因为设计思维被大量应用于以人为本的、情感层面的问题。当人处于系统的核心时，要准确描述痛点或情绪是极其困难的。人们可能不愿意告诉你真相，他们可能不知道如何表达，或者他们根本没有意识到自己真正的问题是什么。你最初观察到的往往只是一个症状。在服务设计中，你会经常看到这种模糊性。在 BPR 等严格专注于运营效率和缩短处理时间的方法论中，或者在六西格玛中的 DMAIC 这种高度具体、专注于降低制造成本或提高产量的方**中，这种模糊性要少得多。在这些方法中，问题通常从一开始就是明确无误且定义清晰的。但在设计思维中，人类问题的本质要求过程本身在开始时就是可修改的且高度探索性的。

  - [18:27 - 18:34] **Efficiency vs. Human-Centered Design: UTown and One North**

    In your readings, I provided a link to the d.school bootleg toolbook, which covers various methods like observation, user journey mapping, and interviewing extreme users. The core concept is human-centered design: when solving a problem, always consider the users' actual needs. This sounds like basic common sense, doesn't it? But if it's so obvious, why is it that only recently we started heavily talking about human-centered design in Singapore? Why was it ignored previously? Historically, the emphasis here was almost entirely on efficiency and utility. If you went to a local hospital in the 1990s, the design was purely functional. The seating was arranged in strict, rigid sequences. There was absolutely zero privacy for the patients or their worried families. The architects didn't consider the emotional state of a patient waiting for a diagnosis; the design simply maximized operational efficiency. You can actually see this philosophical shift in different parts of the NUS campus. Notice the stark architectural difference between where we are right now and UTown (University Town). When I asked what the difference is, one student mentioned UTown has a more lifestyle-oriented vibe, with more restaurants and greenery, while our current location is extremely compact. Why is this side of the campus so compact? Because the old Singapore approach to design was to maximize space utilization. Every single square meter had to be optimized for utility, resulting in high-rise, densely packed buildings. Now, think about who actually uses UTown—the students. I asked you which part of the campus is better for learning, sharing, and interaction. A student noted that the older, compact side forces you into a specific focus, like a traditional library setting. However, another student correctly pointed out that the older buildings are highly confusing; it's difficult to even find the canteen because the layout isn't intuitive. UTown, on the other hand, is a vastly superior environment for learning and collaboration. It has expansive study areas, abundant power plugs for laptops, and places like the 24/7 Starbucks. It is not dominated by imposing high-rises. Instead, the design naturally encourages people to bump into each other, connect, and interact. A university thrives on knowledge creation and sharing, and for that to happen, people need spaces that facilitate serendipitous interactions. Placing cafes and green spaces in the center is a deliberate Design Thinking choice to foster this community engagement. Another excellent example is One North. Anyone here work at One North? One North was originally built by JTC (Jurong Town Corporation). JTC historically excelled at building traditional factories, where success meant ultimate space efficiency and utility. They took that same factory-building mindset and applied it to One North, assuming that R&D facilities just needed maximum space utilization. But very soon, they realized that an R&D hub functions more like a university campus. Startups and researchers desperately need to interact, cross-pollinate ideas, and collaborate. The original physical layout of One North actually hindered this; people never bumped into one another. Consequently, a few years ago, they had to undertake a massive renovation—even physically changing the flow of the escalators—specifically to force more foot traffic intersections and encourage casual interactions among employees from different companies. So, while efficiency is still critical in certain contexts—like an airport, where pure utility is required so passengers don't get lost and miss their flights—when you are designing for innovation, community, or comfort, prioritizing pure efficiency over the human experience is a massive failure.

    在你们的阅读材料中，我提供了一个指向 d.school bootleg 工具手册的链接，其中涵盖了各种方法，如观察、用户旅程地图和极端用户访谈。核心概念是以人为本的设计：在解决问题时，始终考虑用户的实际需求。这听起来像是基本的常识，不是吗？但如果它如此显而易见，为什么直到最近我们在新加坡才开始大量谈论以人为本的设计？为什么以前它被忽视了？从历史上看，这里的重点几乎完全放在效率和实用性上。如果你在 20 世纪 90 年代去当地的医院，其设计纯粹是为了功能性。座位被安排成严格、死板的顺序。病人和他们焦急的家属绝对没有任何隐私可言。建筑师们并没有考虑等待诊断的病人的情绪状态；设计仅仅是为了最大化运营效率。你实际上可以在国大校园的不同区域看到这种哲学上的转变。注意我们现在所在的地方和大学城（UTown）之间鲜明的建筑差异。当我问两者有什么区别时，一位学生提到 UTown 更有生活方式的氛围，有更多的餐厅和绿化，而我们现在的位置非常紧凑。为什么校园的这一边如此紧凑？因为新加坡旧的设计方法是最大化空间利用率。每一平方米都必须为了实用性而优化，这就导致了高层、密集排列的建筑。现在，想想谁真正在使用 UTown——学生们。我问过你们，校园的哪个部分更适合学习、分享和互动。一位学生指出，旧的、紧凑的一侧迫使你进入一种特定的专注状态，就像传统的图书馆环境。然而，另一位学生正确地指出，旧建筑非常令人困惑；因为布局不直观，你甚至很难找到食堂。另一方面，UTown 是一个对学习和协作来说好得多的环境。它有广阔的学习区、大量供笔记本电脑使用的电源插座，以及像 24/7 星巴克这样的地方。它并没有被高耸入云的建筑所主导。相反，这种设计自然地鼓励人们相互偶遇、建立联系和互动。大学的繁荣依赖于知识的创造和分享，而为了实现这一点，人们需要能够促进这种意外互动的空间。将咖啡馆和绿地置于中心位置是设计思维的刻意选择，目的是培养这种社区参与感。另一个很好的例子是纬壹科技城（One North）。这里有人在纬壹工作吗？纬壹最初是由裕廊集团（JTC）建造的。从历史上看，JTC 在建造传统工厂方面非常出色，在传统工厂中，成功意味着极致的空间效率和实用性。他们把那种建造工厂的思维模式应用到了纬壹科技城，理所当然地认为研发设施只需要最大的空间利用率。但很快，他们就意识到研发中心的功能更像是一个大学校园。初创企业和研究人员迫切需要互动、交流想法并进行合作。纬壹最初的物理布局实际上阻碍了这一点；人们彼此之间从不碰面。因此，几年前，他们不得不进行一次大规模的翻修——甚至在物理上改变了自动扶梯的流向——专门为了迫使更多的人流行走交汇，鼓励来自不同公司的员工进行随意的互动。所以，虽然效率在某些情况下仍然至关重要——比如机场，那里需要纯粹的实用性，这样乘客才不会迷路而错过航班——但当你是为了创新、社区或舒适度而设计时，将纯粹的效率凌驾于人类体验之上将是一个巨大的失败。

  - [18:35 - 18:40] **The Power of Observation and the AEIOU Framework**

    There is a classic video demonstrating the IDEO shopping cart redesign from the late 1990s. How many of you have seen this video before taking this course? A few of you, okay. This is a very famous case study that essentially put IDEO on the world stage for Design Thinking. A key takeaway from IDEO's methodology is their intense reliance on field observation rather than traditional market research. Why do you think physical observation is so much more effective than just sending out a questionnaire survey? As a student correctly noted, our behaviors are highly dependent on our environment and surroundings. When you just ask someone to explain their habits, they might not be fully capable of describing the complete picture of their pain points. We are inherently biased. When we recount our experiences, we carry our own biases, and the designers drafting the survey also carry their own biases regarding what questions are worth asking. Observing users in their natural setting bypasses those filters. To help structure these observations so they don't become chaotic, there is a very useful framework known as AEIOU, which stands for Activities, Environments, Interactions, Objects, and Users. Let's say you are observing a concession stand at a football game. The "Activity" is the act of ordering food. The "Environment" encompasses the details of the overall physical space—is it crowded, loud, or poorly lit? "Interactions" are how the fans communicate with the staff. "Objects" are the cash registers, menus, or the food packaging. And "Users" are the hungry fans themselves.
