The entire point of frameworks like AEIOU is to systematically document what users truly struggle with in reality, so you can discern if what they *say* their problem is matches the *actual* root problem preventing a smooth experience.

    有一个经典的视频展示了 20 世纪 90 年代末 IDEO 重新设计购物车的案例。在修这门课之前，你们有多少人看过这个视频？有几个人，好的。这是一个非常著名的案例研究，它本质上将 IDEO 推上了设计思维的世界舞台。从 IDEO 的方法论中得出的一个关键结论是，他们强烈依赖实地观察，而不是传统的市场调查。你为什么认为实地观察比仅仅发送问卷调查有效得多？正如一位学生正确指出的，我们的行为高度依赖于我们的环境和周围的事物。当你只是要求某人解释他们的习惯时，他们可能无法完全描述出他们痛点的全貌。我们天生就有偏见。当我们讲述我们的经历时，我们带着自己的偏见，而起草调查问卷的设计师也带着他们自己关于哪些问题值得问的偏见。在用户的自然环境中观察他们可以绕过这些过滤器。为了帮助构建这些观察结果，使其不至于变得混乱，有一个非常有用的框架被称为 AEIOU，它代表活动（Activities）、环境（Environments）、互动（Interactions）、物体（Objects）和用户（Users）。假设你正在观察一场足球比赛的特许小吃摊。“活动”是点餐的行为。“环境”涵盖了整体物理空间的细节——它是否拥挤、嘈杂或光线昏暗？“互动”是球迷与工作人员如何沟通。“物体”是收银机、菜单或食品包装。而“用户”是饥饿的球迷本身。像 AEIOU 这样的框架的全部意义在于系统地记录用户在现实中真正挣扎的地方，这样你就可以辨别他们口中所*说*的问题是否与阻碍顺畅体验的*实际*根本问题相匹配。

  - [18:40 - 18:48] **Mastering the Process Over Technology**

    Reflecting on the IDEO case, compared to other product design firms, what makes IDEO uniquely successful? Someone mentioned "thinking outside the box" and "being creative." While true, the fundamental differentiator—which David Kelley from IDEO states explicitly—is that they are not experts in the subject matter or the specific technologies of the clients they serve. They design everything from toys to surgical tools to chairs, yet they are not toy makers or doctors. IDEO excels because they are masters of the *problem-solving process*. They adhere strictly to their methodology: empathize, define, ideate, prototype, test. But why is mastering the process more important than mastering the technology? Because if you only rely on a spark of genius or a random moment of inspiration, you become a "one-hit wonder." Without a replicable process, your success is just pure luck. Sometimes you get it right, and sometimes you get it disastrously wrong. However, if you master the methodology, you master the outcome. You can predictably and reliably produce innovative solutions time after time. Think about Statistical Process Control (SPC) in manufacturing. If you are using an injection molding machine, the very first thing you must do is control the baseline process variables—the temperature, the pressure, the time. It is actually better to have a process that produces equally bad results consistently than a highly random process that occasionally produces a perfect part. Once a process is controlled and consistent, you can incrementally tune it until it produces consistently excellent results. The same logic applies universally. A solid HR hiring process drastically increases your baseline probability of hiring the right candidate out of a hundred applicants, preventing you from relying entirely on gut feeling. This is exactly why rigorous research methodology is the most critical element in scientific academia. When a researcher presents a novel finding, I often do not know if their final conclusion is factually correct because the research is cutting-edge; there is no existing answer to verify against. A PhD student could theoretically present a fabricated conclusion. The only way I can trust the validity of the result is by rigorously evaluating the methodology they used to arrive there. The process guarantees the credibility of the outcome.

    回顾 IDEO 的案例，与其他产品设计公司相比，是什么让 IDEO 取得了独一无二的成功？有人提到“跳出框框思考”和“具有创造力”。虽然这是对的，但最根本的区别——正如 IDEO 的大卫·凯利明确指出的——在于他们并非所服务客户的学科或特定技术领域的专家。他们设计从玩具到手术工具再到椅子的所有东西，但他们并不是玩具制造商或医生。IDEO 的卓越之处在于他们是*解决问题过程*的大师。他们严格遵守他们的方法论：共情、定义、构思、原型、测试。但为什么掌握过程比掌握技术更重要？因为如果你仅仅依赖天才的火花或随机的灵感瞬间，你就会成为“昙花一现”的奇迹。没有一个可复制的过程，你的成功就纯粹是运气。有时你做对了，而有时你错得离谱。然而，如果你掌握了方法论，你就掌握了结果。你可以一次又一次可预测地、可靠地产生创新的解决方案。想想制造业中的统计过程控制（SPC）。如果你使用注塑机，你必须做的第一件事就是控制基准过程变量——温度、压力、时间。拥有一个始终如一地产生同样糟糕结果的过程，实际上比一个偶尔产生完美零件的高度随机的过程要好。一旦过程得到控制并保持一致，你就可以逐步调整它，直到它始终产生出色的结果。同样的逻辑具有普遍适用性。一个坚实的 HR 招聘流程会大大增加你从一百名申请者中聘用合适候选人的基准概率，从而防止你完全依赖直觉。这正是为什么严谨的研究方**是科学学术界最关键的元素。当一个研究人员展示一个新颖的发现时，我通常不知道他们的最终结论在事实上是否正确，因为研究是前沿的；没有现成的答案可以用来验证。从理论上讲，一个博士生可能会提出一个捏造的结论。我能信任结果有效性的唯一方法是严格评估他们得出结论所使用的方法论。过程保证了结果的信誉。

  - [18:48 - 18:56] **Contextual Design: Restaurants for Solitary Diners and Club Med**

    To illustrate how context dictates design, I used to give my students an exercise: design a restaurant catering specifically to solitary diners. If you observe people eating alone, what behaviors do you immediately notice? A student mentioned they are usually glued to their phones, watching dramas. But why? Do they actually want to watch a drama, or is it a coping mechanism? There is still a lingering social stigma associated with eating alone, an assumption that you are lonely or antisocial. Staring at a phone signals to the room, "I am busy, I have things to do," which effectively minimizes the social awkwardness. Therefore, if you are designing a lunchtime restaurant for solo office workers, you need to minimize this awkwardness physically. You certainly wouldn't place their tables directly in the center of the room like a grand display. You might design Japanese-style enclosed cubicles, like those found in famous ramen chains, offering complete privacy. These diners want to eat quickly, avoid unwanted interaction, and leave. Now, let's completely shift the context using the exact same target demographic: singles. Imagine designing a restaurant located in a residential neighborhood heavily populated by single people who live alone, but this restaurant is designed for weekend dinners. Would you build isolation cubicles here? No. These individuals have likely been working in isolation or living alone all week. By the weekend, they are incredibly bored and likely crave human interaction. The design needs to reflect this entirely different "job to be done." A student correctly suggested designing the restaurant around a central bar where the chef prepares the food facing the diners. This layout removes the pressure of forced face-to-face interaction with a stranger but allows the diner to casually engage in conversation with the staff or the chef, thereby easing them into social engagement. The context of *when* and *why* they are dining alone fundamentally flips the architectural requirements of the restaurant. We see this concept of forced versus optional interaction at resorts like Club Med. Club Med is famous for its all-inclusive model where "G.O.s" (Gentils Organisateurs) purposely sit with guests during meals to make them feel welcome and foster a lively atmosphere. It's a great concept, but if you are a family seeking a quiet, private dinner, the constant intrusions can be annoying. Therefore, from a user experience perspective, there needs to be a clear signaling mechanism—like a sign on the table—that politely indicates whether the guests are open to interaction or prefer to be left alone.

    为了说明环境背景如何决定设计，我过去常给学生布置一个练习：设计一家专门迎合独自用餐者的餐厅。如果你观察独自吃饭的人，你立刻会注意到什么行为？一位学生提到他们通常紧盯着手机看剧。但为什么呢？他们是真的想看剧，还是这是一种应对机制？在社会上，独自吃饭仍然带有一种挥之不去的污名化，人们会假设你很孤独或反社会。盯着手机看是在向房间里的人发出信号：“我很忙，我有事情要做”，这有效地最大程度地减少了社交尴尬。因此，如果你正在为独自吃午餐的上班族设计一家餐厅，你需要在物理布局上将这种尴尬降到最低。你当然不会把他们的桌子直接放在房间的正中央，像个盛大的展览一样。你可能会设计日式封闭式隔间，就像那些著名的拉面连锁店里的那样，提供完全的隐私。这些用餐者想要快速吃完，避免不必要的互动，然后离开。现在，让我们使用完全相同的目标人群——单身人士，来完全改变环境背景。想象一下，设计一家位于有大量独居单身人士居住的住宅区内的餐厅，但这是一家专为周末晚餐设计的餐厅。你会在这里建造隔离隔间吗？不会。这些人在过去的一周里很可能一直在孤立地工作或独自生活。到了周末，他们感到极其无聊，可能非常渴望人际互动。设计需要反映这种完全不同的“待办任务”（job to be done）。一位学生正确地建议围绕一个中央吧台来设计餐厅，厨师面对着食客准备食物。这种布局消除了与陌生人被迫面对面互动的压力，但允许食客随意地与工作人员或厨师进行交谈，从而让他们轻松地参与到社交互动中。他们*何时*以及*为什么*独自用餐的环境背景从根本上颠覆了餐厅的建筑要求。我们在 Club Med（地中海俱乐部）等度假村看到了这种强制性与选择性互动的概念。Club Med 以其全包模式而闻名，在那里，“G.O.”（亲切的组织者）会在用餐时故意与客人坐在一起，让他们感到受欢迎并营造活跃的气氛。这是一个很棒的概念，但如果你是一个寻求安静、私人晚餐的家庭，不断的打扰可能会令人烦恼。因此，从用户体验的角度来看，需要有一个清晰的信号机制——比如桌子上的一个标志——礼貌地表明客人是愿意互动还是更喜欢独处。

  - [18:56 - 19:01] **"Jobs to be Done": The Milkshake Case Study**

    This brings us to the famous "Jobs to be Done" theory, often illustrated by Clayton Christensen's milkshake study. There was a fast-food chain in the US struggling to increase its milkshake sales. They tried tweaking the flavor profile based on traditional customer surveys, but sales remained flat. Finally, researchers decided to ditch the surveys and conduct field observations. They observed two completely distinct demographic groups buying milkshakes for two entirely different reasons. The first group bought milkshakes early in the morning. These were office workers facing a long, boring, one-hour commute to work. The "job" they hired the milkshake to do was to keep them occupied during the drive and keep them full until lunch. For a driver, a milkshake is superior to a banana (which requires two hands to peel) or a donut (which crumbles and ruins their work clothes). A milkshake can be cleanly consumed with one hand. Knowing this context, if you want to improve the morning milkshake, you don't necessarily change the flavor. Instead, you make the cup spill-proof, you optimize the drive-through lane so they can grab it and go without queuing, and you make the milkshake thicker so it takes the entire hour-long commute to consume. Now, look at the second demographic: parents buying milkshakes for their young children in the evening alongside dinner. If you apply the morning solution to the evening crowd, it’s a disaster. If you give a small child an extra-thick, giant milkshake, it will take them forever to finish it. The parents will get frustrated, scold the child for delaying bedtime, and likely never buy the milkshake again. For the evening "job," the product needs to be served in smaller portions and be less viscous so it can be consumed quickly. The product is fundamentally the exact same milkshake, but the context and the human need—the job to be done—drastically alter how you must design and deliver the final solution.

    这就引出了著名的“待办任务”（Jobs to be Done）理论，这通常通过克莱顿·克里斯坦森（Clayton Christensen）的奶昔研究来说明。美国有一家快餐连锁店一直在努力提高其奶昔的销量。他们试图根据传统的客户调查来调整口味特征，但销售额依然平平。最后，研究人员决定放弃调查，进行实地观察。他们观察到两个完全不同的人群出于两个完全不同的原因购买奶昔。第一组在清晨购买奶昔。他们是面临长达一小时漫长而无聊通勤的上班族。他们“雇佣”奶昔来完成的“任务”是让他们在开车时有事可做，并让他们在午餐前保持饱腹感。对于司机来说，奶昔优于香蕉（需要用两只手剥皮）或甜甜圈（会掉渣弄脏他们的工作服）。奶昔可以干净利落地单手饮用。了解了这个背景，如果你想改进早上的奶昔，你不一定需要改变口味。相反，你要让杯子防漏，优化得来速（drive-through）车道以便他们无需排队就能拿了就走，并且你要把奶昔做得更浓稠，这样他们就需要花整整一个小时的通勤时间来喝完。现在，看看第二个人群：晚上吃晚饭时给年幼的孩子买奶昔的父母。如果你把早上的解决方案应用到晚上的人群身上，那就是一场灾难。如果你给一个小孩子一杯特浓、巨大的奶昔，他们将需要无休止的时间才能喝完。父母会感到沮丧，会责骂孩子拖延了睡觉时间，并且很可能再也不会买这种奶昔了。对于晚上的“任务”，产品需要分量更小、粘稠度更低，以便能够快速饮用。产品在根本上是完全相同的奶昔，但背景和人类需求——需要完成的任务——极大地改变了你必须如何设计和交付最终的解决方案。

  - [19:01 - 19:05] **The Importance of the Actual Context: The Gillette India Story**

    Some years ago, I came across a very interesting case study about Gillette. It might be a bit of a tabloid version of the story, but it perfectly illustrates a crucial point about observation. Procter & Gamble (P&G) decided they wanted to sell their Gillette razors in the Indian market. They knew that observation was important in design, so they decided to conduct an observational study to see how Indian men shave. However, P&G is headquartered in Boston. So, rather than flying to India, they simply invited Indian students studying in the Boston area—from places like MIT and Boston University—into their laboratory. They observed these students shaving, designed the razor blades based on those observations, and launched the product in India. The product failed completely and didn't sell well at all. They realized something was terribly wrong and finally went to India to investigate. It turns out that Indians in rural India shave very differently from Indian students living in Boston. What do you think the differences might be? As someone pointed out, it's the lack of running water. Many men in rural India live in homes without bright indoor lighting or running tap water. To get enough light, they go outside to shave, using a very small mirror and just a small cup or pail of water. Because they don't have a faucet with running water to constantly flush out the blade, a standard razor clogs immediately. Therefore, a blade designed for this market needs to have much wider gaps between the blades to allow room for the shaving debris, so it can be cleaned with a simple swish in a cup of water. The Indian students in Boston, however, were shaving in American bathrooms with bright lights, big mirrors, and running tap water, so they washed their blades constantly. The designers fundamentally assumed that the physical act of shaving was the only thing that mattered, completely ignoring how the greater environment dictates the process. This reminds me of another story from years ago involving Philips. Philips used to design their irons in Singapore and manufacture them nearby in Batam. They set up a lab in Singapore and actually flew European women all the way over just to observe them ironing clothes. Why not just conduct the design observation in the Netherlands where the actual European context is? The key takeaway here is that when we observe, we must observe in the actual, natural setting. You cannot artificially recreate a context because you simply do not know what you do not know.

    几年前，我看到一个非常有趣的关于吉列（Gillette）的案例研究。这可能有点像小报版本的传闻，但它完美地说明了关于观察的一个关键点。宝洁公司（P&G）决定他们想在印度市场销售吉列剃须刀。他们知道观察在设计中很重要，所以他们决定进行一项观察性研究，看看印度男人是如何剃须的。然而，宝洁公司的总部在波士顿。因此，他们没有飞往印度，而是简单地邀请了在波士顿地区（比如麻省理工学院和波士顿大学）学习的印度学生来到他们的实验室。他们观察这些学生剃须，根据这些观察设计了剃须刀片，并在印度推出了这款产品。该产品一败涂地，根本卖不动。他们意识到出了大问题，最终前往印度进行调查。事实证明，印度农村的印度人剃须的方式与生活在波士顿的印度学生截然不同。你认为区别可能是什么？正如有人指出的，是缺乏自来水。许多印度农村的男性家里没有明亮的室内照明或自来水。为了获得足够的光线，他们走到室外剃须，只用一面非常小的镜子和一小杯或一小桶水。因为他们没有水龙头可以用自来水不断地冲洗刀片，标准的剃须刀会立刻堵塞。因此，为这个市场设计的刀片需要在刀片之间有宽得多的缝隙，为剃须碎屑留出空间，这样只需在水杯里轻轻一涮就能清洗干净。而在波士顿的印度学生是在有明亮灯光、大镜子和自来水的美国浴室里剃须，所以他们会不断地冲洗刀片。设计师们从根本上假设剃须这个物理动作是唯一重要的东西，完全忽略了更大的环境是如何决定这个过程的。这让我想起了多年前另一个涉及飞利浦（Philips）的故事。飞利浦过去在新加坡设计熨斗，并在附近的巴淡岛制造。他们在新加坡建立了一个实验室，实际上大老远把欧洲女性飞过来，仅仅为了观察她们熨衣服。为什么不在拥有真实欧洲环境的荷兰直接进行设计观察呢？这里的关键启示是，当我们进行观察时，我们必须在真实的、自然的环境中进行观察。你不能人为地重构一个环境，因为你根本不知道你不知道什么。

  - [19:05 - 19:11] **Media Richness Theory and Avoiding Lean Data**

    Going back to the IDEO shopping cart video, you notice the team goes to the supermarket, does their observation, and takes dozens of photographs. They then pin these pictures all over their project room walls to discuss them. Why do they rely so heavily on pictures instead of just writing down notes? As the saying goes, a picture paints a thousand words. When you only use text to describe a scene, you rarely capture the full picture; you only write down the specific things you consciously noticed at that exact moment. By taking a photograph, you capture the rich background context, allowing you to notice new details later that you initially missed. This brings us to a concept called Media Richness Theory. In communication, there is a spectrum from "lean" media to "rich" media. When I speak to you face-to-face, it is the richest form of media. You don't just hear my words; you hear my tone, you see my body language, my posture, and my facial expressions. All of these cues help you accurately interpret how serious or confident I am. On the other hand, an email is a very lean medium. If you ask me an unambiguous, unequivocal question—like, "What time and date is the quiz next week?"—I don't need to meet you face-to-face. I can just send a lean email with the exact time and date, and the communication is perfect. But, if you ask me a highly ambiguous question, like, "How difficult is the quiz?" and I reply via email saying "It's not so difficult," you might interpret that in several different ways. However, if we are face-to-face and I laugh, smirk, and say, "Oh, it's not so difficult," you will immediately read my body language and realize, "Ah, it's actually going to be incredibly hard." The more ambiguous and complex the situation, the richer the communication medium must be. The problems we tackle in Design Thinking are inherently messy, emotional, and highly ambiguous. Therefore, you cannot rely on lean surveys or written text; you must use face-to-face observation, videos, and photographs. For example, look at the photograph of the mother in the supermarket with her young son standing inside the shopping cart. If you send that mother a survey, she will claim her child behaves perfectly in the store. She will never admit that he jumps up and stands inside the basket. Furthermore, if you just wrote down "child stands in cart," you would miss crucial context. The photograph might show that the cart is full of heavy canned food, which changes the design implications compared to a cart full of fragile glass bottles. You simply cannot capture that level of nuanced reality with just words.

    回到 IDEO 购物车的视频，你会注意到团队去超市进行观察，并拍了几十张照片。然后他们把这些照片钉在项目室的墙上进行讨论。为什么他们如此依赖照片而不是仅仅记笔记？俗话说，一图胜千言。当你只用文字来描述一个场景时，你很少能捕捉到全貌；你只会写下你在那一刻有意识地注意到的特定事物。通过拍照，你捕捉到了丰富的背景信息，让你以后能注意到你最初错过的全新细节。这就引出了一个叫做“媒介丰富度理论”（Media Richness Theory）的概念。在沟通中，存在一个从“精益/贫乏”（lean）媒介到“丰富”（rich）媒介的光谱。当我和你面对面交谈时，这是最丰富的媒介形式。你不仅听到我的话语；你还听到了我的语气，看到了我的肢体语言、我的姿势和我的面部表情。所有这些线索都能帮助你准确地判断我有多严肃或多自信。另一方面，电子邮件是一种非常精益的媒介。如果你问我一个毫不含糊、明确的问题——比如，“下周测验的时间和日期是什么？”——我不需要和你面对面会谈。我只需发一封包含确切时间和日期的精益邮件，沟通就非常完美。但是，如果你问我一个高度模棱两可的问题，比如，“测验有多难？”而我通过电子邮件回复说“没那么难”，你可能会有几种不同的解读。然而，如果我们在面对面交流，我大笑着、假笑着说，“哦，没那么难”，你立刻就会读懂我的肢体语言，并意识到，“啊，实际上它会非常难。”情况越是模棱两可、越是复杂，所需的沟通媒介就必须越丰富。我们在设计思维中处理的问题本质上是混乱的、情感化的、高度模糊的。因此，你不能依赖精益的调查问卷或书面文字；你必须使用面对面的观察、视频和照片。例如，看看那张在超市里的母亲和她站在购物车里的小儿子的照片。如果你给那位母亲发一份问卷，她会声称她的孩子在店里表现得非常完美。她绝对不会承认他会跳起来站在购物篮里。此外，如果你只是写下“孩子站在购物车里”，你就会错过关键的背景信息。照片可能会显示购物车里装满了沉重的罐装食品，这与装满易碎玻璃瓶的购物车相比，会改变设计的考量。你根本无法仅用文字来捕捉那种细微的现实层面。

  - [19:11 - 19:16] **Extreme User Empathy and Designing for the Unfamiliar**

    Sometimes designers must go to extreme lengths to genuinely empathize with users, especially when the designer's physical reality is completely different from the user's. For example, how does a male automotive engineer design a comfortable car for a pregnant woman? It's incredibly difficult to observe the specific bodily restrictions a pregnant woman faces just by watching her get in and out of a car. You can't truly feel the strain of reaching for a seatbelt or the difficulty of opening a heavy door. To solve this, companies like Ford developed an "empathy belly" or a pregnancy simulation suit. Male engineers wear this heavy, restrictive suit to physically experience the awkwardness and limitations of movement. Similarly, researchers at the MIT AgeLab developed a simulation suit called AGNES (Age Gain Now Empathy System) to simulate the physical constraints of an 80-year-old. The suit restricts joint mobility, adds weight to simulate muscle loss, and includes goggles that simulate blurry vision and yellowing of the eyes. By wearing it, young designers suddenly realize how difficult it is for an elderly person to reach the top shelf in a supermarket or read a standard checkout screen. This firsthand empathy is groundbreaking for design. But this raises a valid question: why force a young man to pretend to be an elderly or pregnant woman? Why not just hire a pregnant woman or an 80-year-old to design the product themselves? The fundamental problem is that while the user intimately knows their pain points, they usually lack the technical engineering and design skills required to build the solution. However, when you *can* match the demographic with the required skill set, the results are phenomenal. For instance, Volvo created the YCC (Your Concept Car), which was designed entirely by a team of female engineers and designers, including Camilla Palmertz and Tatiana Butovitsch Temm. Because they understood their own pain points, they incorporated brilliant features. They included run-flat tires so women wouldn't be stranded on the side of the road needing to manually change a heavy punctured tire. They also completely redesigned the gas cap, creating a capless system so drivers wouldn't damage their fingernails or struggle with tight lids at the gas station. Another fantastic example is IDEO. In 2013, a 89-year-old woman named Barbara Knickerbocker-Beskind saw IDEO's founder, David Kelley, speaking on the television program 60 Minutes. She had a background in occupational therapy and design. She applied to IDEO and, at age 91, was hired to help design products specifically for the elderly. She is the ultimate expert because she is the extreme user, combined with the technical mindset.

    有时，设计师必须竭尽全力去真正与用户产生共情，特别是当设计师的身体现实与用户的现实完全不同时。例如，一个男性汽车工程师如何为孕妇设计一辆舒适的汽车？仅仅通过观察孕妇上下车，是极难发现她所面临的具体身体限制的。你无法真正感受到伸手去拉安全带的拉扯感，或者打开沉重车门的困难。为了解决这个问题，像福特（Ford）这样的公司开发了“共情肚”或怀孕模拟服。男性工程师穿上这种沉重、有束缚感的衣服，从物理上体验移动的尴尬和局限。同样，麻省理工学院老年实验室（MIT AgeLab）的研究人员开发了一种名为 AGNES（立即体验衰老共情系统）的模拟服，以模拟 80 岁老人的身体限制。这套衣服限制了关节的活动能力，增加了重量以模拟肌肉萎缩，还包括模拟视力模糊和眼睛发黄的护目镜。穿上它，年轻的设计师们突然意识到，对老年人来说，够到超市的顶层货架或看清标准的结账屏幕是多么困难。这种第一手的共情对设计来说是开创性的。但这就提出了一个合理的问题：为什么要强迫一个年轻人假装成老年人或孕妇？为什么不直接雇佣一个孕妇或一个 80 岁的老人来自己设计产品呢？根本的问题在于，虽然用户非常了解自己的痛点，但他们通常缺乏构建解决方案所需的技术工程和设计技能。然而，当你*能够*将特定人群与所需技能结合起来时，结果是惊人的。例如，沃尔沃（Volvo）创造了 YCC（你的概念车），这完全是由一个女性工程师和设计师团队设计的，包括 Camilla Palmertz 和 Tatiana Butovitsch Temm。因为她们了解自己的痛点，所以她们加入了非常出色的功能。她们配备了防爆轮胎，这样女性就不会被困在路边，需要手动更换沉重的爆胎。她们还彻底重新设计了油箱盖，创造了一个无盖系统，这样司机在加油站就不会弄坏指甲或费力去拧紧盖子。另一个绝佳的例子是 IDEO。2013年，一位名叫 Barbara Knickerbocker-Beskind 的 89 岁女士在电视节目《60分钟》上看到了 IDEO 的创始人 David Kelley 的讲话。她拥有职业治疗和设计的背景。她向 IDEO 提出了申请，并在 91 岁高龄时被聘用，专门帮助设计针对老年人的产品。她是终极专家，因为她既是极端用户，又兼具技术思维。

  - [19:16 - 19:18] **Air New Zealand's Redesign of Long-Haul Flights**

    A great, modern example of these principles in action is Air New Zealand's partnership with IDEO. In mid-2005, Air New Zealand hired IDEO to completely overhaul their long-haul economy flight experience. Instead of the five-stage model, they used a three-stage design thinking framework: Inspiration, Ideation, and Implementation. For the Inspiration phase, the design team spent seven months conducting rigorous observational research. They flew on long-haul flights across the globe, observing what passengers actually did and felt. They realized that passengers felt trapped, with all control wrested from them—told when to eat, sit, and sleep. Following extensive rapid prototyping and mockup designs during the Ideation phase, they eventually launched the Economy "Skycouch". It’s a brilliant solution: they designed a row of three economy seats with footrests that swing up to form a completely flat bed. A couple could buy the third seat at a discount, or a single person could buy the whole row. This was directly inspired by observing New Zealanders—who travel very long distances to Europe or Asia—struggling with agitated young children. The Skycouch gave them a small, flat play area and bed. They also developed a concept called the "Skynest," which are actual bunk beds in economy class. Its rollout was unfortunately delayed by the COVID-19 pandemic, but they have recently reintroduced the concept.

    这些原则在实践中的一个伟大、现代的例子是新西兰航空（Air New Zealand）与 IDEO 的合作。2005 年中，新西兰航空聘请 IDEO 彻底改造其长途经济舱的飞行体验。他们没有使用五阶段模型，而是使用了三阶段的设计思维框架：灵感、构思和实施。在灵感阶段，设计团队花了七个月的时间进行严格的观察性研究。他们乘坐全球的长途航班，观察乘客们的实际行为和感受。他们意识到乘客感到被困住了，所有的控制权都被剥夺了——被告知什么时候吃饭、坐下和睡觉。在构思阶段进行了大量快速原型制作和模型设计之后，他们最终推出了经济舱“空中沙发”（Skycouch）。这是一个绝妙的解决方案：他们设计了一排三个带有脚踏板的经济舱座位，脚踏板可以向上摆动形成一张完全平坦的床。一对夫妇可以打折购买第三个座位，或者一个人可以买下整排。这直接受到观察新西兰人的启发——他们长途跋涉到欧洲或亚洲，并在旅途中与焦躁不安的年幼孩子作斗争。Skycouch 为他们提供了一个小巧、平坦的游乐区和床铺。他们还开发了一个名为“空中睡眠舱”（Skynest）的概念，即经济舱里真正的双层床。不幸的是，它的推出因 COVID-19 疫情而推迟，但他们最近又重新引入了这个概念。

  - [19:18 - 19:26] **Observing the Unobservable: The Lipstick Diary Study**

    A student just asked an excellent question: What do you do when the nature of the product doesn't allow the designer to observe how it's being used? For example, products used in private, like lipstick or toilet paper. You cannot just follow a user into the bathroom to observe them. In a previous course I taught (IE5123), I challenged my students with exactly this: design a better lipstick. The immediate reaction is usually, "Let's just ask the users what they want." But as we established, direct questioning is flawed because users often can't articulate their latent needs. Someone suggested opening an experience shop where women can test lipsticks, and we observe them there. That's a good idea, but remember the Gillette razor example—we must observe the usage in its *actual, natural setting*, not an artificial laboratory. Since we cannot physically shadow the user everywhere, the classic design thinking solution for this is a "Diary Study." The researchers provided users with a detailed diary and asked them to meticulously log every time they applied lipstick: what time they woke up, when they put it on, what color they chose, the specific location, and the reason why. By capturing the exact context of use, the researchers made a fascinating discovery: a vast number of women were applying their lipstick in their cars while driving or commuting. If you had just interviewed them in a focus group, they likely would never have mentioned the car; they would have defaulted to saying they apply it at their bathroom vanity. So, if you know the context is "applying makeup in a moving car," how does that alter your product design? Well, applying it in a car requires looking in the rearview mirror, which means you only have one hand free to operate the lipstick. Therefore, the cap must be easy to remove with one hand. It also needs to be exceptionally durable and break-proof, because it might be subjected to a bumpy ride or melt in a hot car. Maybe you even design a small mirror directly onto the lipstick tube. A modern update to the diary study would be to ask the users to prop up their mobile phones and record a quick video of themselves applying the lipstick in these specific contexts. Another insight gathered was that women often apply one color in the morning, and then want a different, bolder color in the evening before going out. This directly leads to the ideation of a dual-sided lipstick tube with two different shades for different occasions. You systematically map the context to uncover the hidden design requirements.

    刚才有位同学问了一个极好的问题：当产品的性质不允许设计师去观察它如何被使用时，该怎么办？例如，在私下使用的产品，如口红或卫生纸。你不能直接跟着用户进洗手间去观察他们。在以前我教的一门课（IE5123）上，我给学生们布置的挑战正是这个：设计一款更好的口红。人们的第一反应通常是，“我们直接问用户想要什么吧。”但正如我们已经确定的，直接提问是有缺陷的，因为用户往往无法表达他们潜在的需求。有人建议开一家体验店，让女性在里面试用口红，我们在那里观察她们。这是个好主意，但请记住吉列剃须刀的例子——我们必须在*真实的、自然的环境中*观察使用情况，而不是在人工实验室里。既然我们不能在物理上到处跟踪用户，设计思维中针对这个问题的经典解决方案就是“日记研究”（Diary Study）。研究人员为用户提供了一本日记，并要求她们一丝不苟地记录每次涂口红的情况：她们几点起床，什么时候涂，选择了什么颜色，具体的地点，以及原因。通过捕捉确切的使用背景，研究人员有了一个惊人的发现：大量的女性是在开车或通勤时在车里涂口红的。如果你只是在焦点小组中采访她们，她们可能永远不会提到车里；她们会默认说她们是在浴室的梳妆台前涂的。所以，如果你知道背景是“在行驶的汽车里化妆”，这会如何改变你的产品设计呢？嗯，在车里涂口红需要看后视镜，这意味着你只有一只手可以空出来操作口红。因此，盖子必须容易单手取下。它还需要异常耐用且防碎，因为它可能会经历颠簸的旅程，或者在炎热的车内融化。也许你甚至可以直接在口红管上设计一面小镜子。日记研究在现代的升级版将是要求用户支起他们的手机，在这些特定的背景下录制一段她们自己涂口红的简短视频。收集到的另一个见解是，女性通常在早上涂一种颜色，然后在晚上出门前想要一种不同的、更大胆的颜色。这直接催生了双头口红管的构思，即针对不同场合提供两种不同的色号。你系统地绘制出使用背景，以揭示隐藏的设计需求。

  - [19:26 - 19:31] **From Efficiency to Experience: Designing the MOM Employment Pass Office**

    Let's look at another classic local example. The Ministry of Manpower (MOM) in Singapore actually invited IDEO to redesign the Employment Pass (EP) application process at their service center near the Singapore River. If you look at the history of MOM’s process improvements, they initially used Business Process Re-engineering (BPR). In the late 90s, the first BPR initiative vastly improved efficiency: they reduced processing time from two weeks down to one day, and cut the number of required physical visits from six down to just one. In the early 2000s, another BPR effort further reduced waiting times. However, by 2009, they realized that pure efficiency wasn't enough; they needed to redesign for a great *customer experience*. This is when IDEO stepped in to conduct observational research. One of the specific changes they implemented was installing a children's play area right in the middle of the government agency floor. Why would you want noisy kids playing in an official government pass office? Think about the negative impact if there *isn't* a play area. Parents become highly distracted trying to manage bored, crying children. This distraction leads to miscommunication with the government officers, causing errors in the paperwork and ultimately slowing down the entire operational efficiency of the center. But why are these applicants bringing young kids to the MOM office in the first place? You have to understand their broader context. These are foreigners applying for an Employment Pass; they have quite literally just arrived in Singapore. They might still be living in a hotel, they haven't hired a domestic helper yet, and they have no friends, neighbors, or extended family to look after their children. They have absolutely no choice but to bring their kids with them. You can't just mandate "No Children Allowed." Another interesting design choice was opening up a specific wall to create a large viewing window overlooking the Singapore River. What is the benefit of letting people stare out a window? It's about perception management. It keeps the applicants visually occupied and relaxed so they don't feel impatient while waiting for their queue number. It is exactly the same psychological principle behind putting large mirrors inside elevators—people look at themselves, fix their hair, and by the time they are done, they have arrived at their destination without noticing the wait. These are prime examples of high-touch, human-centric design interventions.

    让我们来看另一个经典的本地例子。新加坡人力部（MOM）实际上曾邀请 IDEO 重新设计其位于新加坡河附近服务中心的就业准证（EP）申请流程。如果你看看人力部流程改进的历史，他们最初使用的是业务流程重组（BPR）。在 90 年代末，第一次 BPR 举措极大地提高了效率：他们将处理时间从两周缩短到一天，并将所需的实地办理次数从六次减少到仅一次。在 2000 年代初，另一次 BPR 努力进一步缩短了等待时间。然而，到了 2009 年，他们意识到纯粹的效率是不够的；他们需要为了打造卓越的*客户体验*而重新设计。就在这时，IDEO 介入进行观察性研究。他们实施的具体改变之一是在政府机构大厅的正中央设置了一个儿童游乐区。你为什么会希望在一个正式的政府通行证办理办公室里有吵闹的孩子在玩耍？想想如果*没有*游乐区会产生的负面影响。父母在试图安抚无聊、哭闹的孩子时会非常分心。这种分心会导致与政府官员的沟通不畅，造成文书工作中的错误，并最终拖慢整个中心的运营效率。但首先，为什么这些申请人会带着年幼的孩子来人力部办公室？你必须了解他们更广泛的背景。这些是申请就业准证的外国人；他们确确实实是刚刚抵达新加坡。他们可能还住在酒店里，还没有雇佣女佣，也没有朋友、邻居或大家庭来照顾他们的孩子。他们绝对别无选择，只能把孩子带在身边。你不能只是一纸禁令“禁止儿童入内”。另一个有趣的设计选择是打通一面特定的墙，创造一个可以俯瞰新加坡河的大观景窗。让人们盯着窗外看有什么好处？这关乎感知管理。它让申请人在视觉上保持忙碌和放松，这样他们在等待排队号码时就不会感到不耐烦。这与在电梯里安装大镜子背后的心理学原理完全相同——人们看着自己，整理头发，等他们弄完的时候，他们已经到达了目的地，根本没有注意到等待的时间。这些都是高接触、以人为本的设计干预的绝佳例子。

  - [19:31 - 19:34] **Comparing Methodologies: Searching for Guiding Principles**

    The ultimate goal of what I am teaching you is not to make you a master of Design Thinking—there are other professors who dedicate entire semesters just to that. My goal is for you to understand the specific contexts in which these methodologies were born and why certain tools are used. This leads directly into next week's topic and your first essay assignment: comparing and contrasting the three major methodologies we have covered (Design Thinking, DMAIC, and BPR). At the beginning of this course, I shared my personal journey of learning many highly specific problem-solving frameworks. Over time, I began to see the generic, underlying guiding principles that connect all of them. Think of it like cooking: you can learn specific methods for Chinese cooking, Indian cooking, or Western cooking. But if you compare them, you extract universal principles, like managing heat so food doesn't stick to the pan. If you look at BPR and DMAIC, they are designed for highly quantifiable, factory-like, or large organizational settings focused on quality, cost, and efficiency. They rely heavily on hard numbers. You rarely see a step demanding that engineers go take photographs of workers' emotions. In contrast, Design Thinking is explicitly built for human-centric, ambiguous product or service problems, which is why it utilizes tools like AEIOU observation, extreme users, and user journey mapping. You need to analyze the three methodologies and deduce the universal principles that cut across them, despite their vastly different contexts.

    我教你们的最终目标并不是让你们成为设计思维的大师——有其他的教授会花整整一个学期的时间专门教这个。我的目标是让你们理解这些方法论是在什么样的特定背景下产生的，以及为什么要使用某些工具。这直接引出了下周的主题和你们的第一篇论文作业：比较和对比我们已经涵盖的三大方法论（设计思维、DMAIC 和 BPR）。在这门课的开头，我分享了我学习许多高度具体的问题解决框架的个人历程。随着时间的推移，我开始看到将它们全部联系起来的通用的、潜在的指导原则。把它想象成做饭：你可以学习中国菜、印度菜或西餐的特定做法。但如果你对它们进行比较，你就会提取出普遍的原则，比如控制火候以使食物不粘锅。如果你看看 BPR 和 DMAIC，它们是为高度可量化的、类似工厂的或大型组织环境设计的，专注于质量、成本和效率。它们严重依赖确凿的数字。你很少会看到有一个步骤要求工程师去拍摄工人情绪的照片。相比之下，设计思维是明确为以人为本的、模糊的产品或服务问题而建立的，这就是为什么它使用了诸如 AEIOU 观察、极端用户和用户旅程地图等工具。你需要分析这三种方法论，并推导出跨越它们的普遍原则，尽管它们的应用背景截然不同。

  - [19:34 - 19:54] **Group Activity and Break**

    (The class took a break and engaged in a group activity or silent reading exercise regarding the comparison of methodologies.)

    （全班休息，并进行了关于方法论比较的小组活动或默读练习。）

  - [19:54 - 19:56] **The Rules of Brainstorming and Deferred Judgment**

    Let's briefly touch on the rules of brainstorming. Why do we have rules like "defer judgment" or "encourage wild ideas"? Why do we focus on inclusion and building on the ideas of others? If a team member proposes Idea A and it is technically terrible, and another proposes Idea B which is equally bad, our natural instinct is to immediately criticize and shut them down. But what if a third person looks at the flawed Idea A and Idea B, combines their underlying concepts, and creates Idea C, which turns out to be brilliant? If you prematurely shut down and discard Ideas A and B, Idea C will never have the opportunity to emerge because the conceptual foundation was destroyed. That is exactly why we enforce these rules during the initial Ideation stage: we want to maximize the sheer quantity of ideas. By definition, our initial, immediate ideas are never truly "out-of-the-box." It takes pushing past the obvious solutions to reach the truly creative ones. I will show you more data on this after the semester break, but even companies like IDEO strictly follow the "go for quantity" rule, even if they don't always formally articulate the cognitive science behind why it works so well. My whole point in teaching this is that when you learn a methodology, do not just blindly accept the steps. If you go to a Design Thinking workshop next week, challenge the instructor. Ask them *why* observation is necessary, or *why* we must take photos. If you truly want to be exceptional at problem-solving, you must constantly ask "why" to understand the mechanics behind the tools.

    让我们简要地谈谈头脑风暴的规则。为什么我们有诸如“延迟评判”或“鼓励疯狂的想法”这样的规则？为什么我们专注于包容性和在他人想法的基础上进行构建？如果一个团队成员提出了想法 A，而它在技术上非常糟糕，另一个提出了同样糟糕的想法 B，我们的本能反应是立即批评并否定它们。但是，如果第三个人看着有缺陷的想法 A 和想法 B，结合它们潜在的概念，创造出想法 C，结果证明这是一个绝妙的主意呢？如果你过早地否定和抛弃了想法 A 和 B，想法 C 将永远没有机会出现，因为概念基础被破坏了。这正是为什么我们在最初的构思阶段要强制执行这些规则：我们希望最大化想法的绝对数量。根据定义，我们最初的、直接的想法从来都不是真正“跳出框框”的。你需要超越那些显而易见的解决方案，才能找到真正具有创造力的方案。在学期中旬的假期之后，我会向你们展示更多关于这方面的数据，但即使是像 IDEO 这样的公司也严格遵循“追求数量”的规则，即使他们并不总是正式地阐明它为何如此有效的认知科学原理。我教这个的全部意义在于，当你们学习一种方法论时，不要只是盲目地接受其中的步骤。如果你下周去参加一个设计思维研讨会，去挑战讲师。问他们*为什么*观察是必要的，或者*为什么*我们必须拍照。如果你真的想在解决问题上出类拔萃，你必须不断地问“为什么”，以理解工具背后的运作机制。

  - [19:56 - 20:07] **Essay Instructions and the Future Value of Problem Definition**

    Before we wrap up, let's go over what you need to do for your first essay assignment. You need to complete an analysis of Design Thinking—outlining the stages, their purposes, the tools used (like journey mapping or AEIOU), and common pitfalls, just as you did for BPR and DMAIC. You can be generic, or you can use a specific case study, like the IDEO shopping cart, to structure your answer. Now, regarding the comparison essay. It has never been easier to generate text, thanks to AI tools like ChatGPT. I am actually struggling with how to best incorporate it into the curriculum, but I am certainly not going to discourage you from using it. However, if you just go to ChatGPT and type a lazy prompt like, "Give me some good guiding principles for problem-solving," it will spit out a completely generic, unhelpful answer. The true value lies in how you prompt the AI. In the era of ChatGPT, the ability to clearly define a problem is vastly more valuable than the ability to find the answer. Finding the answer has become a cheap commodity. In the past, people who could memorize facts or search efficiently had great value. Today, those skills are diminished. It is exactly like the invention of the spell checker: before it, being a great speller was a distinct advantage; today, the gap between a good speller and a bad speller is erased by software. Therefore, the people who know how to deeply and structurally *define* the problem will differentiate themselves. To get a good result for this essay, you shouldn't just ask ChatGPT to compare the methods. You must structure the prompt. Ask yourself: What dimensions am I comparing? What is each method used for? What kinds of problems? (E.g., Design Thinking is for product/user-centric issues; BPR is for large organizational efficiency; DMAIC is for quality and cost). What are the specific stages? Why are they labeled that way? What defines a successful outcome for each? Set up a table with these specific questions across the rows, and the three methodologies across the columns. Use the facts from our lectures as your raw data. By prompting the AI with this strict, evidence-based structure, you will force it to generate accurate, profound insights, and from there, you can intelligently deduce the universal guiding principles of problem-solving. This is an evidence-based approach, which is far better than me just forcing you to memorize a list of principles I wrote on a slide. Please ensure your essay is written in complete sentences and good English so I can evaluate it properly. Thank you very much, and I will see you next week.

    在我们结束之前，让我们过一遍你们第一篇论文作业需要做的事情。你们需要完成对设计思维的分析——概述各个阶段、它们的目的、使用的工具（如旅程地图或 AEIOU）以及常见的陷阱，就像你们对 BPR 和 DMAIC 所做的那样。你可以写得很宽泛，或者你也可以使用一个特定的案例研究，比如 IDEO 的购物车，来构建你的答案。现在，关于比较论文。由于 ChatGPT 等 AI 工具的存在，生成文本变得前所未有的容易。我其实一直在苦恼如何最好地将其纳入课程中，但我绝对不会阻止你们使用它。然而，如果你只是去 ChatGPT 输入一个懒惰的提示词，比如“给我一些解决问题的好指导原则”，它会吐出一个完全通用的、毫无帮助的答案。真正的价值在于你如何向 AI 提供提示。在 ChatGPT 时代，清晰定义问题的能力比寻找答案的能力有价值得多。寻找答案已经成为一种廉价的商品。在过去，能够记住事实或高效搜索的人具有很大的价值。今天，这些技能的价值已经减弱了。这完全就像拼写检查器的发明：在它出现之前，成为一个拼写能手是一个明显的优势；今天，拼写好的人和拼写差的人之间的差距被软件抹平了。因此，那些知道如何深入地、结构化地*定义*问题的人将会脱颖而出。为了在这篇论文中取得好成绩，你不应该只是让 ChatGPT 比较这些方法。你必须构建你的提示词。问问你自己：我正在比较哪些维度？每种方法是用来做什么的？解决什么样的问题？（例如，设计思维是针对产品/以用户为中心的问题；BPR 是为了大型组织的效率；DMAIC 是为了质量和成本）。具体的阶段是什么？它们为什么被这样标记？什么定义了每种方法的成功结果？建立一个表格，行是这些具体的问题，列是这三种方法论。使用我们讲座中的事实作为你的原始数据。通过用这种严格的、基于证据的结构来提示 AI，你将迫使它产生准确、深刻的见解，然后从那里，你可以聪明地推导出解决问题的普遍指导原则。这是一种基于证据的方法，这比我仅仅强迫你们背诵我在幻灯片上写的一长串原则要好得多。请确保你们的论文是用完整的句子和良好的英语写成的，这样我才能正确地评估它。非常感谢，我们下周见。
## Week 5 Topic 6 Problem Definition 09.11
  - [19:08 - 19:15] **Framing the Right Problem and the S.M.A.R.T. Framework**

    And here, the core idea is relevance: you have to solve the right problem, not just solve the problem right. If you look at the DMAIC framework from Six Sigma, the first part of the Define phase talks extensively about the context, the company, the industry they are in, customer complaints, and so on. They want to meticulously frame the problem so that what we are solving actually fits the broader context. This is what I meant earlier about the timeline being a critical factor in the S.M.A.R.T. framework. Saying you want to "reduce customer complaints by 20% within a year" is vastly different from "20% within five years." Being highly specific is paramount. You can always do a quick Google or ChatGPT search to remind yourself of the S.M.A.R.T. framework—Specific, Measurable, Achievable, Relevant, and Time-bound. There is a great book called "Bulletproof Problem Solving" written by two McKinsey consultants, and their primary advice is that when you define a problem, you must explicitly identify the owner. Who asked you to solve this problem, and what is their underlying perspective?

    All problems, to some extent, are embedded in a deeper context. As much as we try to write things down in a problem statement, there are always unwritten expectations and constraints that underpin those written words. Knowing your key stakeholders is crucial because if the problem statement is brief or incomplete for some reason, you have to deduce what the owner truly wants—things they might have forgotten to mention, were reluctant to mention, or simply assumed you knew. For example, your boss might ask you to conduct a Business Process Reengineering (BPR) exercise. They give you a statement, but there is always a bigger objective behind it. What underlying issues actually gave rise to the directive to do BPR? This is similar to the "Double Diamond" model in Design Thinking, specifically the Empathize stage. It teaches you not to just blindly accept the problem handed to you. You have to explore the setting. Ask yourself, "Is there anything else I should uncover so I can refine this problem statement?" Good problem solvers help uncover the true problem the owner actually needs solved.

    Think of it like being a doctor. If you go to the doctor and say, "I'm not feeling well here," and the doctor just takes your word for it and prescribes medicine only for that specific localized symptom without investigating if it's part of a larger, systemic illness, you would say that doctor failed at their job. The same applies to you as a consultant or a problem solver. If you just look at the brief given by your boss and don't bother to explore, discover, or validate if it's the true root problem, you might argue later, "Well, I just solved the exact statement he gave me." But that's a failure of problem-solving. A good problem solver guides the boss to get closer to the true problem statement. However, you also must establish clear boundaries. There are times when a problem is part of a massive systemic issue that you, as a problem solver, simply cannot fix. A doctor might diagnose that a patient's chronic illness is due to poor living conditions. The doctor will tell the patient, "Your illness is caused by your environment, but fixing your housing is beyond my control." You have to draw that boundary and define what is out of scope.

    During this definition stage, it is your job to elicit the criteria for success. How would the decision-maker judge a successful problem-solving effort? The next time your boss asks you to solve a problem, you should ask them: "Boss, five years from now, if this problem is successfully solved, what exactly do you see?" You are essentially forcing your boss to describe the "to-be" state—just like we do in BPR. This question prompts them to think much more deeply about why they want the problem solved and what tangible metrics define success.

    在这里，核心理念是相关性：你必须解决“正确的”问题，而不仅仅是“正确地”解决问题。如果你看看六西格玛的DMAIC框架，在“定义”（Define）阶段的第一部分，大量篇幅都在讨论背景、公司、所处行业、客户投诉等内容。他们希望精心界定问题，以确保我们正在解决的问题真正契合大环境。这就是我刚才提到的S.M.A.R.T.框架中时间线为何如此关键的原因。说“一年内将客户投诉减少20%”与“五年内减少20%”截然不同。具体化（Specific）是至关重要的。你可以随时用Google或ChatGPT查阅S.M.A.R.T.框架——具体（Specific）、可衡量（Measurable）、可实现（Achievable）、相关性（Relevant）和有时限（Time-bound）。有两位麦肯锡顾问写了一本很好的书叫《无懈可击的解决问题之道》（Bulletproof Problem Solving），他们的首要建议是，在定义问题时，必须明确问题的“所有者”。是谁让你解决这个问题的？他们潜在的视角是什么？

    在某种程度上，所有问题都植根于更深层的背景中。无论我们多么努力地将问题写在纸上，总有一些不成文的期望和限制支撑着这些字面上的陈述。了解你的关键利益相关者至关重要，因为如果问题陈述过于简略或不完整，你必须推断出所有者真正想要什么——那些他们可能忘记提及、不愿提及或理所当然以为你已经知道的事情。例如，你的老板可能要求你进行业务流程重组（BPR）。他们给了你一份陈述，但背后总有一个更大的目标。究竟是什么潜在问题促使了进行BPR的指令？这类似于设计思维（Design Thinking）中的“双钻模型”（Double Diamond），特别是“共情”（Empathize）阶段。它教导你不要盲目接受别人交给你的问题。你必须探索背景环境。问问自己：“我还需要挖掘什么其他信息，以便进一步完善这个问题陈述？”优秀的解决问题者会帮助挖掘出问题所有者真正需要解决的真实问题。

    把这想象成当医生。如果你去看医生，说：“我这里不舒服”，如果医生只是相信你的话，只针对那个特定的局部症状开药，而不去调查它是否是更大、系统性疾病的一部分，你会说这个医生失职了。这同样适用于作为顾问或解决问题者的你。如果你只看老板给的简报，而不去探索、发现或验证它是否是真正的根本问题，你事后可能会争辩：“嗯，我只是解决了老板给我的确切问题陈述。”但那是解决问题的失败。一个好的解决问题者会引导老板更接近真实的问题陈述。然而，你也必须建立清晰的边界。有时候，一个问题是庞大系统性问题的一部分，作为解决问题者的你根本无法修复。医生可能会诊断出病人的慢性病是由于恶劣的居住条件引起的。医生会告诉病人：“你的病是环境造成的，但改善你的住房超出了我的控制范围。”你必须划定那个边界，并定义什么是不在范围内的。

    在定义阶段，引出成功的标准是你的工作。决策者将如何评判一次成功的解决问题努力？下次你的老板让你解决问题时，你应该问他们：“老板，五年后，如果这个问题被成功解决了，您具体会看到什么景象？”你实际上是在强迫你的老板描述“未来状态”（to-be state）——就像我们在BPR中所做的那样。这个问题促使他们更深入地思考为什么要解决这个问题，以及究竟哪些有形的指标能定义成功。

  - [19:15 - 19:23] **The Theme Park Example and the MECE Principle**

    Let me give you an example. Most of you have probably watched the video module. Suppose you are hired by a theme park owner in China. The owner says, "I need to improve my profit, and you are given one month. What would you do?" If you just take a wild shot in the dark, what comes to mind? A student asks, "Improve by how much?" That's a very good start—trying to define the specific metric of the problem. But the most important first step is that you have to break the big problem down. The question is, how do you logically break it down? We use what is called the Issue Tree approach. "Profit" is a massive, highly aggregated problem. We need to break it down into smaller, manageable sub-issues so that when you solve one of the sub-issues, you significantly solve the overall problem. You want to identify the right "knobs" to turn. You don't want to work tirelessly on an area where, no matter how hard you try, it barely moves the needle on the overall profit.

    To build this issue tree, we must think in a highly structured, logical way. There are different ways to break down a big issue, but the gold standard rule for branching out is the MECE principle. What does MECE stand for? A student correctly identifies it: Mutually Exclusive, Collectively Exhaustive. Let's look at the math. We know Profit = Revenue - Cost. Immediately, we know that to improve profit, we either have to increase revenue or decrease cost. Your massive problem has now become two smaller problems. Now, applying the MECE principle, how would you break down "Cost"? A student suggests "Fixed cost versus variable cost." Exactly. A very easy way to remember mutually exclusive is using binary thinking—like 1 and 0. What is a fixed cost cannot be a variable cost, and what is a variable cost cannot be a fixed cost. They do not overlap; that is the "Mutually Exclusive" part. The "Collectively Exhaustive" part means that any cost under the sun incurred by the theme park must fit perfectly into one of those two buckets. The two categories exhaust all possible costs.

    Now, how would you further divide the Revenue side? What specific items contribute to a theme park's revenue? You could have revenue from ticket sales, revenue from merchandise, and revenue from Food and Beverage (F&B). You could technically divide it in a binary way—edible revenue versus non-edible revenue—but in the consulting world, you don't always have to force a strict binary structure. As long as the categories (Tickets, Merchandise, F&B) are distinct and don't overlap, and logically cover all revenue streams, that is sufficient. A student asks, "Is it okay to create an 'Others' category under collectively exhaustive?" It's not impossible, but it strongly shows that you are being lazy. It is incredibly tempting to dump everything you can't classify into an "Others" bucket. But the whole point of an issue tree is to force your brain to open up the black box and be painfully specific about what is inside. If you just use "Others," you defeat the entire diagnostic purpose of the tool.

    In the consulting world, they have a saying: "Don't boil the ocean." You don't need to overdo the MECE tree. Usually, building it down to three or four levels is plenty. You don't have to drive down to microscopic variables that only influence a few cents. You need to know your context to determine the required accuracy. In the medical field, a tiny decimal error is life and death, so you must be super precise. But in a business context, a plus or minus 5% or 10% margin of error on millions of dollars is completely fine. You have to constantly judge the immense effort of achieving super precision against the actual practical value you get out of it. Let's map out the mathematical relationships in plain text. Total Revenue = Ticket Revenue + Merchandise Revenue + F&B Revenue. Ticket Revenue itself is a product: Number of Visitors multiplied by Average Ticket Price. You can see how the different branches interact—some are additive, some are multiplicative.

    让我给大家举个例子。你们大多数人可能已经看过了视频模块。假设你受雇于中国的一位主题公园老板。老板说：“我需要提高利润，给你一个月的时间。你会怎么做？”如果你只是盲目地瞎猜，脑海里会浮现出什么？有位同学问：“要提高多少？”这是一个非常好的开端——试图定义问题的具体指标。但最重要的一步是，你必须将大问题进行分解。问题是，你如何合乎逻辑地将其分解？我们使用一种被称为“议题树”（Issue Tree）的方法。“利润”是一个庞大且高度综合的问题。我们需要将其分解为更小、易于管理的子议题，这样当你解决其中一个子议题时，就能大幅解决整体问题。你需要找到正确的“旋钮”来转动。你绝对不想在一个无论你怎么努力都对整体利润毫无影响的领域上白费力气。

    为了构建这棵议题树，我们必须以高度结构化、合乎逻辑的方式进行思考。分解大议题有不同的方法，但展开分支的黄金标准法则是MECE原则。MECE代表什么？有位同学准确地指出了：相互独立，完全穷尽（Mutually Exclusive, Collectively Exhaustive）。让我们看看数学关系。我们知道：利润 = 收入 - 成本。我们立刻明白，要提高利润，要么增加收入，要么降低成本。你庞大的问题现在变成了两个较小的问题。现在，应用MECE原则，你将如何分解“成本”？一位同学建议：“固定成本与可变成本。”完全正确。记住“相互独立”的一个非常简单的方法是使用二元思维——就像1和0。固定成本绝对不可能是可变成本，可变成本也绝对不可能是固定成本。它们没有重叠；这就是“相互独立”的含义。“完全穷尽”的部分意味着主题公园产生的任何成本都必须完美地归入这两个分类之一。这两个类别穷尽了所有可能的成本。

    现在，你将如何进一步划分“收入”部分？哪些具体项目构成了主题公园的收入？你可以有门票销售收入、商品销售收入以及餐饮（F&B）收入。在技术上，你可以用二元方式划分它——可食用收入与不可食用收入——但在咨询领域，你并不总是需要强求严格的二元结构。只要这些类别（门票、商品、餐饮）是截然不同的且不重叠，并且在逻辑上涵盖了所有的收入流，那就足够了。有位同学问：“在‘完全穷尽’的要求下，创建一个‘其他’（Others）类别可以吗？”这并非绝对不行，但这强烈表明你正在偷懒。把所有无法分类的东西全塞进“其他”桶里是非常诱人的。但议题树的全部意义在于强迫你的大脑打开黑匣子，极其详细地弄清楚里面到底有什么。如果你只是使用“其他”，你就完全违背了这个工具的诊断初衷。

    在咨询界，他们有一句俗语：“不要煮沸整个海洋”（Don't boil the ocean）。你不需要把MECE树做得过度复杂。通常，构建三到四个层级就足够了。你没必要深挖到只影响几毛钱的微观变量。你需要了解你的背景环境来决定所需的精确度。在医疗领域，微小的十进制错误就是生与死的问题，所以你必须极其精确。但在商业环境中，数百万美元上下5%或10%的误差幅度是完全可以接受的。你必须不断权衡实现超高精度的巨大努力与其能带来的实际应用价值。让我们用纯文本勾勒出数学关系。总收入 = 门票收入 + 商品收入 + 餐饮收入。门票收入本身是一个乘积：游客数量乘以平均票价。你可以看到不同分支是如何相互作用的——有些是加法，有些是乘法。

  - [19:23 - 19:38] **Applying the Diagnostic Lens to Formulate Solutions**

    So let's break down the fixed costs. What are the fixed costs in a typical theme park? You have land rental, utilities (which generally run at a baseline regardless of crowd size), and permanent staff salaries. What about variable costs? A student suggests maintenance. That is a tricky one. Routine maintenance regardless of usage is a fixed cost, but conditional maintenance—repairing rides because of heavy wear and tear from crowds—is a variable cost. You also have seasonal or temporary workers as a massive variable cost. Now, notice that we have spent all this time drawing boxes, and we actually haven't solved a single problem yet. We don't even know which of these boxes is the root cause of the profit issue. So what is the purpose of this exercise? The purpose is that you have deliberately engineered a diagnostic lens. You have built a customized radar or a pair of magnifying glasses specifically designed to detect issues in this theme park.

    Armed with this MECE checklist, you now go out into the field to measure the actual local conditions. You look at the "Number of Visitors" branch. You collect the data on how many visitors come per week. You discover that the theme park is completely packed. For safety capacity, they max out the number of allowed customers every single day. So, can you improve revenue by increasing the number of visitors? No, expanding is impossible. That entire branch of the tree is instantly ruled out. Next, you look at "Ticket Price." You check the data and find that your park charges exactly on par with major competitors. If you hike the price, customers will simply flock to the competition. So, increasing ticket prices is also a dead end. But then you look at "Merchandise." You realize your park has an incredibly poor selection—just the same old branded water bottles, no seasonal items, no unique toys. Suddenly, this is a massive opportunity for improvement. You analyze the "F&B" branch. For some historical reason—maybe a rule set by the founder decades ago—the park allows visitors to bring in outside food. Visitors are having picnics instead of buying your expensive park food. This is another massive area for intervention.

    You take your lens to the Cost side. You look at "Land Cost" and find out the owner signed an unbreakable 20-year lease agreement. If your mandate is to improve profits within the next five years, that 20-year fixed land cost is entirely out of your control. You cross it off the list. You look at "Temp Workers" and realize you pay them a tiny fraction above the market rate. Even if you ruthlessly slashed their wages to match the exact market average, it wouldn't move the needle on overall profit. It is financially insignificant. So what have you effectively done? You have isolated the noise. You are not going to waste your mental bandwidth fighting legally binding land contracts or squeezing pennies from temp workers. You are going to aggressively target Merchandise and Food & Beverages.

    Now—and only now—do you brainstorm specific solutions. Your grand, vague goal of "increasing profit" has been sharply narrowed down to: "How do we fix low revenue from merchandise?" and "How do we stop bleeding money on F&B?" You can brainstorm targeted solutions: create limited-edition Christmas toys, ban outside food, or rent out vacant park space to established fast-food chains. Because of your issue tree, you can perfectly forecast how these specific solutions drive the ultimate goal. You take the Number of Visitors (which is maxed out) and make a calculated assumption: if we ban outside food, what percentage of these visitors will now buy lunch? Multiply that percentage by the number of visitors, and multiply that by the average spend per meal. You can now present a mathematically traceable pathway to your boss showing exactly how banning outside food flows all the way up the branches to significantly increase the top-line Profit. Most untrained people skip the tree. They just throw a random bunch of ideas at the wall—"Let's fire some staff!" or "Let's build a new roller coaster!"—and they have absolutely no verifiable way to prove which idea will actually impact the bottom line.

    那么，让我们分解一下固定成本。典型主题公园的固定成本有哪些？有土地租金、水电费（无论人群规模如何，通常都在基准线运行）以及全职员工的工资。可变成本呢？有位同学建议“维护费用”。这是一个比较棘手的问题。无论使用情况如何都要进行的常规维护是固定成本，但条件性维护——因为人群密集造成严重磨损而修理游乐设施——则是可变成本。你还将季节性或临时工人作为一项巨大的可变成本。现在请注意，我们花了这么多时间画方框，但实际上我们还没有解决任何一个问题。我们甚至不知道这些方框中哪一个是导致利润问题的根本原因。那么，这个练习的目的是什么？目的是你精心设计了一个诊断透镜。你构建了一个定制的雷达或一副专门设计用来探测这个主题公园问题的放大镜。

    带着这份MECE清单，你现在进入现场去测量实际的当地情况。你查看“游客数量”分支。你收集了每周有多少游客来的数据。你发现主题公园已经完全爆满了。出于安全容量的考虑，他们每天允许进入的顾客数量都达到了极限。那么，你能通过增加游客数量来提高收入吗？不，扩建是不可能的。树的那整个分支立刻被排除了。接下来，你查看“门票价格”。你查看数据后发现，你的公园收费与主要竞争对手完全一致。如果你提高价格，顾客就会简单地涌向竞争对手。因此，提高票价也是一条死胡同。但接着你看了“商品”。你意识到你的公园商品种类极其匮乏——只有那些老掉牙的印有商标的水壶，没有季节性商品，也没有独特的玩具。突然之间，这是一个巨大的改进机会。你分析“餐饮”分支。由于某些历史原因——也许是几十年前创始人设定的规矩——公园允许游客自带外食。游客们在野餐，而不是购买你们昂贵的公园食品。这是另一个需要采取干预措施的巨大领域。

    你将透镜转向成本端。你查看“土地成本”，发现老板签署了一份不可违约的20年租赁协议。如果你的任务是在未来五年内提高利润，那么这20年的固定土地成本完全超出了你的控制范围。你把它从清单上划掉。你查看“临时工”，发现付给他们的工资只比市场价高出极小一点。即使你无情地削减他们的工资以完全匹配市场平均水平，也不会对整体利润产生明显影响。这在财务上微不足道。那么你实际上做了什么？你隔离了噪音。你不会把精力浪费在对抗具有法律约束力的土地合同或从临时工那里榨取几分钱上。你将火力全开，猛攻商品和餐饮领域。

    现在——也只有在现在——你才开始集思广益具体的解决方案。你那“提高利润”的宏大而模糊的目标，已被极其敏锐地缩小为：“我们如何解决商品收入低迷的问题？”以及“我们如何阻止在餐饮上持续亏钱？”你可以头脑风暴出有针对性的解决方案：推出限量版圣诞玩具，禁止自带外食，或者将公园空闲场地出租给知名快餐连锁店。得益于你的议题树，你可以完美预测这些具体解决方案如何推动最终目标的实现。你提取游客数量（已达极限）并做出一个经过计算的假设：如果我们禁止自带外食，这些游客中有百分之几现在会购买午餐？将该百分比乘以游客数量，再乘以每顿饭的平均花费。你现在可以向你的老板展示一条在数学上可追溯的路径，确切地展示禁止自带外食是如何沿着分支一路向上传导，从而显著增加顶层利润的。大多数未经训练的人会跳过这棵树。他们只是胡乱抛出一堆点子——“我们解雇一些员工吧！”或“我们建一个新的过山车吧！”——而他们完全没有可验证的方法来证明哪个点子会真正影响底线利润。

  - [19:38 - 19:46] **Dynamic Scenarios and Quality Function Deployment (QFD)**

    A student poses a great question: "What if it's not that simple? What if the variables are deeply dynamic? For instance, what if decreasing the ticket price or shifting from annual passes to daily tickets impacts how much people spend on merchandise and food?" This is a very sharp observation. The standard issue tree is somewhat static. It assumes a state of equilibrium in your market—that competition and visitor behavior are stable during your analysis phase. But in reality, variables are often intertwined. If you treat your highly lucrative annual pass holders poorly (like Disneyland aggressively shifting focus to daily ticket sales during COVID to extract higher daily margins), those pass holders stop buying food and merchandise. You are actively degrading one revenue branch to pump up another. This stops being a simple optimization problem and enters the realm of core strategic trade-offs. Do you want to be a premium, high-margin, low-volume park, or a mass-market, high-volume, low-margin park? If a massive new competitor opens across the street, fundamentally altering the entire market dynamic, tweaking the price of your hotdogs isn't going to save you. You might need an entirely new strategic framework.

    But how do we handle it when solving one problem actively creates a new problem elsewhere? In product design engineering, we have a specific framework for this called Quality Function Deployment, or QFD. Let's step away from the theme park and look at a mobile phone. What do customers want in a mobile phone? They want a massive, brilliant screen. They want a battery that lasts all week. And they want the phone to be incredibly lightweight and thin. If your engineering solution is simply to "install a bigger screen," you have successfully met the first customer requirement. However, a bigger screen instantly applies a heavy negative impact to the battery life (because it draws massive power) and a negative impact to the weight (because the physical glass is heavy). By solving one variable, you have severely compromised two others.

    In a QFD matrix, you map these out. A "plus" means the feature supports the goal; a "minus" means it detracts from it. The true challenge of engineering creativity—the reason why Apple and Samsung engineers get paid millions of dollars—is finding the breakthrough solution that destroys the trade-off. Can you invent a revolutionary new OLED material that is larger, but actually consumes *less* power and weighs *less* than the previous generation? When an engineering team discovers a new material or software architecture that achieves conflicting goals simultaneously without triggering the negative cascading effects, that is true innovation. That is how companies reap massive profits. It is the relentless pursuit of meeting complex, conflicting goals all at once.

    有位同学提出了一个很好的问题：“如果情况没那么简单怎么办？如果变量是高度动态的呢？例如，如果降低票价或从年卡转向日票，会影响人们在商品和食物上的支出怎么办？”这是一个非常敏锐的观察。标准的议题树多少有些静态。它假设你的市场处于一种均衡状态——在你的分析阶段，竞争和游客行为是稳定的。但在现实中，变量往往是交织在一起的。如果你慢待了利润丰厚的年卡持有者（就像迪士尼在疫情期间为了榨取更高的每日利润率，而将重心大举转移到单日门票销售上），那些年卡持有者就会停止购买食物和商品。你正在主动牺牲一个收入分支来推高另一个分支。这不再是一个简单的优化问题，而是进入了核心战略权衡的领域。你是想成为一个高端、高利润率、低客流量的公园，还是一个大众市场、高客流量、低利润率的公园？如果街对面开了一家庞大的新竞争对手，从根本上改变了整个市场格局，那么微调热狗的价格是救不了你的。你可能需要一个全新的战略框架。

    但是，当解决一个问题主动在别处制造了新问题时，我们该如何处理？在产品设计工程中，我们有一个专门针对这种情况的框架，叫做“质量功能展开”（Quality Function Deployment），简称QFD。让我们暂且把主题公园放一边，看看手机。顾客对手机有什么要求？他们想要一块巨大、明亮的屏幕。他们想要一块能用一整周的电池。他们还希望手机极其轻薄。如果你的工程解决方案仅仅是“安装一个更大的屏幕”，你已经成功满足了第一个客户需求。然而，更大的屏幕会立刻对电池寿命产生严重的负面影响（因为它耗电量巨大），并且对重量产生负面影响（因为物理玻璃很重）。通过解决一个变量，你严重损害了另外两个变量。

    在QFD矩阵中，你需要将这些绘制出来。“加号”意味着该功能支持该目标；“减号”意味着它阻碍该目标。工程创造力的真正挑战——也是苹果和三星工程师之所以能拿到数百万美元高薪的原因——在于找到能够打破这种权衡取舍的突破性解决方案。你能否发明一种革命性的新型OLED材料，它不仅尺寸更大，而且实际上比上一代耗电更少、重量更轻？当一个工程团队发现了一种新材料或软件架构，能够同时实现相互冲突的目标，而不会引发负面的连锁反应时，这才是真正的创新。这也是公司获取巨额利润的方式。这就是同时实现复杂、冲突目标的孜孜不倦的追求。

  - [19:46 - 19:54] **Process-Based MECE and Time Constraints**

    Let's revisit an example we discussed two weeks ago: the QB House hair salon. If our core problem definition was that the overall hair cutting process took too long, and I asked you to build a MECE issue tree based on the customer journey process, how would you structure it? A student correctly breaks it down sequentially: First, the customer books the appointment. Then they travel to the salon. Then they wait in the queue. Then the actual haircut happens. Then they pay, and finally, they leave. This sequence perfectly satisfies the MECE rule. Why? Because the time spent traveling is mutually exclusive from the time spent cutting hair. You cannot be cutting hair while you are booking an appointment on your phone. And collectively, those distinct steps account for 100% of the total time the customer spends interacting with your service.

    Once you have this process tree, you quantify the time spent at each node. Let's say booking takes 5 minutes, traveling takes 30 minutes, waiting takes 10 minutes, the actual cut takes 10 minutes, and the payment takes another 10 minutes. Where do you focus your energy? You don't build a massive, complex "super app" that attempts to fix everything at once. That's a terrible way to solve problems. You identify that travel time and waiting time are the biggest bottlenecks. If you build an app specifically tailored just to reduce booking friction and eliminate physical waiting times, you directly impact the total time equation. You avoid the "small potatoes" and focus entirely on the steps that dominate the outcome. This process-based approach is exactly what you do during the journey mapping phase of Design Thinking—you map out the pain points sequentially and attack the largest ones.

    A student then asks an excellent question about the timeline: "How does the timeframe affect our choice of solutions?" Let's go back to our theme park F&B problem. We discovered we aren't making enough money on food. I mentioned earlier that we could either rent out the space to fast-food chains, or we could build and operate our own proprietary, high-end theme park restaurant. If your boss demands a profit increase within one year, that completely dictates your solution. Designing a restaurant, acquiring health and safety licenses, hiring specialized executive chefs, and building the facility will easily take three years. Therefore, building your own restaurant is mathematically eliminated as a viable solution due to the time constraint. You are forced to pursue the faster, perhaps slightly less profitable, short-term solution of renting the space to McDonald's or KFC. We saw this exact dynamic during the COVID pandemic. Companies had to deploy highly imperfect, costly, but *immediate* short-term solutions because they simply could not afford to wait years for the optimal long-term structural fix.

    让我们回顾一下两周前讨论过的例子：QB House理发店。如果我们对核心问题的定义是整体理发过程耗时太长，而我要求你们基于客户旅程流程构建一棵MECE议题树，你们会如何构建？一位同学准确地按顺序进行了分解：首先，客户预约。然后他们前往理发店。然后他们排队等候。接着是实际的理发环节。然后他们付款，最后离开。这个顺序完美地满足了MECE法则。为什么？因为花在路上的时间与理发的时间是相互独立的。你不可能在手机上预约的同时正在理发。总的来说，这些截然不同的步骤占了客户与你们的服务互动的总时间的100%。

    一旦你有了这个流程树，你就可以量化在每个节点花费的时间。假设预约需要5分钟，路上需要30分钟，等候需要10分钟，实际理发需要10分钟，付款又需要10分钟。你的精力应该集中在哪里？你绝对不要去构建一个庞大复杂的“超级应用”试图一次性解决所有问题。那是非常糟糕的解决问题的方式。你要识别出路途时间和等待时间是最大的瓶颈。如果你专门开发一个旨在减少预约摩擦、消除物理等待时间的应用，你就直接影响了总时间方程。你要避开那些“芝麻绿豆”的小事，将全部精力集中在主导结果的步骤上。这种基于流程的方法正是你们在设计思维（Design Thinking）的旅程映射阶段所做的——你们按顺序绘制出痛点，并集中火力攻击最大的那些。

    随后，一位同学提出了一个关于时间线的绝佳问题：“时间框架如何影响我们对解决方案的选择？”让我们回到主题公园餐饮问题。我们发现我们在食物上赚的钱不够。我之前提到过，我们要么把场地租给快餐连锁店，要么我们自己建造并运营我们自己专有的、高端的主题公园餐厅。如果你的老板要求在一年内提高利润，这完全决定了你的解决方案。设计一家餐厅、获取卫生和安全许可、聘请专业的行政总厨以及建设设施，轻而易举就需要三年时间。因此，受时间限制，自己开餐厅在数学上被直接淘汰了。你被迫寻求更快、也许利润稍低一些的短期解决方案——将场地租给麦当劳或肯德基。在新冠疫情期间，我们看到了完全一样的动态。企业不得不部署极其不完美、成本高昂但立竿见影的短期解决方案，因为他们根本等不起数年去实施最优的长期结构性修复。

  - [19:54 - 20:01] **Top-Down Frameworks and Thinking Fast and Slow**

    Up to this point, we've discussed building issue trees from the ground up, generating specific questions to form a hierarchy. But there are also times when you can use established, top-down frameworks. You might borrow external knowledge like Porter's Five Forces, a PEST analysis, a SWOT analysis, or the Fishbone (Ishikawa) diagram often used in manufacturing. The Fishbone diagram is essentially a predefined MECE framework. It forces you to categorize any shop-floor problem into Man, Machine, Material, or Method (and sometimes Measurement and Milieu). The entire goal of these structured frameworks is to force comprehensiveness. We explicitly want to stop ourselves from jumping to conclusions and only treating the most visible, glaring symptom. We want to force our brains to be rigorously logical, leaving no stone unturned in the pursuit of the true root cause.

    But it turns out, humans are exceptionally bad at this. When we see a problem, our overwhelming biological urge is to instantly jump to a solution. Why? Because structured thinking requires what Daniel Kahneman refers to as "System 2" processing—conscious, effortful, deeply analytical thought. System 2 is neurologically exhausting. Your brain despises using it unless the payoff is massively obvious. Instead, your brain wants to use "System 1"—automatic, rapid, pattern-matching processing. You see a fire; you don't build an issue tree to analyze the combustion triangle. You just run. This flight, fight, or freeze response is deeply hardwired into us for survival. Most of the time in business, we default to System 1. We say, "Oh, I've seen a profit issue like this before; last time I fired the marketing team, so let's just do that again." We refuse to open the black box because it hurts our brains to do so.

    I have an image on the slides that illustrates this beautifully. It shows a massive line of police officers slowly walking shoulder-to-shoulder across a vast open field. When the police have absolutely no clue where the murder weapon or piece of evidence is, they form a strict line and sweep the area systematically, inch by inch. They do this to guarantee that not a single centimeter is left unchecked. It consumes a tremendous amount of time and manpower, but it eliminates blind spots. Building an issue tree is the intellectual equivalent of a police sweep. When you have no immediate clue what the root cause of a business failure is, you are forced to systematically sweep the profit logic. It's tough, slow work, but a year later, your logic is fully traceable. You can defend exactly why you chose to attack a specific variable.

    到目前为止，我们已经讨论了如何自下而上地构建议题树，生成具体的问题以形成层级结构。但有时候你也可以使用既定的、自上而下的框架。你可能会借用外部知识，如波特五力模型（Porter's Five Forces）、PEST分析、SWOT分析，或者制造业中常用的鱼骨图（石川图）。鱼骨图本质上是一个预先定义好的MECE框架。它迫使你将任何车间问题归类为“人、机、料、法”（Man, Machine, Material, Method，有时还包括测量和环境）。这些结构化框架的全部目标就是强制实现全面性。我们明确希望阻止自己妄下结论，只处理最明显、最刺眼的症状。我们希望强迫我们的大脑保持严谨的逻辑，在追寻真正根本原因的过程中不遗漏任何线索。

    但事实证明，人类极其不擅长做这件事。当我们看到一个问题时，我们在生物学上压倒性的冲动是立即跳到解决方案。为什么？因为结构化思维需要丹尼尔·卡尼曼（Daniel Kahneman）所说的“系统2”（System 2）处理模式——有意识的、费力的、深度的分析性思考。系统2在神经学上是令人筋疲力尽的。你的大脑极其厌恶使用它，除非回报极其明显。相反，你的大脑喜欢使用“系统1”（System 1）——自动的、快速的、模式匹配的处理模式。你看到火灾；你不会去画个议题树来分析燃烧的三角要素。你只会赶紧跑。这种战斗、逃跑或僵住（fight, flight, or freeze）的反应，为了生存已经深深地硬连线到了我们的身体里。在商业环境中，大多数时候我们默认使用系统1。我们会说：“哦，我以前见过这种利润问题；上次我解雇了营销团队，那我们这次再照做吧。”我们拒绝打开黑匣子，因为这么做会让大脑感到痛苦。

    我的幻灯片上有一张图片非常完美地说明了这一点。它展示了一长排警察肩并肩地缓慢穿过一片广阔的空地。当警察对凶器或证据藏在哪里毫无头绪时，他们会排成一条严密的直线，一点一点地系统地扫荡该区域。他们这样做是为了确保没有任何一厘米的土地被遗漏。这消耗了大量的时间和人力，但它消除了盲区。构建议题树在智力层面等同于警方的地毯式搜查。当你对商业失败的根本原因毫无头绪时，你被迫系统地扫荡利润逻辑。这是一项艰苦、缓慢的工作，但一年后，你的逻辑是完全可追溯的。你可以确切地为自己为什么要攻击某个特定的变量辩护。

  - [20:01 - 20:09] **Evolutionary Neuroscience and the Body Budget**

    I want to show you a fascinating video by Lisa Feldman Barrett, a renowned neuroscientist. In the video, she argues that the deeply ingrained belief that our brains evolved in an upward progression strictly for "advanced thinking" is a profound misconception. If we go back 500 million years to the Cambrian period, animals began hunting each other in a fierce evolutionary arms race. The most critical question for survival was: "Is that blob in the distance something I can eat, or is it going to eat me?" In that brutally competitive environment, energy efficiency was the absolute key to survival. If you chased prey too slowly, another predator got it. If you burned massive amounts of energy running away from a threat that didn't actually exist, you wasted precious calories you desperately needed to survive the night.

    She explains that your brain functions exactly like a financial budget, but for your body's resources—tracking water, salt, and glucose. Every time you run or swim, you make a withdrawal. Every time you eat or sleep, you make a deposit. Your brain's primary evolutionary job is not to ponder philosophy; its job is to run this biological body budget with extreme efficiency so you can survive to pass on your genes. Therefore, our brains are biologically hardwired to be lazy. We avoid conscious, complex thinking because "thinking" burns massive amounts of glucose. Millions of years ago, sugar and calories were incredibly difficult to come by. Our neural circuitry has simply not caught up to the modern era where a massive influx of calories is available at the nearest convenience store. Our brains still operate on the ancient assumption that energy is critically scarce.

    This evolutionary lag applies to many aspects of modern life. For instance, there is a fascinating theory regarding the obesity epidemic. Human digestion evolved alongside food that required immense mechanical effort to break down. We had to chew heavily fibrous, tough foods. Because chewing took a long time, the biological feedback loop—the signal traveling from the stomach to the brain telling you "I am full, stop eating"—had plenty of time to engage before you over-consumed calories. But in the modern era, food manufacturers engineered highly processed, ultra-chewable, melt-in-your-mouth foods. You can inhale thousands of calories in minutes. Because you eat so incredibly fast, by the time the chemical signal finally reaches your brain to tell you that you've had enough, you have already vastly overeaten. That delayed signal is a direct artifact of millions of years of evolution. Our physical perception simply hasn't adapted to our engineered reality.

    我想给大家看一段非常引人入胜的视频，由著名神经科学家莉莎·费德曼·巴瑞特（Lisa Feldman Barrett）主讲。在视频中，她指出，人们根深蒂固地认为我们大脑的进化是不断向上发展，完全是为了进行“高级思考”，这是一个深刻的误解。如果我们回到5亿年前的寒武纪，动物们开始在激烈的进化军备竞赛中相互捕食。关乎生存的最关键问题是：“远处的那个模糊物体是我能吃的，还是它要吃我？”在那个残酷竞争的环境中，能量效率是生存的绝对关键。如果你追捕猎物的速度太慢，其他捕食者就会捷足先登。如果你消耗大量能量去逃避一个实际上并不存在的威胁，你就会浪费熬过夜晚极其需要的宝贵卡路里。

    她解释说，你的大脑运作起来完全就像一个财务预算系统，只不过它是为你身体的资源做预算——跟踪水分、盐分和葡萄糖。你每次跑步或游泳，都在进行“提款”。你每次进食或睡觉，都在进行“存款”。你的大脑在进化中的首要任务不是思考哲学问题；它的任务是以极高的效率运行这个生物身体预算，以便你能活下来并将基因传递下去。因此，我们的大脑在生物学上被硬连线为“懒惰”。我们避免进行有意识的、复杂的思考，因为“思考”会燃烧大量的葡萄糖。在数百万年前，糖分和卡路里极其难以获得。我们的神经回路根本没有赶上现代社会，如今在最近的便利店里就能轻易获得大量卡路里。我们的大脑仍然基于“能量极度稀缺”这个古老的假设在运作。

    这种进化滞后适用于现代生活的许多方面。例如，关于肥胖症流行有一个非常有趣的理论。人类的消化系统是与那些需要巨大机械力才能分解的食物一起进化的。我们必须咀嚼高纤维、坚硬的食物。因为咀嚼需要很长时间，生物反馈回路——即从胃部传导到大脑告诉它“我饱了，别吃了”的信号——有充足的时间在你摄入过多卡路里之前发挥作用。但在现代，食品制造商设计出了高度加工、极易咀嚼、入口即化的食物。你可以在几分钟内吸入数千卡路里。因为你吃得太快了，当化学信号最终到达你的大脑告诉你已经吃够了的时候，你其实早就已经吃得太多了。那种延迟的信号是数百万年进化的直接产物。我们的生理感知根本还没有适应我们被人工改造过的现实。

  - [20:09 - 20:14] **Group Activity: Preventing Hospital Falls**

    Applying these highly structured, System 2 frameworks to intangible services is notoriously difficult. If you are diagnosing a piece of physical hardware, the boundaries are obvious—you can physically see the components. Service problems are invisible. Some time ago, I was teaching an executive course during the COVID period for a group of hospital nurses. They brought up the massive problem of medication errors—patients receiving the wrong drugs. I asked them to develop a MECE issue tree. Initially, they split it by human error versus non-human error. Then they tried splitting human error by profession: doctors, nurses, and pharmacists. But that wasn't truly MECE, because their tasks heavily overlap in chaotic hospital environments. Later that night, while taking a shower, I realized a much better framework was a process-based MECE approach, exactly like our QB House example. The process is strictly sequential: Prescription, Picking, and Collection. The doctor writes the prescription in the system, the pharmacist physically picks and packages the medicine, and the nurse collects and administers it. By using this sequential lens, a specific clinic can analyze their data and pinpoint exactly where the breakdown occurs. Clinic A might find the root cause is illegible doctor prescriptions, while Clinic B might discover it's a labeling error during the pharmacy picking phase. The diagnostic framework remains universally valid across all healthcare operations, even if the local root causes differ wildly.

    Now, it's time for you to practice forcing your brain out of its lazy state. I have a problem statement for you: "How to prevent falls in hospital X?" Older patients slipping and falling is a massive liability. I am giving you 10 minutes to discuss with your group and construct a MECE issue tree for this problem. Fight the urge to jump straight to random solutions like "buy better non-slip socks." Break down the environment or the process logically. You can draw it on paper, or you can use PowerPoint. If you go to the "Insert" tab in PowerPoint, there is a great feature called SmartArt. Choose the "Hierarchy" option—specifically the horizontal organization chart—to quickly map out your branches. Get started.

    将这些高度结构化的、系统2的框架应用于无形服务是出了名的困难。如果你正在诊断一件物理硬件，边界是显而易见的——你可以亲眼看到各个组件。服务问题则是无形的。前些时候，在疫情期间，我在为一个医院护士团队教授高管课程。她们提出了一个巨大的问题：用药错误——病人收到错误的药物。我要求她们开发一个MECE议题树。起初，她们将其分为人为错误和非人为错误。然后，她们试图按职业细分人为错误：医生、护士和药剂师。但那并不是真正的MECE，因为在混乱的医院环境中，她们的任务严重重叠。那天晚上晚些时候，我在洗澡时意识到，一个好得多的框架是基于流程的MECE方法，完全就像我们QB House的例子一样。这个流程有着严格的先后顺序：开处方（Prescription）、配药（Picking）和领取发放（Collection）。医生在系统中开出处方，药剂师实际挑选并包装药物，护士领取并给药。通过使用这种按顺序排列的透镜，特定的诊所可以分析他们的数据，准确找出故障发生在哪里。诊所A可能会发现根本原因是医生处方字迹潦草，而诊所B可能会发现是药房配药阶段的标签错误。尽管各地的根本原因可能大相径庭，但这个诊断框架在所有医疗运营中普遍适用。

    现在，是时候练习强迫你们的大脑脱离懒惰状态了。我给你们一个问题陈述：“如何预防X医院里的跌倒事件？”老年患者滑倒摔伤是一个巨大的责任风险。我给你们10分钟时间与小组讨论，并针对这个问题构建一棵MECE议题树。抵制住直接跳到像“买更好的防滑袜”这种随机解决方案的冲动。合乎逻辑地分解环境或流程。你们画在纸上，也可以使用PowerPoint。如果你点击PowerPoint中的“插入”（Insert）选项卡，里面有一个很棒的功能叫SmartArt。选择“层次结构”（Hierarchy）选项——特别是水平组织结构图——来快速绘制你们的分支。开始吧。

  - [20:37 - 20:38] **Iterating the Diagnostic Lens**

    And that's where you really have to start hunting for solutions differently, even though the overarching symptom looks the same. Think about it like a surge in car accidents. You have a lot of car accidents, but that's just the symptom. Is the root cause poor eyesight of the drivers? Is it due to hazardous road conditions? Or is it because of faulty traffic lights? It's the exact same symptom, but it can be driven by entirely different underlying causes. Sometimes, you just have to test out a specific diagnostic lens. If that lens doesn't give you clarity, you pivot and try another lens until the picture becomes clear. That is why you often need to iterate your problem-solving process. But theoretically, if you do a bit more homework upfront, put in the effort to brainstorm analytically, and follow the MECE principle, you can come up with a highly effective framework right out of the gate.

    这就是为什么即使总体症状看起来一样，你也必须以不同的方式去寻找解决方案。把它想象成车祸激增。发生了很多车祸，但这只是症状。根本原因是司机的视力不好吗？是由于危险的路况造成的吗？还是因为交通信号灯故障？完全相同的症状，但可能是由截然不同的潜在原因引起的。有时，你只能去尝试一个特定的诊断透镜。如果那个透镜不能让你看清问题，你就转而尝试另一个，直到情况变得明朗。这就是为什么你经常需要迭代你的解决问题流程。但从理论上讲，如果你前期多做一点功课，投入精力进行分析性头脑风暴，并遵循MECE原则，你就能在第一时间得出一个非常有效的框架。

  - [20:38 - 20:41] **Testing ChatGPT on MECE Issue Trees**

    Now, let's try a live experiment. I want you to open up ChatGPT. Try feeding it a prompt and see whether it actually works for this. Tell ChatGPT what a MECE issue tree is, and then ask it to give you a MECE issue tree that explains the root causes of patient falls in a hospital. Let's see what comes up. Okay, look at what it gives you. It's actually giving you a list of solutions, right? At this initial stage of problem definition, you want a diagnostic lens to understand the root causes. It is fundamentally wrong to jump straight to solutions, especially when you do not know which solution is mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive. If you ask it specifically, "Give me an issue tree for falls in the hospital," does it give you a logical, MECE breakdown? No, it really doesn't. A student just shared their screen, and it's clearly not MECE. It might give you an issue tree format, but it is highly unlikely to be strictly MECE unless someone has previously published a perfect report on this exact topic on the web, and the model happened to train heavily on it. So, are you happy with the solution from ChatGPT? Is it genuinely MECE? Not really, right?

    现在，让我们做一个现场实验。我要你们打开ChatGPT。试着给它输入一个提示词，看看它在这方面是否真的管用。告诉ChatGPT什么是MECE议题树，然后让它给你生成一个MECE议题树，来解释医院病房里病人跌倒的根本原因。让我们看看会得出什么。好了，看看它给了你们什么。它实际上给你们的是一份解决方案清单，对吧？在问题定义的初始阶段，你需要的是一个诊断透镜来了解根本原因。直接跳到解决方案从根本上就是错误的，特别是当你不知道哪个解决方案是相互独立且完全穷尽（MECE）的时候。如果你明确要求它：“给我一个关于医院跌倒事件的议题树”，它会给你一个合乎逻辑的、符合MECE的分解吗？不，它真的做不到。刚才有位同学分享了屏幕，它显然不符合MECE。它可能会给你一个议题树的格式，但极不可能是严格遵守MECE原则的，除非以前有人在网上发表过关于这个确切主题的完美报告，并且模型刚好对其进行了大量训练。那么，你们对ChatGPT给出的解决方案满意吗？它真的是MECE的吗？并不完全是，对吧？

  - [20:41 - 20:44] **The Limitations of Large Language Models**

    So, why do you think ChatGPT currently cannot give you a proper MECE issue tree? A student suggests, "Not enough data regarding this specific topic." That is partially correct, but it doesn't get to the core of the issue. The reality is that Large Language Models (LLMs) are simply trained on massive volumes of text data. Their underlying mechanism is fundamentally based on "next token prediction." Out of a thousand possibilities, it mathematically predicts what the most likely next word should be based on the previous words, and it spits that out. If it has ingested a lot of consulting reports that feature issue trees, it might generate an answer that looks seemingly logical on the surface. But the crucial distinction is that the AI does not actually understand logic. It doesn't differentiate between strict binary categories or truly analyze mutual exclusivity. It just predicts the patterns it has seen in its training data. It can churn out incredibly articulate essays, but it is merely predicting the next word sequence. It doesn't mean the sequence is logically sound. If an illogical argument has been repeated enough times in its training data, the model will confidently give you that illogical answer. That is the current limitation of large language models; they are trained to replicate human text patterns, not to execute rigorous logical reasoning. Of course, as these models advance to handle actual logical reasoning, we should be careful. I always joke that one day we might really face a Terminator situation. Seeing the capabilities of drones and AI on the internet today compared to a year ago when I last taught this topic—it is moving incredibly fast and it is quite scary. But for now, its fundamental training prevents it from generating a truly rigorous logical breakdown. As a final test, try prompting it to create an issue tree for improving profit and see if it comes close to the logical framework we manually built earlier today.

    那么，你们认为为什么目前ChatGPT无法给你们一个规范的MECE议题树？有位同学猜测：“关于这个特定主题的数据不够。”这部分正确，但没有触及问题的核心。现实情况是，大型语言模型（LLMs）仅仅是在海量的文本数据上进行训练的。其底层机制从根本上讲是基于“预测下一个词符”（next token prediction）。在成千上万种可能性中，它在数学上预测基于前面的词最可能出现的下一个词是什么，然后把它吐出来。如果它吸收了大量包含议题树的咨询报告，它可能会生成一个表面上看起来合乎逻辑的答案。但关键的区别在于，人工智能实际上并“不理解”逻辑。它不会区分严格的二元类别，也不会真正分析相互独立性。它只是预测在其训练数据中见过的模式。它可以连篇累牍地写出极其流畅的文章，但它仅仅是在预测下一个词的序列。这并不意味着该序列在逻辑上是严密的。如果一个不合逻辑的论点在其训练数据中被重复了足够多次，模型就会自信地给你那个不合逻辑的答案。这就是目前大型语言模型的局限性；它们被训练来复制人类的文本模式，而不是执行严谨的逻辑推理。当然，随着这些模型进步到能够处理实际的逻辑推理时，我们应该小心。我总是开玩笑说，也许有一天我们真的会面临《终结者》里的局面。看看今天互联网上无人机和人工智能的能力，再对比一年前我上次教这个主题时——发展速度快得令人难以置信，确实相当可怕。但就目前而言，其基础的训练方式阻碍了它生成真正严谨的逻辑分解。作为最后的测试，你们可以试着提示它创建一个关于提高利润的议题树，看看它是否接近我们今天早些时候手动构建的逻辑框架。

  - [20:44 - 20:47] **Class Dismissal and Group Assignments**

    Alright, I really don't want to keep you any longer. We will wrap up here. After the break, I will share a specific process map with you all based on what we've discussed today. But by then, you should have a solid idea of what your specific group problem is and have a draft of your issue tree ready to use. Thank you everyone, I will see you in two to three weeks. Please make absolutely sure you have signed the attendance sheet before you leave.

    好了，我真的不想再耽误大家的时间了。我们今天就到这里。休息之后，我将根据我们今天讨论的内容与大家分享一个具体的流程图。但到那时，你们应该对自己小组的具体问题有一个清晰的概念，并准备好你们议题树的草稿以便使用。谢谢大家，我们两三周后再见。离开前请务必确认你们已经签了到。
## Week 6 Consulting in the Age of GenAI 09.18
  - [18:06 - 18:08] **Guess Who Was a Consultant?**

    Let's start by looking at some famous people and guessing who among them has never been a consultant. Most of you probably recognize them. We have the CEO of Google, Sundar Pichai. Then there is Mitt Romney; before he was a senior senator and ran for President of the United States against Obama, he was the Managing Director at Bain & Company. We also have John Legend, the famous musician. When I was preparing this presentation, I told my wife I was going to sing his song, and she threatened to beat me if I did!
