  - [18:52 - 18:58] **The Role of Hard Work and Constraints**

    Thinking about these technical problems, you have to realize it's a dual process. On one end, you have to work incredibly hard. Then, on the other end, you have to go for a walk, go to sleep, relax, and let the ideas incubate. But then you have to come back again and work hard to verify them. It's almost like you have to bounce between two extremes. Most people romanticize creativity; they think that if you just wait, the "Muse" will visit you, or God will just drop the perfect idea into your lap. But it turns out that is not the case at all. You have to exist in the real world, build your skills, and be at your desk ready to receive the inspiration if it comes.

    One of my favorite quotes is from the composer Igor Stravinsky. Someone asked him about his writing routine, and he said, "I go to the piano at 10 AM, and I work until 1 PM, and then I go back from 2 PM to 8 PM, and that's my routine." And the interviewer asked, "What if the Muse doesn't visit?" Stravinsky replied, "At least I kept my appointment." And that is exactly the point! He practiced and played for hours every single day. The preparation phase is mandatory. Without those long hours of deep diving into the problem, you can guarantee you will have no ideas.

    Furthermore, real-world constraints are the actual muses of creativity. Deadlines, budget limits, the need for promotion—these constraints limit the infinite ocean of ideas and force you to focus. The musician Jack White famously said, "Opportunity kills creativity." If you have too many opportunities and zero limits, you can't be creative. Creativity only happens when you are faced with constraints. Think about the Louvre pyramid in Paris designed by I.M. Pei. They needed a new entrance for the museum, but there was absolutely no space above ground. The constraint was space. So the architect said, "I'm going to take this underground and build a glass pyramid." That constraint forced a completely novel and iconic solution. Or think about smartphone design. Why did companies like Samsung work so hard to develop the Galaxy Flip? The constraint was that consumers wanted a massive screen, but it still had to fit inside a standard pocket. If that constraint didn't exist, engineers would have never endured the agonizing process of inventing a foldable screen.

    思考这些技术问题时，你必须意识到这是一个双重过程。在一端，你必须非常努力地工作。然后，在另一端，你必须去散步、去睡觉、放松，让想法孵化。但随后你必须再次回来，努力验证它们。这几乎就像你必须在两个极端之间来回切换。大多数人将创造力浪漫化了；他们认为如果你只是等待，“缪斯女神”就会来拜访你，或者上帝会直接把完美的想法塞到你怀里。但事实证明根本不是这样。你必须存在于现实世界中，培养你的技能，并坐在书桌前准备好迎接可能到来的灵感。

    我最喜欢的名言之一来自作曲家伊戈尔·斯特拉文斯基。有人问他的写作习惯，他说：“我早上10点走到钢琴前，一直工作到下午1点，然后我从下午2点再回去工作到晚上8点，这就是我的日常。”采访者问：“如果缪斯女神没有来拜访怎么办？”斯特拉文斯基回答说：“至少我赴约了。”这正是关键所在！他每天都练习和演奏好几个小时。准备阶段是强制性的。如果没有那些长时间深入研究问题的时间，你可以保证你不会有任何想法。

    此外，现实世界的限制才是创造力真正的缪斯女神。最后期限、预算限制、晋升的需求——这些限制缩小了无限的想法海洋，并迫使你集中注意力。音乐家杰克·怀特有句名言：“机会扼杀创造力。”如果你有太多的机会且没有任何限制，你就无法发挥创造力。创造力只有在面对限制时才会发生。想想贝聿铭设计的巴黎卢浮宫金字塔。他们需要为博物馆建一个新入口，但地面上绝对没有空间了。限制就是空间。所以建筑师说：“我要把它建在地下，并建造一个玻璃金字塔。”这种限制迫使产生了一个完全新颖且标志性的解决方案。或者想想智能手机的设计。为什么像三星这样的公司要如此努力地开发Galaxy Flip折叠屏手机？限制在于消费者想要一个巨大的屏幕，但它仍然必须能装进标准的口袋里。如果不存在这种限制，工程师们就永远不会忍受发明折叠屏幕这一痛苦的过程。

  - [18:58 - 19:05] **Embracing Constraints in the Workplace**

    How many of you play musical instruments? If you do, you know that all music involves limits. You restrict the scales, the tuning, or the rhythm to create a specific style. You use constraints to solve the problem of expression. If you can come up with a new way to address all these constraints, then you are a creative engine. The main takeaway for me is a change in mindset. From now on, every time my boss asks me to do something with fewer resources, or in a faster time, I will not complain. Do you know why? Because I now see that constraint as a direct challenge to my brain to be truly creative.

    <image: Graphic showing constraints (time, money, space) acting as a funnel that produces creative ideas>

    你们当中有多少人演奏乐器？如果你演奏的话，你就会知道所有的音乐都包含限制。你限制音阶、定弦或节奏来创造一种特定的风格。你利用限制来解决表达的问题。如果你能想出一种新方法来应对所有这些限制，那么你就是一个创造力引擎。对我来说，主要的收获是心态的转变。从现在开始，每次我的老板要求我用更少的资源或在更短的时间内完成某件事时，我不会抱怨。你知道为什么吗？因为我现在把这种限制视为对我的大脑发挥真正创造力的直接挑战。

  - [19:05 - 19:09] **Where Do the Best Ideas Come From?**

    A student just shouted out "the toilet!" Yes, that's actually a very valid answer. Where and when do you get your best ideas? When you wake up from sleep? Listening to a broadcast? Talking with other people? Or taking a walk? Walking is a massive one. It turns out all of these mundane activities are highly conducive to creative breakthroughs. Let me give you a famous historical example. This is James Watt, the man who significantly improved the design of the steam engine. He was struggling with the issue of the cylinder constantly needing to be cooled and reheated. The story goes that on a fine Sabbath afternoon, he went for a stroll. He was walking past the old washing-house, mildly thinking about the engine, when suddenly the idea of the separate condenser came to him. The idea that steam would rush into an exhausted vessel without cooling the main cylinder just clicked into place. So, a simple walk definitely helps you get creative ideas.

    Another major one is dreaming. You are absolutely right that many famous scientists and musicians get their best ideas while asleep. Consider Dmitri Mendeleev, the Russian chemist who created the Periodic Table. The story is that he worked exhaustively for three days, trying to classify the elements, but he couldn't make it fit. He was completely drained, so he finally fell asleep. In a dream, he saw a table where all the elements fell into place perfectly. He woke up and immediately wrote it down. Until that moment of sleep, he was struggling. Furthermore, in modern business surveys, most busy executives and CEOs report that their most breakthrough ideas come to them when they are exercising, commuting, or taking a shower. So, is it purely a matter of blind luck? Are we just hoping to get lucky at that exact moment?

    刚才有位同学大喊“厕所！”是的，这实际上是一个非常有效的答案。你通常在何时何地获得最好的想法？从睡梦中醒来时？听广播时？与他人交谈时？还是散步时？散步是一个很重要的途径。事实证明，所有这些平凡的活动都非常有助于创造性突破。让我给你们举一个著名的历史例子。这是詹姆斯·瓦特，他大幅改进了蒸汽机的设计。他当时正在与气缸需要不断冷却和重新加热的问题作斗争。据说在一个天气晴朗的安息日下午，他去散步。他走过旧洗衣房，脑子里漫不经心地想着蒸汽机，突然分离式冷凝器的想法浮现在他的脑海中。蒸汽会涌入一个排空容器而无需冷却主气缸的想法就这样自然而然地形成了。所以，简单的散步绝对能帮助你获得创意的想法。

    另一个重要的途径是做梦。你们说得很对，许多著名的科学家和音乐家在睡觉时获得了他们最好的想法。想想俄罗斯化学家德米特里·门捷列夫，他创造了元素周期表。据说他为了对元素进行分类，连续三天极其疲惫地工作，但他怎么也排不好。他已经筋疲力尽，所以最终睡着了。在梦里，他看到了一张表格，所有的元素都完美地落在了各自的位置上。他醒来后立刻把它写了下来。在入睡前的那一刻，他一直都在挣扎。此外，在现代商业调查中，大多数忙碌的高管和首席执行官报告说，他们最具突破性的想法都是在锻炼、通勤或洗澡时产生的。那么，这纯粹是盲目靠运气吗？我们只是希望在那一确切时刻能走运吗？

  - [19:09 - 19:13] **The Incubation Phase and Subconscious Associations**

    If you look at the stage of "Incubation," it answers this question. What incubation means is that because you have a difficult problem, you have tried to solve it consciously and worked hard at it. But when your mind is fully conscious, your strict logic rejects certain ideas; the solutions just don't seem to work. Incubation requires you to step back and be relaxed, but not *fully* relaxed. What does that mean? For example, if you go to a new city and take their subway system, or if you drive a completely new route in a traffic jam, you are constantly scanning the environment. You are worried about missing your stop or getting into an accident. Your brain is highly engaged and not relaxed. The optimal profile for incubation is engaging in a task that is so familiar—like showering or walking your usual route—that your conscious brain goes on autopilot.

    When you are in this half-conscious, mildly active state, your brain starts throwing out crazy, random ideas and begins combining them. When you are fully awake and conscious, your strict logical filter will tell you, "These two ideas cannot possibly be connected," and you will immediately dismiss them. You judge ideas too early. But when you are half-conscious, you do not pre-judge. You don't dismiss the bizarre combination of two completely unrelated concepts. You allow your mind to entertain them. Out of the thousands of crazy combinations your subconscious plays with, one turns out to be novel and effective. And when that happens, you suddenly say, "Aha! That's it!" When you are fully awake, your mind simply will not get to a stage where you allow all these crazy ideas to intermingle. That's why people don't realize that sometimes the most productive thing you can do for a project is to sleep, go jogging, or take a shower.

    如果你看看“孵化”阶段，它就回答了这个问题。孵化的意思是，因为你遇到了一个难题，你已经尝试有意识地去解决它，并在上面付出了努力。但当你的大脑完全清醒时，你严密的逻辑会排斥某些想法；解决方案似乎就是行不通。孵化要求你退后一步并放松，但不能*完全*放松。这是什么意思？例如，如果你去一个新城市并乘坐他们的地铁系统，或者在堵车时开一条全新的路线，你会不断地扫描周围的环境。你担心错过站或者发生事故。你的大脑高度集中，并不放松。最适合孵化的状态是从事一项你非常熟悉的任务——比如洗澡或走你常走的路线——这样你清醒的大脑就会进入自动驾驶模式。

    当你处于这种半清醒、轻度活跃的状态时，你的大脑开始抛出疯狂的、随机的想法，并开始将它们组合起来。当你完全清醒和有意识时，你严格的逻辑过滤器会告诉你，“这两个想法不可能联系在一起”，然后你会立即将它们驳回。你过早地评判了这些想法。但当你处于半清醒状态时，你不会预先评判。你不会驳回两个完全不相关的概念之间离奇的组合。你允许你的大脑去思考它们。在你的潜意识摆弄的成千上万种疯狂组合中，有一种最终被证明是新颖且有效的。当那种情况发生时，你会突然说：“啊哈！就是这个！”当你完全清醒时，你的大脑根本不会达到允许所有这些疯狂想法交融的阶段。这就是为什么人们没有意识到，有时对一个项目你能做的最有成效的事情就是去睡觉、去慢跑，或者去洗个澡。

  - [19:13 - 19:15] **The Verification Phase: The Archimedes Eureka Moment**

    Of course, once the idea hits you, you move to the Verification phase. You have to prove that this crazy idea actually works. Think about the famous story of Archimedes. We all know he jumped out of the bath shouting "Eureka!" (I found it!). But remember the context: his King gave a goldsmith pure gold to make a crown. When the crown was finished, the King suspected the goldsmith had siphoned off some of the gold and mixed in cheaper silver. But the King refused to let the crown be dismantled or melted down to check. He handed Archimedes this seemingly impossible constraint: "Please prove this. Tell me whether it's pure gold or mixed with silver, without destroying the crown." Archimedes thought about it intensely for days. It was only later, when he stepped into a bath and saw the water spill over, that his subconscious connected water displacement with volume and density. That was his Eureka moment.

    If you only look at the Eureka moment, you might think creativity is pure luck. But if you look at the entire process—Preparation, Incubation, Illumination, and Verification—you know that creativity involves massive amounts of hard work. You have to wrestle with the problem initially, and you have to rigorously test the solution at the end. It's like the story of the blind men and the elephant; depending on which part of the creativity process you are observing, you might come to a completely different conclusion about what creativity actually is.

    当然，一旦你有了这个想法，你就会进入验证阶段。你必须证明这个疯狂的想法实际上是行得通的。想想阿基米德的著名故事。我们都知道他跳出浴缸大喊“尤里卡！”（我找到了！）。但记住这个背景：他的国王给了金匠纯金来做一顶王冠。王冠完成后，国王怀疑金匠克扣了一些黄金并掺入了较便宜的白银。但国王拒绝将王冠拆解或熔化来检查。他把这个看似不可能的限制交给了阿基米德：“请证明这一点。告诉我它是纯金的还是掺了白银的，而且不能破坏王冠。”阿基米德为此苦思冥想了好几天。直到后来，当他走进浴缸，看到水溢出来时，他的潜意识才将水体积的排开与物体的体积和密度联系起来。那就是他的尤里卡时刻。

    如果你只看尤里卡时刻，你可能会认为创造力纯粹是运气。但如果你看看整个过程——准备、孵化、启发和验证——你就会知道创造力包含了大量的艰苦工作。你必须在开始时与问题搏斗，并且必须在最后严格测试解决方案。这就像盲人摸象的故事；根据你观察的创造力过程的不同部分，你可能会得出关于创造力到底是什么的完全不同的结论。

  - [19:16 - 19:20] **The Neuroscience of REST and Dreaming**

    Let's look at what is physically happening in the brain. Professor Nancy Andreasen, a neuroscientist, conducted PET scans on people's brains during a state called REST (Random Episodic Silent Thought). She found that when people are asked to just relax and close their eyes, their brain is actually incredibly active. Specifically, the association cortices—the areas responsible for memory networks and making connections—consume immense amounts of energy. This is where your brain rapidly races through different memories and ideas. Think about when you dream: your brain might take a childhood friend and put them in the same room with your current university professor. Logically, these people shouldn't be in the same scenario, but in your dream, your brain freely associates them. Highly creative people tend to have richer activity in these association cortices; they literally see connections that average people miss.

    Recently, sleep scientists at the MIT Media Lab conducted a fascinating study on this. They focused on the N1 sleep stage, which is that brief window when you are just dozing off—the transition between wakefulness and sleep. They discovered that this specific window is a "sweet spot" for creativity. By using a device to track sleep onset and then waking the subjects up just as they drifted off, they could capture these bizarre, hypnagogic insights before they were lost to deep sleep. Historically, inventors like Thomas Edison and Salvador Dali did the exact same thing organically; they would hold heavy objects while dozing, so that as soon as their muscles relaxed in the N1 stage, the object would drop, wake them up, and allow them to write down their dream associations.

    让我们看看大脑中到底发生了什么物理变化。神经科学家南希·安德烈亚森教授对处于一种称为REST（随机情景默想）状态的人的大脑进行了PET扫描。她发现，当人们被要求仅仅放松并闭上眼睛时，他们的大脑实际上非常活跃。具体来说，联络皮层——负责记忆网络和建立联系的区域——消耗了巨大的能量。这是你的大脑快速闪过不同记忆和想法的地方。想想你做梦的时候：你的大脑可能会把你儿时的朋友和你现在的大学教授放在同一个房间里。从逻辑上讲，这些人不应该出现在同一个场景中，但在你的梦里，你的大脑自由地将他们联系在一起。具有高度创造力的人往往在这些联络皮层有更丰富的活动；他们确确实实能看到普通人错过的联系。

    最近，麻省理工学院媒体实验室的睡眠科学家对此进行了一项引人入胜的研究。他们主要关注N1睡眠阶段，也就是你刚刚打瞌睡时的那个短暂窗口期——清醒与睡眠之间的过渡期。他们发现这个特定的窗口期是创造力的“最佳击球点”。通过使用一种设备来追踪入睡点，然后受试者刚一入睡就将他们唤醒，他们就能在这些离奇的、半梦半醒间的灵感消失在深度睡眠之前将其捕捉到。从历史上看，像托马斯·爱迪生和萨尔瓦多·达利这样的发明家自然而然地做过完全相同的事情；他们会在打瞌睡时拿着重物，这样一旦他们在N1阶段肌肉放松，重物就会掉落，把他们吵醒，让他们能够写下他们梦中的联想。

  - [19:20 - 19:24] **The Waterproof Notepad Debate**

    So, knowing that showers are a great place for incubation, an entrepreneur once pitched a product idea: "If people get their best ideas during a shower, then I should invent a waterproof notepad and pen to hang in the shower! That way, when the idea strikes, they can quickly write it down." What do you all think? Do you think that's a good idea? A student is shaking their head. Why might this fail in practice?

    As we discussed, the magic of the shower is that it's a routine task where your mind can completely untether itself. But if you hang a notepad directly in front of you, every time you look at it, your brain goes, "Oh, right, I'm supposed to be generating ideas." You are constantly being reminded of the problem. If you are constantly scanning it, your brain cannot reach that semi-lucid, relaxed state required for the association cortices to do their work. It should be somewhere accessible, sure, but it should not be a focal point that demands conscious attention. By forcing the issue, you ruin the very environment that makes incubation possible.

    所以，既然知道洗澡是孵化想法的好地方，一位企业家曾经提出了一个产品创意：“如果人们在洗澡时能产生最好的想法，那么我应该发明一种防水记事本和笔挂在淋浴室里！这样，当灵感闪现时，他们就能迅速把它写下来。”大家觉得怎么样？你们觉得这是个好主意吗？有位同学在摇头。为什么这在实践中可能会失败？

    正如我们所讨论的，洗澡的魔力在于它是一项常规任务，你的大脑可以完全解开束缚。但如果你把一个记事本直接挂在你面前，每次你看着它，你的大脑就会说：“哦，对了，我应该要产生一些想法。”你不断地被提醒那个问题。如果你不断地扫描它，你的大脑就无法达到联络皮层发挥作用所需的半清醒、放松的状态。它应该是放在方便拿到的地方，没错，但它不应该成为一个需要集中意识注意力的焦点。由于强迫自己去想，你反而破坏了使孵化成为可能的那个环境本身。

  - [19:24 - 19:36] **Procrastination, Recombinant Search, and Capturing Ideas**

    I was preparing for a new exam the other day, and I came across a research policy paper by Schilling and Green. They analyzed high-impact papers in the social sciences. They found that breakthrough ideas typically come from "recombinant search"—which means taking an understanding of a problem in great depth and then connecting it with atypical, seemingly unrelated knowledge from entirely different domains. This perfectly aligns with everything we've talked about regarding memory networks and the association cortices during incubation.

    Interestingly, this also sheds light on procrastination. When you procrastinate on a difficult problem, what are you actually doing? You're just giving yourself more time to take showers, to go for long walks, and to sleep. By extending the timeline, you effectively give your subconscious more opportunities and a higher probability of making that remote, recombinant connection. However, the critical caveat is that when that Eureka moment eventually hits you—whether you're on a walk or stepping out of the shower—you must immediately capture it. Your conscious brain will quickly forget those fleeting subconscious associations. I actually make it a habit to quickly record voice memos or jot down notes on my phone the second an idea crystallizes. So, that's how the entire creative process strings together.

    前几天我在准备一个新的考试，我偶然看到了席林和格林的一篇研究政策论文。他们分析了社会科学领域的高影响力论文。他们发现，突破性的想法通常来自“重组搜索”——这意味着对问题有极深度的理解，然后将其与来自完全不同领域的非典型、看似不相关的知识联系起来。这与我们所讨论的关于孵化期间记忆网络和联络皮层的所有内容完美契合。

    有趣的是，这也让我们对拖延症有了新的理解。当你在一个难题上拖延时，你实际上在做什么？你只是给了自己更多的时间去洗澡、去长途散步、去睡觉。通过延长这个时间线，你实际上给了你的潜意识更多的机会和更高的概率来进行那种遥远的、重组的连接。然而，关键的警告是，当那个尤里卡时刻最终击中你时——无论你是在散步还是刚从淋浴间出来——你必须立即捕捉它。你清醒的大脑很快就会忘记那些转瞬即逝的潜意识联想。我实际上养成了这样一个习惯，一旦想法成型，我就会立刻在手机上录制语音备忘录或记下笔记。所以，这就是整个创造性过程是如何串联在一起的。
## Week 9 Topic 9 Becoming a creative problem solver 10.23
  - [18:16 - 18:24] **Introduction to Creative Problem-Solving**

    We often talk about the desire to always be able to come up with creative solutions. Think about your colleagues or friends; there is usually someone who has that reputation. People say, "Oh, he's very creative. He always comes out with creative solutions." Think about that kind of label and whether it is a label you want to have for yourself. But the real question is, how do we actually become that person? Prior to this class, we more or less established how we solve problems. To solve problems effectively, we need to manage the effort as a rigorous process. There are different stages in this process, and at each different stage, you need to maximize certain distinct outcomes. During the problem definition stage, your goal is to converge your thinking to come up with a single, highly refined, and good problem statement. Conversely, at the stage of coming up with solutions, you want to maximize the possibilities; your goal is to come up with as many diverse solutions as possible. Then, you transition back to a convergent stage where you evaluate and choose the one or two solutions that best meet your specific requirements and constraints.

    At different stages, you focus on very different outcomes. You cannot just single-mindedly focus on one mode of thinking throughout the whole process. We know this because all the formal methodologies we learn—whether it is Design Thinking, Six Sigma, DMAIC, or others—all follow these distinct, separated stages. However, simply knowing the process does not mean we can routinely and repeatedly come up with creative solutions. We do not want to be a "one-hit wonder" singer who has one lucky song and then disappears. We want to be like a legendary artist, like Michael Jackson, who repeatedly produces popular hit songs. So how do we become consistently creative? Have you ever actually measured your own creativity? Last week, we touched on this and I provided a link to a creativity test. It measures how good or how likely you are to brainstorm different ideas, but doing well on that test alone does not mean you are automatically a great problem solver.

    我们经常谈论希望总是能够想出创造性的解决方案。想想你的同事或朋友；通常总有那么一个人拥有这样的名声。人们会说，“哦，他非常有创造力。他总能想出创造性的解决方案。”想想那种标签，以及这是否是你希望自己拥有的标签。但真正的问题是，我们如何才能成为那样的人？在上一堂课之前，我们或多或少已经确定了我们解决问题的方式。为了有效地解决问题，我们需要将其作为一个严谨的过程来管理。这个过程有不同的阶段，在每一个不同的阶段，你需要最大化某些特定的结果。在问题定义阶段，你的目标是收敛你的思维，得出一个单一的、高度提炼的、优秀的问题陈述。相反，在提出解决方案的阶段，你想要最大化可能性；你的目标是想出尽可能多、尽可能多样的解决方案。然后，你再回到收敛阶段，在这个阶段你评估并选出最符合你特定要求和限制的一两个解决方案。

    在不同的阶段，你关注的是截然不同的结果。你不能在整个过程中只一心一意地专注于一种思维模式。我们知道这一点，因为我们学习的所有正式方法论——无论是设计思维、六西格玛、DMAIC还是其他方法——都遵循这些截然分开的阶段。然而，仅仅了解这个过程并不意味着我们就能常规地、重复地想出创造性的解决方案。我们不想成为那种只有一首幸运歌曲然后就销声匿迹的“昙花一现”的歌手。我们想成为像迈克尔·杰克逊那样传奇的艺术家，能够不断地创作出大受欢迎的流行歌曲。那么我们如何变得持续具有创造力呢？你有没有真正衡量过你自己的创造力？上周，我们提到了这一点，我提供了一个创造力测试的链接。它衡量的是你在头脑风暴不同想法时有多厉害或多有潜力，但仅仅在那个测试中表现出色并不意味着你自动就是一个解决问题的高手。

  - [18:24 - 18:28] **The Difficulty of Out-of-the-Box Thinking**

    As we established, solving problems involves four or five distinct stages. Thinking divergently—brainstorming out-of-the-box ideas—is only one stage out of those four. You might be incredibly good at brainstorming, but if you are not very good at the verification stage, or the problem definition stage that comes earlier, you still cannot solve problems creatively. You will always be constrained by your weakest ability in one of the four stages. That is exactly why creative problem solving is so difficult. In some sense, you need to be highly proficient in four completely different areas, and the skills required for all four areas are somewhat contradictory. They are almost autonomous and opposing forces to each other. Why is it so difficult for us to think out of the box? We often default to the most obvious, conventional associations rather than remote, unusual ones. As psychologists have proposed with the "cognitive miser theory," our brains are fundamentally lazy. Our brain did not evolve solely for high-level abstract thinking; it evolved for survival. The brain will minimize the energy needed by relying on fast, automatic, non-thinking responses if that is more beneficial for immediate survival. It only increases energy expenditure for conscious thought if it calculates that the act of deep thinking will bring a significantly higher benefit.

    正如我们所确定的，解决问题包括四个或五个不同的阶段。发散性思维——进行打破常规的头脑风暴——仅仅是这四个阶段中的一个。你可能在头脑风暴方面非常出色，但如果你在验证阶段，或者在更早的问题定义阶段表现不佳，你仍然无法创造性地解决问题。你将永远受到你在这四个阶段中最弱能力的制约。这正是创造性解决问题如此困难的原因。在某种意义上，你需要在四个完全不同的领域都高度精通，而这四个领域所需的能力在某种程度上是相互矛盾的。它们几乎是相互独立且对立的力量。为什么打破常规的思考对我们来说如此困难？我们通常会默认选择最明显、最常规的关联，而不是遥远、不寻常的关联。正如心理学家提出的“认知吝啬鬼理论”那样，我们的大脑从根本上来说是懒惰的。我们的大脑并非仅仅为了高水平的抽象思维而进化；它是为了生存而进化的。如果依赖快速、自动、无意识的反应对眼前的生存更有利，大脑就会通过这种方式将所需消耗的能量降到最低。只有当它计算出深度思考的行为将带来明显更高的收益时，它才会增加用于有意识思考的能量消耗。

  - [18:28 - 18:35] **Neurons, Energy, and Learning**

    When we look at how the brain is wired, we have to talk about learning and neurons. Especially when it comes to energy consumption, things like sweets and sugar give us immediate physical energy, which our brain craves because it relies on that energy to function and process complex thought. When you are presented with a stimulus—say, I give you a specific word or show you food—there is an immediate reaction. You are biologically wired to eat a lot when there is food in front of you, which is why we overeat, and you are hard-wired to crave sugar. In terms of cognition, the brain is primarily made up of billions of neurons. When you learn something or experience something new, neurons in your brain fire up. They get excited, trigger electrical signals, and stimulate other connected neurons. As these signals propagate through this massive, complex neural network, learning occurs. This growth and connection of the neuronal network is the physical manifestation of learning.

    (During this time, a student interjects with a question comparing the process of learning manual mental models before relying on Artificial Intelligence, drawing an analogy to how students are taught manual mathematics before they are allowed to use calculators.)

    当我们观察大脑是如何运作时，我们必须谈谈学习和神经元。特别是在能量消耗方面，像糖果和糖分这样的东西会给我们提供直接的物理能量，我们的大脑渴望这些能量，因为它依赖这种能量来运作和处理复杂的思维。当你面对某种刺激时——比如，我给你一个特定的词或者给你看食物——会产生立即的反应。在生物学上，当食物摆在你面前时，你天生就会吃很多，这就是我们暴饮暴食的原因，而且你天生就渴望糖分。在认知方面，大脑主要由数十亿个神经元组成。当你学习新东西或体验新事物时，你大脑中的神经元就会活跃起来。它们变得兴奋，触发电信号，并刺激其他相连的神经元。随着这些信号在这个庞大、复杂的神经网络中传播，学习就发生了。这种神经网络的生长和连接就是学习的物理表现。

    （在这段时间里，一名学生插话提出了一个问题，将依赖人工智能之前学习手动心智模型的过程，比作学生在被允许使用计算器之前先学习手工数学的过程。）

  - [18:35 - 18:37] **Student Q&A: The Role of AI versus Manual Calculation**

    That is a very good point from the student, and it highlights a major struggle we are currently facing in education. For mathematics, we generally agree on the pedagogical sequence: we know at what point students are allowed to use a calculator. But before that point, you absolutely need to learn manual calculation. You have to understand the foundational logic. AI is a bit more tricky. If you are already an expert in a field, your brain already has all those established neural connections; you already understand the domain deeply, so using AI to speed up your work does not harm your fundamental understanding. But human nature dictates that we are most likely to use AI in subjects that we are *not* familiar with. That is exactly the area where our own neural network is disconnected and lacks foundational knowledge.

    The danger of using Generative AI in an area you do not know is severe. Think about the calculator analogy: when you use a calculator, you know the final answer is mathematically sound because you know the underlying mathematics and scientific formulas. The calculator is simply automating the mechanical process for you. If you are old enough, like me, you remember when we did not have advanced calculators for everything. We used to have printed logarithmic or statistical tables. We had to manually look up the value in the table, plug it into our formula, and calculate it by hand. The calculator just automated that lookup and computation process. But the problem with Generative AI is that because it is so all-encompassing, so articulate, and so seemingly powerful, we might easily lose sight of where its outputs are factually true and where they are false. Because you might confidently know *part* of the subject, you trust the AI. But as you use AI more and more, you unconsciously drift into areas you actually know very little about. You think you know much more than you do, and you continue to blindly trust it. If Generative AI were static and one-dimensional like a calculator, it would be much easier for us to manage. But it is not.

    这位学生提出的观点非常好，它突出了我们在教育中目前面临的一个主要挣扎。对于数学，我们在教学顺序上普遍有共识：我们知道在什么阶段允许学生使用计算器。但在那个阶段之前，你绝对需要学习手工计算。你必须理解基础逻辑。人工智能则更为棘手。如果你已经是某个领域的专家，你的大脑已经拥有了所有那些既定的神经连接；你已经深入理解了该领域，因此使用人工智能来加速你的工作并不会损害你的基础理解。但人的本性决定了我们最有可能在自己*不*熟悉的学科中使用人工智能。而这恰好是我们自身的神经网络处于断开状态、缺乏基础知识的领域。

    在你完全不了解的领域使用生成式人工智能是非常危险的。想想计算器的比喻：当你使用计算器时，你知道最终答案在数学上是合理的，因为你了解底层的数学和科学公式。计算器只是为你自动化了机械过程。如果你像我一样年纪够大，你会记得我们以前并没有可以计算一切的高级计算器。我们过去使用的是印刷的对数或统计表。我们必须在表中手动查找数值，将其代入我们的公式中，然后手工计算。计算器只是将查找和计算过程自动化了。但是，生成式人工智能的问题在于，由于它包罗万象、表达流利，而且看起来如此强大，我们很容易忽略它的输出在哪些地方是符合事实的，哪些地方是错误的。因为你可能自信地只了解该学科的*一部分*，你就会信任人工智能。但是，随着你越来越多地使用人工智能，你会在不知不觉中漂移到你实际上知之甚少的领域。你以为自己知道的很多，然后你就继续盲目地信任它。如果生成式人工智能像计算器一样是静态和一维的，我们就很容易管理了。但它并不是。

  - [18:37 - 18:40] **Brain Plasticity and the Value of Struggle**

    Returning to how our brains think out of the box, we have to look at how environments shape our neural pathways. Let us look at the nerves of animals in two completely different scenarios. Research shows the brain tissue of an animal living in a bare, empty cage with no other animals. Compare that to the brain of an animal living in a highly stimulating environment, surrounded by other animals, toys, and constant challenges. The neural density in the stimulated brain is vastly superior. The big takeaway from this whole area of neuroplasticity research is that you absolutely can change your intelligence. Your brain is literally like a muscle: the more you use it, the stronger and more interconnected it gets. And the best way to grow it is not to just do things that are easy for you. Doing easy things might maintain it a little bit, but what really forces your brain to grow is when you struggle with difficult concepts.

    Research shows that your brain grows the most not when you easily get a question right, but when you get a question wrong. For me, this is incredibly exciting and comforting. It lets me know that when I am going through a difficult problem and facing times of frustration or adversity, I can feel good about the fact that those exact moments of struggle are when I am actually growing intellectually. This is not just me saying nice, motivational words. The science tells us that when you get something wrong, when you deeply challenge your brain, and when you review why you got it wrong to process that feedback, that is the exact moment your brain grows the most. If you keep subjecting yourself to that productive struggle, you are well on your way to having a stronger, more capable, and smarter brain. Basically, facing difficulty and making mistakes stimulates your neurons to reach out and form closer connections, much more so than if you had just gotten the answer right easily. The brain is forced to adapt.

    回到我们的大脑如何打破常规思考的话题，我们必须看看环境是如何塑造我们的神经通路的。让我们来看看动物在两种完全不同场景下的神经。研究展示了一只生活在空荡荡的笼子里、没有其他动物陪伴的动物的脑组织。将其与生活在高度刺激环境中、周围有其他动物、玩具和持续挑战的动物的大脑进行比较。受刺激的大脑中的神经密度要优越得多。从整个神经可塑性研究领域得出的最大结论是，你绝对可以改变你的智力。你的大脑确实就像一块肌肉：你越使用它，它就变得越强壮、连接越紧密。而让它成长的最好方法，不是仅仅做对你来说容易的事情。做容易的事情可能只会起一点维持作用，但真正迫使你的大脑成长的，是当你在艰难的概念中挣扎的时候。

    研究表明，大脑成长最快的时刻，并不是你轻松答对问题的时候，而是你答错问题的时候。对我来说，这非常令人兴奋和欣慰。这让我知道，当我在解决一个难题，面临挫折或逆境时，我可以对这个事实感到欣慰：正是那些挣扎的时刻，才是我真正在智力上成长的时刻。这不仅仅是我在说好听的、鼓舞人心的话。科学告诉我们，当你弄错了某件事，当你深度挑战你的大脑，当你复习为什么你弄错了以处理那些反馈时，正是你的大脑成长最快的时刻。如果你继续让自己经历这种富有成效的挣扎，你就走在了一条拥有更强壮、更有能力、更聪明的大脑的康庄大道上。基本上，面对困难和犯错会刺激你的神经元伸展并形成更紧密的连接，这比你轻而易举就得到正确答案要有效得多。大脑是被迫去适应的。

  - [18:40 - 18:51] **Video Presentation: Brain Tissue Under a Microscope**

    (The professor plays a video demonstrating brain tissue under a microscope, visually illustrating the concepts of neuroplasticity, cell body connections, and the physical manifestation of learning discussed previously. The class observes the video in silence.)

    （教授播放了一段展示显微镜下脑组织的视频，直观地说明了神经可塑性、细胞体连接以及前面讨论的学习的物理表现等概念。全班同学安静地观看视频。）

  - [18:51 - 18:55] **Divergent Thinking Techniques: SCAMPER and Remote Association**

    There is a time and place for everything in the brain, but when it is time to generate creative ideas, we need thinking tactics that generate variability. One powerful divergent thinking technique is SCAMPER, which stands for Substitute, Combine, Adapt, Modify, Put to other uses, Eliminate, and Reverse. For example, for "Substitute," can you substitute one material for another? Instead of using plastic, can Apple use aluminum for the MacBook Air to make it lighter or more conductive? For "Combine," we look at everyday items. Today, we combine car keys with our handphones, or shampoos with conditioners. For "Adapt," can you adapt a successful concept from a totally different industry? Last week, I mentioned the famous story of Henry Ford. He went to a slaughterhouse in Chicago and observed the meat-packing process where the animal carcasses moved along a line while the workers stood still. He adapted that exact concept to build the modern assembly line for his car factory.

    Another brilliant example of thinking outside the box involves a man who invented a candy called the Spin Pop. It was a spinning lollipop where you press a button and a small battery mechanism spun the candy. The inventor made a lot of money from this novelty toy. But then he engaged in remote association; he looked at the core technology and thought about "Putting it to other uses." He realized he had created an incredibly cheap, energy-efficient spinning mechanism. He took this idea to Procter & Gamble (P&G) and suggested they use it to make a low-cost electric toothbrush. P&G turned him down, telling him his idea was stupid because they already sold high-end, heavy-duty rechargeable toothbrushes. So, this inventor created his own company and used his Spin Pop mechanism to manufacture a simple electric toothbrush that ran on just standard AA batteries for months. He made a massive amount of money selling this low-cost, energy-efficient "SpinBrush." Eventually, P&G realized their mistake, came back, and bought his company for an absolute fortune. Unfortunately for P&G, by the time they bought it, competitors had already caught up, so they lost big time on that acquisition. But for the entrepreneur, it was a massive success story born entirely out of taking an existing mechanism and adapting it to a totally different product category.

    大脑在不同的时间有不同的用武之地，但当到了产生创意想法的时候，我们需要能够产生多样性的思维策略。一个强大的发散性思维技巧是SCAMPER，它代表替代（Substitute）、合并（Combine）、改造（Adapt）、修改（Modify）、用于其他用途（Put to other uses）、消除（Eliminate）和颠倒（Reverse）。例如，“替代”，你能用一种材料替代另一种材料吗？苹果公司能不能不使用塑料，而使用铝来制造MacBook Air，使其更轻或导电性更好？对于“合并”，我们来看看日常用品。今天，我们将车钥匙与手机结合，或者将洗发水与护发素结合。对于“改造”，你能从一个完全不同的行业中改造一个成功的概念吗？上周，我提到了亨利·福特的著名故事。他去了芝加哥的一家屠宰场，观察了肉类包装过程，动物尸体沿着生产线移动，而工人则站立不动。他完全照搬了那个概念，为他的汽车工厂建立了一条现代化的装配线。

    另一个打破常规思考的绝佳例子涉及一个发明了一种叫做“旋转棒棒糖”（Spin Pop）的人。这是一种旋转的棒棒糖，你按下一个按钮，一个小型电池装置就会让糖果旋转。这位发明家从这个新奇的玩具中赚了很多钱。但随后他进行了远距离联想；他着眼于核心技术，并考虑将其“用于其他用途”。他意识到自己创造了一个极其便宜、节能的旋转装置。他把这个想法带给了宝洁公司（P&G），并建议他们利用这个装置生产一种低成本的电动牙刷。宝洁公司拒绝了他，告诉他他的想法很愚蠢，因为他们已经在销售高端、耐用的充电式牙刷了。于是，这位发明家创立了自己的公司，利用他的旋转棒棒糖装置制造了一种简单的电动牙刷，这种牙刷仅靠标准的AA电池就能运行数月。通过销售这种低成本、节能的“SpinBrush”，他赚取了巨额财富。最终，宝洁公司意识到了他们的错误，回过头来花天价收购了他的公司。对宝洁来说不幸的是，在他们收购时，竞争对手已经赶上来了，所以他们在那次收购中损失惨重。但对这位企业家来说，这是一个巨大的成功故事，完全源于将现有的机制改造并应用于完全不同的产品类别。

  - [18:55 - 18:58] **Case Study: The Maritime Innovation Ecosystem**

    I was lucky enough to experience the power of remote association firsthand. I was the principal investigator for a research project tasked with advising the government on how to build a Silicon Valley-style innovation ecosystem for the maritime industry in Singapore. I read extensively about innovation ecosystems. The literature, like the Mode 3 innovation ecosystem model, suggested there are three core parties needed: academic firms/universities, commercial firms, and university-related institutions like VC firms or law firms. Literature on the actual Silicon Valley showed highly complex networks connecting all these entities. I took these findings, applied the MECE (Mutually Exclusive, Collectively Exhaustive) principle to clean up the existing frameworks, and organized the players into three clear layers: Process Organization (start-ups and established companies), Business Development (solution providers and demand drivers), and Commercialization/Application.

    However, I was then challenged: how do we actually *build* this ecosystem from scratch? Do we gather 50 academic researchers (the yellow dots), then bring in 50 start-ups (the blue dots) to connect them, and finally add 50 large firms (the red dots)? That layered approach is too slow and resource-heavy. So, with no clear direction, I opened up my mental network and found inspiration from reading English football commentaries. When building a strong football team like Arsenal, a manager does not just buy a bunch of forwards with no defenders. The concept is to build a "spine" first. You get a strong goalkeeper, one core defender, one solid midfielder, and one great forward. Once that central spine is strong, you use your remaining budget to build out the rest of the squad around it. I made the cognitive leap and applied that football "spine" concept to my three-layered innovation ecosystem. Instead of building layer by layer, I proposed we identify one key player in each level—one core university center of excellence, link it to one or two core start-ups, and connect them to one established industry giant. We build the "spine" of the ecosystem first, ensuring vertical connection, and then replicate it. Your ability to think abstractly allows you to take a concept from football and leap over to maritime innovation, just like Henry Ford leaping from a slaughterhouse to car manufacturing.

    我非常幸运地亲身体验了远距离联想的力量。我曾是一个研究项目的首席研究员，任务是就如何为新加坡海事行业建立一个硅谷式的创新生态系统向政府提供建议。我广泛阅读了关于创新生态系统的文献。像模式3创新生态系统模型这样的文献表明，需要三个核心方：学术公司/大学、商业公司，以及像风险投资公司或律师事务所这样与大学相关的机构。关于真正的硅谷的文献展示了连接所有这些实体的高度复杂的网络。我采纳了这些发现，应用MECE（相互独立，完全穷尽）原则来清理现有的框架，并将参与者组织成三个清晰的层级：流程组织（初创企业和老牌公司）、业务开发（解决方案提供商和需求驱动者）以及商业化/应用。

    然而，我随后受到了挑战：我们实际上如何从零开始*建立*这个生态系统？我们是先召集50名学术研究人员（黄点），然后引入50家初创企业（蓝点）将它们连接起来，最后再加入50家大型公司（红点）吗？这种分层的方法太慢且耗费资源。所以，在没有明确方向的情况下，我打开了我的思维网络，从阅读英国足球评论中找到了灵感。在建立像阿森纳这样强大的足球队时，经理不会只买一堆前锋而没有后卫。这个理念是首先建立一条“中轴线”。你要找到一个强大的守门员，一个核心后卫，一个稳健的中场和一个伟大的前锋。一旦这条中央中轴线稳固了，你再利用剩余的预算围绕它建立阵容的其余部分。我实现了认知的飞跃，将这种足球“中轴线”的概念应用到我的三层创新生态系统中。我建议我们不要逐层建立，而是在每一层中确定一个关键参与者——一个核心的大学卓越中心，将其与一两家核心初创企业联系起来，并将它们与一家成熟的行业巨头连接起来。我们首先建立生态系统的“中轴线”，确保垂直连接，然后再复制它。你进行抽象思考的能力，使你能够将足球中的概念跨越到海事创新中，就像亨利·福特从屠宰场跨越到汽车制造一样。

  - [18:58 - 19:02] **Traits of Creative People and the Problem-Solving Process**

    So the vital question now is: how can we repeatedly make these remote connections? If you do an internet search for "how to be a creative problem solver" or "characteristics of creative people," you will get massive lists of traits. They will say you have to be open-minded, a risk-taker, original, flexible, playful, sensitive, curious, independent, and so on. If you look at all these required traits, you might feel completely overwhelmed. You might look at the list and think, "Oh my God, I am already an adult. I am who I am. There is no time left in my life to acquire all these traits." Changing your core personality could take years, and frankly, you may not want to change. If you naturally prefer structure and do not like making jokes, why should you force yourself to be playfully humorous just to be "creative"? I cannot force you to change your personality traits. When I used to teach media classes, they would give me these same long lists of creative traits. But research shows a critical nuance: these traits are indeed important, but they are only important for *specific stages* of the creative problem-solving process.

    For example, traits like being hardworking, analytical, and passionate are excellent and necessary for the problem definition stage and the final implementation stage. However, during the solution generation stage—the "incubation" stage—being relentlessly hardworking might actually hinder you. During incubation, you need to step away. You should take a shower, go for a long walk, or daydream so your brain can wander. That is where traits like being open-minded, curious, and having a tolerance for ambiguity come into play. They allow your brain to synthesize crazy ideas without immediate judgment. Therefore, different suggestions apply to different stages. The advice that helps you in one phase of problem-solving might actively harm you in another phase. This contradictory nature is exactly why creative problem-solving is so tough. You need to be deeply analytical, yet wonderfully lazy; you need to be wildly crazy, but practically reasonable. This realization inspired the puzzle game we played last week. People often quote Thomas Edison saying creativity is "99% perspiration and 1% inspiration," emphasizing hard work. But others say their best ideas come effortlessly during a long walk. They are simply describing different phases of the exact same process.

    那么现在至关重要的问题是：我们如何能够不断地进行这些远距离联想？如果你在互联网上搜索“如何成为一个创造性的问题解决者”或“有创造力的人的特征”，你会得到大量的特征列表。他们会说你必须思想开阔、敢于冒险、有独创性、灵活、爱玩、敏感、好奇、独立等等。如果你看着所有这些被要求的特质，你可能会感到完全不知所措。你可能会看着列表想：“哦，天哪，我已经是个成年人了。我就是我。我生命中没有时间去获得所有这些特质了。”改变你的核心性格可能需要几年的时间，而且坦率地说，你可能并不想改变。如果你天生喜欢条理，不喜欢开玩笑，为什么你非要强迫自己变得幽默风趣仅仅为了显得“有创造力”呢？我不能强迫你改变你的性格特质。当我过去教媒体课时，他们也会给我同样长长的一串关于创造力特质的列表。但研究显示了一个关键的细微差别：这些特征确实很重要，但它们仅仅对创造性解决问题过程的*特定阶段*重要。

    例如，像勤奋、善于分析和充满热情这样的特质，对于问题定义阶段和最终的执行阶段是非常棒和必要的。然而，在解决方案生成阶段——即“孵化”阶段——不懈地努力实际上可能会阻碍你。在孵化期间，你需要抽身而出。你应该去洗个澡，去散个长步，或者做做白日梦，让你的大脑可以自由漫游。这就是像思想开阔、好奇以及对模糊性的容忍度这些特质发挥作用的地方。它们允许你的大脑在没有立即评判的情况下综合那些疯狂的想法。因此，不同的建议适用于不同的阶段。在一个解决问题阶段对你有帮助的建议，可能会在另一个阶段对你造成积极的伤害。这种矛盾的本质正是为什么创造性解决问题如此艰难的原因。你需要有深度的分析能力，但又需要奇妙的慵懒；你需要极其疯狂，但在实践中又要讲道理。这种认识启发了我们上周玩的游戏。人们经常引用托马斯·爱迪生的话，说创造力是“99%的汗水加上1%的灵感”，强调努力工作。但也有人说，他们最好的主意是在长途散步时毫不费力地产生的。他们只是在描述同一个过程的不同阶段。

  - [19:02 - 19:09] **Building Creative Habits and the "Lego Bricks" of the Mind**

    While I cannot fundamentally alter your character, we do know exactly what the brain needs for creative ideas to spark. We need diverse memories stored in our brain, we need to allow time for incubation, and we need to provide opportunities for those distant concepts to connect. Creativity is not something you can cram for. If you work intensely on being creative for two days and then drop it, you will fail. Being a creative problem solver is exactly like maintaining physical fitness. You cannot just run one marathon and stay healthy forever; you must consistently exercise and maintain a habit of eating healthily. To become a routine creative problem-solver, you must consciously build certain habits. As outlined by experts like James Clear in "Atomic Habits" and Charles Duhigg in "The Power of Habit," habits rely on a "Cue-Routine-Reward" loop. If you want to build a habit to support creativity—like reading diverse materials or protecting your incubation time—you have to design the environment to minimize friction.

    For example, people who want to form the habit of flossing their teeth are advised to pre-cut the floss the night before and commit to flossing just one tooth. By minimizing the friction, the behavior is more likely to occur and eventually becomes automatic. If you want to run in the morning, place your running shoes out the night before. If you want a habit of reading, establish a specific cue. I have a corner in my house where, every Saturday morning, I make myself a good cup of coffee, sit in that specific chair, and always have a book waiting for me. Eventually, that sequence becomes completely automatic. Another habit I have is related to travel. Every time I go to the airport and pass passport control, my cue is the environment. My routine is to go straight to the airport bookshop, buy a book, and read it on the plane. I have done this so consistently that on a six-hour flight, I do not even turn on the entertainment screen. The reward is new knowledge and feeling like I used the flight productively.

    Why do we need these habits? Countless studies, including those published in the Journal of Personality and Social Psychology, prove that living abroad, traveling, and exposing yourself to diverse stimuli have a direct, positive effect on creativity. As I study, travel, and work in different sectors, my brain accumulates thousands of different ideas. I want you to think of these ideas as different types of Lego bricks. Some are metal, some are plastic, some are circles, some are triangles. You accumulate these randomly over years. Then, one day, you are hit with a massive, highly difficult challenge—like designing the maritime innovation ecosystem. You engage in your established incubation habit, like taking a shower or meditating, and suddenly your relaxed brain starts throwing these random Lego bricks together. Out of thousands of crazy combinations, one combination perfectly fits. That is the "Eureka!" moment. But here is the stark reality: if you never developed the habit of reading widely or traveling, you have no Lego bricks stored in your brain. You have absolutely nothing for your subconscious to connect. You cannot synthesize an idea out of an empty mental warehouse. Remember, your brain is a cognitive miser. It will take the path of least resistance. You must deliberately cultivate the habits of diverse input and quiet incubation to override that natural laziness. Long-term habits and incubation routines matter far more than short-term bursts of effort.

    虽然我无法从根本上改变你的性格，但我们确实清楚地知道大脑需要什么来激发创意的火花。我们需要将各种各样的记忆储存在我们的大脑中，我们需要留出孵化的时间，我们还需要为那些遥远概念的连接提供机会。创造力不是你可以死记硬背的东西。如果你为了变得有创造力而突击努力两天，然后就放弃了，你肯定会失败。成为一个创造性的问题解决者就像保持身体健康一样。你不可能只跑一次马拉松就永远保持健康；你必须持续锻炼并保持健康饮食的习惯。要成为一个常规的创造性问题解决者，你必须有意识地培养某些习惯。正如詹姆斯·克莱尔（James Clear）在《原子习惯》和查尔斯·都希格（Charles Duhigg）在《习惯的力量》等专家所概述的那样，习惯依赖于一个“提示-惯常行为-奖励”的循环。如果你想建立一个支持创造力的习惯——比如阅读多样化的材料或保护你的孵化时间——你必须设计环境以将阻力降至最低。

    例如，想要养成用牙线清洁牙齿习惯的人，被建议在前一天晚上预先剪好牙线，并承诺只清洁一颗牙齿。通过将阻力降至最低，这种行为更有可能发生，并最终变得自动。如果你想在早上跑步，前一天晚上就把跑鞋拿出来。如果你想养成阅读的习惯，建立一个特定的提示。我在家里有一个角落，每个星期六早上，我都会给自己泡一杯好咖啡，坐在那把特定的椅子上，而且总会有一本书在等着我。最终，这个过程变得完全自动化了。我的另一个习惯与旅行有关。每次我去机场通过护照检查站时，我的提示就是那个环境。我的惯常行为是直接去机场书店买一本书，然后在飞机上读。我一直如此坚持，以至于在六个小时的飞行中，我甚至都不会打开娱乐屏幕。奖励是获得新知识，并感觉自己有效地利用了飞行时间。

    我们为什么需要这些习惯？无数的研究，包括发表在《个性与社会心理学杂志》上的研究，证明在国外生活、旅行和让自己接触各种刺激，对创造力有直接、积极的影响。当我在不同的领域学习、旅行和工作时，我的大脑积累了成千上万个不同的想法。我希望你们把这些想法看作是不同类型的乐高积木。有些是金属的，有些是塑料的，有些是圆形的，有些是三角形的。你会在几年内随机积累这些东西。然后，有一天，你遇到了一个巨大的、高度困难的挑战——比如设计海事创新生态系统。你进行你既定的孵化习惯，比如洗个澡或冥想，突然间，你放松的大脑开始将这些随机的乐高积木拼凑在一起。在数以千计的疯狂组合中，有一种组合完美契合。那就是“尤里卡！”（我发现了！）的时刻。但是残酷的现实是：如果你从未养成广泛阅读或旅行的习惯，你的大脑中就没有储存乐高积木。你的潜意识根本没有任何可以连接的东西。你无法从一个空荡荡的精神仓库中综合出一个想法。记住，你的大脑是一个认知吝啬鬼。它会选择阻力最小的路径。你必须刻意培养多样化输入和安静孵化的习惯，以克服那种天生的懒惰。长期习惯和孵化惯例比短期的突击努力重要得多。

  - [19:09 - 19:10] **Q&A Transition**

    Are there any questions on how you can become a creative problem solver? I have explained the theory and given you the recipe. The hard part now is whether you possess the discipline to actually develop those habits over time. No questions? Okay, then here comes the final, very difficult part of our discussion.

    关于如何成为一个创造性的问题解决者，还有什么问题吗？我已经向你们解释了理论并给出了秘诀。现在的困难在于你是否有自律性在随着时间的推移真正培养这些习惯。没有问题？好的，接下来是我们讨论中最后、也是非常困难的部分。

  - [19:10 - 19:18] **Generative AI in the Problem-Solving Process**

    Now we move to the final core question of this course: How can we solve problems creatively using Generative AI (GenAI), and how will it fundamentally change the way we approach problem-solving? This space is still rapidly evolving. My research team and I have thought deeply about this for a year, and some of the ideas we are developing are actually quite crazy. To look at this from a research perspective, if we treat problem-solving as a four-stage process, we must ask: How will GenAI change problem definition? How will it change solution generation? How will it change selection and implementation? We also have to decide how we view the AI: Do we treat the AI as a fellow student and teammate with limited but broad knowledge, or do we blindly trust it as an oracle that knows all the answers?

    Fundamentally, GenAI models like ChatGPT operate on next-token prediction. They generate text based on what combinations of words have appeared most frequently together in their training data. It is highly analogous to how neural connections work in our own brains—if concepts were connected heavily in the past text data, the AI predicts they should be connected in the output. Knowing this, we must evaluate its utility across the pre-AI problem-solving stages. In the pre-AI world, during problem definition, we relied heavily on manual tools like building MECE issue trees to logically break down the scope. During solution generation, we relied on human incubation and brainstorming.

    When I first explored GenAI's capabilities, I panicked. I honestly thought that AI's ability to instantly generate an issue tree was going to make a large part of my course curriculum completely irrelevant. To some extent, that is true, but there is a profound twist. The twist involves the concept of "hallucination." In the AI world, hallucination—where the model confidently makes up facts—is generally considered a terrible flaw. But think back to our own incubation process. When we dream or brainstorm, our brains are literally hallucinating. We connect bizarre, completely unreasonable things together to find a novel solution. I realized that for the solution generation stage, we might actually want to *force* the AI to hallucinate. If we deliberately prompt it to make wild, unconstrained associations, knowing full well that 99% of its outputs will be absolute garbage, that 1% might just be the extraordinary, creative remote association we need.

    Furthermore, look at the discipline of "Prompt Engineering." You see all these elaborate frameworks on LinkedIn claiming to revolutionize how you talk to AI—acronyms telling you to specify the role, define the context, establish the goal, and list the constraints. If you look closely at these fancy frameworks, they are absolutely identical to traditional, rigorous Problem Definition. When you are hired as a consultant, you establish the context, define the stakeholder's role, specify the target outcome, and clarify the boundaries. Good prompt engineering is simply good problem definition. There is fundamentally no difference.

    现在我们转向本课程的最后一个核心问题：我们如何使用生成式人工智能（GenAI）创造性地解决问题，以及它将如何从根本上改变我们解决问题的方式？这个领域仍在快速发展。我和我的研究团队对这个问题进行了长达一年的深入思考，我们正在开发的一些想法实际上相当疯狂。从研究的角度来看，如果我们把解决问题看作是一个四阶段的过程，我们必须问：GenAI将如何改变问题定义？它将如何改变解决方案的生成？它将如何改变选择和实施？我们还必须决定我们如何看待人工智能：我们是把人工智能当作一个知识有限但涉猎广泛的同学和队友，还是盲目地相信它是一个无所不知的神谕？

    从根本上说，像ChatGPT这样的GenAI模型是基于下一个标记（token）预测来运行的。它们根据哪些词汇组合在它们的训练数据中最频繁地一起出现来生成文本。这与我们自己大脑中神经连接的工作方式高度相似——如果概念在过去的文本数据中被紧密连接，人工智能就会预测它们在输出中也应该被连接。了解了这一点，我们必须评估它在人工智能出现之前的解决问题各个阶段的效用。在没有人工智能的世界里，在问题定义阶段，我们严重依赖于诸如构建MECE（相互独立，完全穷尽）议题树等手动工具来逻辑地分解范围。在解决方案生成阶段，我们依赖于人类的孵化和头脑风暴。

    当我最初探索GenAI的能力时，我感到恐慌。老实说，我以为人工智能瞬间生成议题树的能力会让我的大部分课程大纲变得完全无关紧要。在某种程度上，这是真的，但这里面有一个深刻的反转。这个反转涉及到“幻觉”的概念。在人工智能领域，幻觉——即模型自信地捏造事实——通常被认为是一个可怕的缺陷。但回想一下我们自己的孵化过程。当我们做梦或进行头脑风暴时，我们的大脑实际上也在产生幻觉。我们将怪异的、完全不合理的事物联系在一起，以寻找新颖的解决方案。我意识到，对于解决方案生成阶段，我们实际上可能想*迫使*人工智能产生幻觉。如果我们故意促使它进行疯狂的、不受约束的联想，虽然完全清楚它99%的输出都将是绝对的垃圾，但那1%可能正是我们需要的那种非凡的、创造性的远距离联想。

    此外，看看“提示工程（Prompt Engineering）”这门学科。你在领英（LinkedIn）上看到所有这些精心设计的框架，声称要彻底改变你与人工智能对话的方式——那些首字母缩略词告诉你指定角色、定义背景、建立目标并列出约束条件。如果你仔细观察这些花哨的框架，它们与传统、严谨的“问题定义”绝对完全相同。当你被聘为顾问时，你会建立背景，定义利益相关者的角色，指定目标结果，并澄清边界。优秀的提示工程仅仅就是优秀的问题定义。从根本上来说没有任何区别。

  - [19:18 - 19:23] **AI and the Evolution of Issue Trees**

    Finally, we must address the traditional MECE issue tree. For your upcoming exams, building a highly logical, mutually exclusive, and collectively exhaustive issue tree is very difficult. It challenges you to become an expert in a problem context and break down every logical cause. Why do we do this manually? Because the issue tree provides a comprehensive checklist that we can take back to our specific problem context to verify step by step. A manual issue tree maps the explicit logical linkages between a symptom and its root causes.

    Now, if you go to ChatGPT and ask, "What are the possible causes of low profit in a theme park?"—which was one of our early case studies—it will instantly give you a highly comprehensive list. It will tell you that the common causes are separated into demand side and revenue side, such as low attendance, bad pricing strategies, F&B operations, or merchandise sales. You can use this AI-generated list exactly like a checklist, going back to your context and verifying them one by one. In that sense, ChatGPT drastically reduces the need for you to manually brainstorm a perfect issue tree from scratch. It gives you the categories instantly.

    However, there is a crucial limitation. While the Large Language Model provides a phenomenal, comprehensive list of categories, it often cannot accurately map the deep, causal logic linking those categories together unless it has been explicitly trained on that exact specific scenario many times. A human building an issue tree understands exactly *why* and *how* A drives B. The AI just knows that A and B frequently appear together in text about theme parks. Using AI here is exactly like using a calculator to get the final answer without knowing the formula. The AI gives you the output, but it cannot show the manual arithmetic of the problem's architecture. It is an incredibly powerful tool for ensuring your checklist is exhaustive, but relying on it entirely means you forfeit the deep understanding of the problem's underlying mechanisms.

    最后，我们必须探讨传统的MECE（相互独立，完全穷尽）议题树。对于你们即将到来的考试来说，建立一个高度逻辑化、相互独立且完全穷尽的议题树是非常困难的。它挑战你成为问题情境下的专家，并分解出每一个符合逻辑的原因。我们为什么要手动做这个？因为议题树提供了一份全面的清单，我们可以将其带回到我们具体的问题情境中进行逐步验证。手动的议题树描绘了症状及其根本原因之间明确的逻辑联系。

    现在，如果你去问ChatGPT，“一个主题公园利润低下的可能原因是什么？”——这是我们早期的案例研究之一——它会立刻给你一个非常全面的列表。它会告诉你常见的原因分为需求侧和收入侧，比如入园人数少、定价策略糟糕、餐饮运营或商品销售问题。你可以完全像使用检查清单一样使用这个人工智能生成的列表，回到你的情境中逐一验证它们。从这个意义上说，ChatGPT大大减少了你从头开始手动头脑风暴出一个完美的议题树的需求。它瞬间为你提供了分类。

    然而，这存在一个关键的局限性。虽然大型语言模型提供了一个惊人的、全面的分类列表，但它通常无法准确描绘将这些分类联系在一起的深层因果逻辑，除非它在那种确切的特定场景中被多次明确训练过。构建议题树的人类清楚地理解A是*为什么*以及*如何*驱动B的。而人工智能只知道A和B经常在关于主题公园的文本中一起出现。在这里使用人工智能完全就像使用计算器直接得到最终答案而不知道公式一样。人工智能给你输出了结果，但它无法展示问题架构的“手工算术”过程。对于确保你的检查清单毫无遗漏来说，它是一个极其强大的工具，但完全依赖它意味着你放弃了对问题潜在机制的深入理解。
***

# assignment 1
## assignment 1 question
  DMAIC shines best when you’re not chasing vague “innovation” but trying to fix or improve an existing process that already runs but stumbles. It’s built for situations where problems are measurable, recurring, and data-rich—not one-off emergencies or blue-sky brainstorming.
  1. what kind of problems are DMAIC suitable for?

  2. what are the steps/stages involved? why are the stages named / labeled on such a manner?

  3. what defines a successful outcome for a particular stage? what pitfalls should be avoid?

  4. what are the typical tools used at the various stages? why are these tools selected? How do these tools assist the method?
## Q1. What kind of problems are DMAIC suitable for?
  - From the Sime Darby case you shared, DMAIC was applied to reduce **high chemical costs in wastewater treatment**—a problem that had:
    * **Clear business impact** (97% of variable cost came from chemicals, especially Alum and Caustic).
    * **Underlying complexity** (multiple root causes like influent pH, dosing practices, chemical pricing, and chemical type, none of which could be solved by a quick fix).
    * **Data availability** (historical cost and process data, pH readings, turbidity levels) that allowed for measurement, analysis, and validation of improvements.
  - So, in general, DMAIC is best for:
    * Reducing **defects, waste, or costs** in a measurable process.
    * Improving **efficiency, quality, or cycle times** where multiple factors influence performance.
    * Problems that require **structured root cause analysis** instead of intuition-based fixes.
    * Situations where improvements must be **sustainable and verified with data**.
  - It’s less suitable for brand-new process design (that’s what **DMADV** or design thinking is for), or for vague strategic issues without measurable metrics.
## Q2 / Q3 / Q4 in table answer
  | **Stage**   | **Purpose (Q2)**                                                                     | **Successful Outcome (Q3)**                                                                                                                                                            | **Pitfalls to Avoid (Q3)**                                                                                                    | **Typical Tools & Why (Q4)**                                                                                                                                                                                                                           |
  | ----------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
  | **Define**  | Clarify *what* problem to solve, why it matters, and who is involved.                | • Sharp problem statement tied to business impact (e.g. “cut cost from 57¢ to 47¢/m³”).<br>• Scope realistic (not too broad/narrow).<br>• Stakeholders aligned (charter, SIPOC, RACI). | • Vague goals (“make it cheaper”).<br>• Scope creep (trying to fix everything).<br>• No buy-in → resistance later.            | • **Project Charter** (scope, timeline).<br>• **SIPOC** (map suppliers–customers).<br>• **VOC/VOP** (align with needs).<br>• **Pareto/Selection Matrix** (pick biggest pain).                                                                          |
  | **Measure** | Establish *how bad* the problem is with reliable data; baseline current performance. | • Baseline data verified.<br>• Process variation quantified (e.g. chemical cost distribution at 57¢/m³).<br>• Measurement system consistent (no operator bias).                        | • Bad/biased data.<br>• Collecting too few data points.<br>• Cherry-picking to fit assumptions.                               | • **Process Mapping/Gemba Walks** (see workflow).<br>• **Control Charts** (check stability).<br>• **Capability Analysis (Cp, Cpk)** (process vs. specs).<br>• **MSA/Gage R\&R** (validate measurement system).<br>• **Histograms** (see distribution). |
  | **Analyze** | Dig into data to uncover *why* the problem exists.                                   | • Root causes validated by data (not guesses).<br>• Prioritization of vital few causes.<br>• Cause–effect proven (e.g. influent pH drives caustic cost).                               | • Jumping to solutions prematurely.<br>• Confusing correlation with causation.<br>• Blaming individuals instead of processes. | • **5 Whys, Brainstorming** (surface causes).<br>• **Fishbone Diagram** (categorize).<br>• **Cause & Effect Matrix** (rank causes).<br>• **FMEA** (rank by risk).<br>• **Statistical tests** (regression, correlation, hypothesis).                    |
  | **Improve** | Create and test solutions to eliminate root causes.                                  | • Solutions linked to root causes (e.g. alum dosing, tannin-based chemical).<br>• Pilot/DOE validates changes.<br>• ROI/cost-benefit proven feasible.                                  | • Pet projects (opinion-driven).<br>• Over-engineering fixes.<br>• Ignoring side effects (fix cost but ruin quality).         | • **Brainstorming + Affinity** (generate solutions).<br>• **Solution/Pay-Off Matrix** (rank by effort vs. benefit).<br>• **DOE** (test factors).<br>• **Pugh Matrix** (compare alternatives).<br>• **Pilot Runs** (trial before rollout).              |
  | **Control** | Lock in the gains; prevent process drift.                                            | • SOPs, checklists, training updated.<br>• Monitoring system in place (SPC, dashboards).<br>• Benefits verified (finance confirms savings).<br>• Gains sustained beyond project team.  | • “One and done” → no monitoring.<br>• No updated SOPs/training (knowledge loss).<br>• Celebrating savings on paper only.     | • **Control Plans/Checklists** (who/what/when).<br>• **SOP Updates** (institutionalize change).<br>• **SPC Charts** (detect drift).<br>• **Dashboards/Audits** (track performance).<br>• **Training & Handover** (ownership).                          |
## Q2. What are the steps/stages involved? Why are they named in such a manner?
  - DMAIC is like a scientific expedition into the jungle of messy business processes. Its five stages — **Define, Measure, Analyze, Improve, Control** — are named in such a way because each one represents a logical, step-by-step way of turning a vague problem into a data-backed, sustainable solution. The names are deliberately plain verbs—each one signals *what the team must do at that stage*.

  - **1. Define**
    * *What happens*: The team defines the problem, scope, goals, and stakeholders. A project charter and high-level process map (like SIPOC: Supplier–Input–Process–Output–Customer) are created.
    * *Why the name*: It forces you to nail down the “what exactly” before running into “how.” Without definition, improvement efforts drift.
  - **2. Measure**
    * *What happens*: Data is collected to establish a baseline of current performance. Tools like control charts, Pareto analysis, or capability analysis are used to check variation and defects.
    * *Why the name*: You cannot improve what you cannot measure. This stage turns complaints (“too expensive!”) into numbers (“57 cents/m³ of treated water”).
  - **3. Analyze**
    * *What happens*: The team digs into data to uncover root causes of the problem. Methods like Fishbone diagrams, 5 Whys, regression analysis, and FMEA (Failure Modes & Effects Analysis) are used.
    * *Why the name*: You already know “what” and “how much” — now you must know “why.” This prevents jumping to solutions without evidence.
  - **4. Improve**
    * *What happens*: Solutions are designed and tested to eliminate root causes. Tools like brainstorming, affinity diagrams, DOE (Design of Experiments), solution matrices, and pilot testing are used.
    * *Why the name*: It’s where change actually happens — you’re no longer studying the disease, you’re administering the cure.
  - **5. Control**
    * *What happens*: Systems are put in place to sustain the gains — control plans, updated SOPs, statistical process control, monitoring dashboards.
    * *Why the name*: Without control, processes slip back into chaos. Control locks in the improvements so you don’t fight the same fire twice.
## Q3. What defines a successful outcome for a particular stage? What pitfalls should be avoided?
  - **1. Define**
    - **Successful outcome:**
      * Clear problem statement tied to business impact (e.g. “Reduce WWTP chemical cost from 57¢ to 47¢ per m³ by June 2016”).
      * Well-scoped project (not too broad, not too narrow).
      * Stakeholders and roles identified (RACI matrix, SIPOC).
    - **Pitfalls to avoid:**
      * Vague goals (“make things cheaper” instead of measurable targets).
      * Taking on too big a scope (trying to “fix everything in the refinery” rather than just the WWTP).
      * Missing stakeholder buy-in (without it, resistance will sink the project later).
  - **2. Measure**
    - **Successful outcome:**
      * Reliable baseline data collected (control charts, capability analysis).
      * Process variation understood (e.g. knowing chemical cost was normally distributed around 57¢/m³).
      * Valid measurement system in place (operators measure the same way, no bias).
    - **Pitfalls to avoid:**
      * Garbage data (measuring inconsistently or with faulty instruments).
      * Rushing into analysis without enough data points.
      * Cherry-picking data that supports a preconceived solution.
  - **3. Analyze**
    - **Successful outcome:**
      * Root causes identified and validated with evidence (not guesses). Sime Darby, for example, proved that influent pH directly drove higher caustic use.
      * Prioritization done (via Cause & Effect Matrix, FMEA).
      * “Vital few” causes separated from “trivial many.”
    - **Pitfalls to avoid:**
      * Jumping to solutions too early (“Let’s just buy cheaper chemicals!” before root cause analysis).
      * Confusing correlation with causation.
    * Blaming people instead of processes (“operators are careless” vs “sensor placement is flawed”).
  - **4. Improve**
    - **Successful outcome:**
      * Solutions directly linked to validated root causes (e.g. optimizing alum dosage, changing supplier, switching to tannin-based chemical).
      * Solutions tested and validated (DOE, pilot runs, pay-off matrix).
      * Cost-benefit analysis shows value and feasibility.
    - **Pitfalls to avoid:**
      * “Pet projects” (solutions based on opinion, not data).
      * Over-engineering (creating complex solutions when simple process tweaks work).
      * Ignoring side effects (a cheaper chemical that ruins water quality isn’t a win).
  - **5. Control**
    - **Successful outcome:**
      * Controls in place: monitoring charts, SOP updates, training, audits.
      * Benefits sustained beyond the project team (institutionalized into the process).
      * Project formally closed with validated financial and performance gains (Sime Darby: 41k USD/year savings, ROI 992%, payback in 1 month).
    - **Pitfalls to avoid:**
      * “One and done” mentality—no monitoring, so the process drifts back.
      * Not updating SOPs or training (knowledge dies when the team moves on).
      * Celebrating savings on paper only, without verification by finance or auditors.
## Q4. What are the typical tools used at the various stages? Why are these tools selected? How do these tools assist the method?
  - **1. Define – Frame the problem**
    - **Typical tools:**
      * **Project Charter** – defines scope, goals, timeline.
      * **SIPOC (Supplier–Input–Process–Output–Customer)** – maps the process at a high level.
      * **Voice of the Customer (VOC) / Voice of the Process (VOP)** – ensures goals match customer/business needs.
      * **Pareto Analysis / Project Selection Matrix** – helps choose the problem with biggest impact.
    - **Why selected:** They turn “we think X is a problem” into “we know Y is the biggest pain, with Z impact.”
    - **How they assist:** Provide clarity and prioritization, align the team, and secure buy-in before heavy lifting starts.
  - **2. Measure – Baseline the process**
    - **Typical tools:**
      * **Process Mapping / Gemba Walks** – visualize current workflow, spot data collection points.
      * **Control Charts (I-MR, Xbar-R)** – check process stability.
      * **Capability Analysis (Cp, Cpk, Sigma level)** – assess how well process meets specs.
      * **Measurement System Analysis (MSA, Gage R\&R)** – verify measurement reliability.
      * **Graphical Summary / Histograms** – understand data distribution.
    - **Why selected:** You need trustworthy numbers before analysis.
    - **How they assist:** Transform gut feelings into hard evidence, prove whether variation is real or just noise.
  - **3. Analyze – Find the root cause**
    - **Typical tools:**
      * **Brainstorming & 5 Whys** – generate possible causes.
      * **Fishbone (Ishikawa) Diagram** – categorize causes into Man, Machine, Method, Material, Measurement, Mother Nature.
      * **Cause & Effect Matrix** – prioritize causes with biggest impact.
      * **FMEA (Failure Modes & Effects Analysis)** – score risks by severity, occurrence, detectability.
      * **Hypothesis Testing / Regression / Correlation** – statistically prove relationships (e.g. low pH ↔ higher caustic cost).
    - **Why selected:** Humans love to blame the wrong thing; these tools separate “likely suspects” from “true culprits.”
    - **How they assist:** They force evidence-based thinking, filtering dozens of guesses into a handful of proven root causes.
  - **4. Improve – Fix the root cause**
    - **Typical tools:**
      * **Brainstorming + Affinity Diagram** – generate and group possible solutions.
      * **Solution Matrix / Pay-Off Matrix** – rank solutions by benefit vs. effort.
      * **Design of Experiments (DOE)** – test factor interactions and optimize settings.
      * **Pugh Matrix** – compare alternatives against criteria (used for chemical selection).
      * **Pilot Runs / Prototyping** – validate before full rollout.
    - **Why selected:** Solutions must be chosen rationally, not emotionally.
    - **How they assist:** They ensure improvements are both effective and feasible, balancing cost, quality, and effort.
  - **5. Control – Sustain the gains**
    - **Typical tools:**
      * **Control Plans & Checklists** – document what to do, when, by whom.
      * **Standard Operating Procedures (SOP) Updates** – institutionalize changes.
      * **Statistical Process Control (SPC) Charts** – ongoing monitoring for drift.
      * **Audits & Dashboards (SAP, Excel trackers)** – validate sustained performance.
      * **Training & Handover** – ensure operators own the new way.
    - **Why selected:** Improvements decay if not reinforced.
    - **How they assist:** They lock in success, making the new way “the normal way.”
***

# assignment 2
## Comparison Table of DMAIC, BPR, and Design Thinking
  | Dimension                     | **DMAIC**                                         | **BPR**                                                      | **Design Thinking**                                  | **Conclusion / Observation Across All Three**                                                                                                     |
  | ----------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
  | **Context / Purpose**         |                                                   |                                                              |                                                      |                                                                                                                                                   |
  | Problems designed for         | Existing processes with measurable inefficiencies | Outdated or fragmented processes requiring radical redesign  | Ambiguous, human-centered challenges                 | All address “problems,” but scope differs: continuous improvement (DMAIC), radical change (BPR), or fuzzy/ill-defined (Design Thinking). **(G4)** |
  | Organizational level          | Operational / shopfloor                           | Enterprise-wide, cross-functional                            | Product/service innovation, user-facing              | They work at **different organizational levels**: bottom-up, top-down, and outside-in. **(G4)**                                                   |
  | Who drives it                 | Frontline teams, quality engineers                | Senior management, consultants                               | Designers, multidisciplinary teams, end-users        | Each has a **different driver**: workers (DMAIC), leaders (BPR), users/designers (DT). **(G5)**                                                   |
  | Scale of change               | Incremental (5–10%)                               | Radical (>50%)                                               | Innovative / exploratory                             | Scale runs on a spectrum: **small → radical → novel**. **(G6)**                                                                                   |
  | Risks / trade-offs            | May miss strategic issues                         | High disruption, resistance, cost                            | Uncertain ROI, iterative failures                    | Trade-off between **certainty vs. disruption vs. ambiguity tolerance**. **(G6)**                                                                  |
  | Sustainability of outcomes    | High if culture embraces quality control          | Risky unless cultural buy-in achieved                        | Sustainable if user-centric mindset persists         | Sustainability depends on embedding into **culture, not just project success**. **(G8)**                                                          |
  | Hypothesis statement          | If measurable inputs change, process improves     | If processes are redesigned, performance improves radically  | If we understand users deeply, solutions will work   | All have **testable hypotheses**, but in **different evidence domains**: data (DMAIC), performance (BPR), user validation (DT). **(G3)**          |
  | Hypothesis test               | Statistical significance, control charts          | Benchmarks and post-implementation performance               | User testing & feedback                              | Each uses a **different validation lens**: numbers, outcomes, or users. **(G3)**                                                                  |
  | **Content / Approach**        |                                                   |                                                              |                                                      |                                                                                                                                                   |
  | Steps / stages involved       | Define → Measure → Analyze → Improve → Control    | Envision → Diagnose → Redesign → Implement → Evaluate        | Empathize → Define → Ideate → Prototype → Test       | All are **stage-based frameworks** with structured flows. **(G1)**                                                                                |
  | Why stages are named that way | Action verbs for systematic, measurable progress  | Strategic verbs for organizational transformation            | Human-centered verbs for creative exploration        | Names reflect their **philosophical roots**: science, strategy, human experience. **(G2)**                                                        |
  | Sequence vs. iteration        | Mostly linear, limited iteration                  | Semi-linear, can reorder under pressure                      | Highly iterative                                     | Degree of iteration: low (DMAIC), medium (BPR), high (DT). **(G7)**                                                                               |
  | Good outcome at each stage    | Clear data, validated root cause, stable process  | Shared vision, effective redesigned process, adoption        | Validated insights, workable prototypes, user buy-in | Success = stage-specific milestones → all stress **measurable progress**. **(G1)**                                                                |
  | Pitfalls                      | Wrong problem, poor data, premature solutions     | Over-focus on IT, ignoring people, cultural pushback         | Shallow empathy, over-polished prototypes            | Pitfalls reflect their **biases**: too narrow (DMAIC), too disruptive (BPR), too fuzzy (DT). **(G5)**                                             |
  | Typical tools                 | Process map, Fishbone, Pareto, control charts     | Value chain analysis, benchmarking, flowcharting, simulation | Journey maps, interviews, observation, prototyping   | Tools differ but serve same purpose: **structure thinking & reduce bias**. **(G2)**                                                               |
  | Why tools are used            | To quantify and verify                            | To visualize, benchmark, and rethink radically               | To uncover needs, ideate, and validate               | Tools match each method’s DNA: numbers, strategy, human insights. **(G2)**                                                                        |
  | How tools support philosophy  | Data-driven continuous improvement                | Radical, systemic rethinking                                 | Human-centered, iterative learning                   | Tools **operationalize the philosophy** of each method. **(G2)**                                                                                  |
## Consolidated Guiding Principles of Problem-Solving
  **G1. Good problem-solving follows a process**

  * All methods emphasize structured stages. Skipping steps risks failure.
  * Iteration is useful, but discipline ensures clarity and learning.

  **G2. Each stage has a distinct focus, supported by tools**

  * Stages have different goals: defining, diagnosing, ideating, validating.
  * Tools sharpen thinking but should not become ends in themselves.

  **G3. Decisions must be evidence-based**

  * DMAIC: quantitative data.
  * BPR: benchmarks and business cases.
  * Design Thinking: user research and prototypes.
  * Evidence can be numbers or stories — validation matters more than assumption.

  **G4. Match method to problem context**

  * Incremental problems → DMAIC.
  * Radical transformation → BPR.
  * Ambiguous, user-centered innovation → Design Thinking.
  * No single method is best; fit depends on context and risk appetite.

  **G5. People and culture are central**

  * Workers sustain DMAIC, leadership drives BPR, users shape Design Thinking.
  * Stakeholder buy-in is essential; culture embeds sustainability.

  **G6. Balance risk and reward**

  * DMAIC: low risk, steady gains.
  * BPR: high risk, high return.
  * Design Thinking: uncertain risk, creative payoff.
  * Smart problem-solvers choose consciously where to act.

  **G7. Embrace iteration and learning**

  * DMAIC: feedback loops in Control.
  * BPR: refine after implementation.
  * Design Thinking: prototype-test cycles.
  * Universal cycle: **Plan → Act → Learn → Adjust**.

  **G8. Sustainability is the ultimate goal**

  * Improvement must become habit, not a one-off.
  * Embed solutions into routines, incentives, and culture.

  **Consolidated Advice:**

  - Effective problem-solving is structured and evidence-driven, but also flexible enough to fit context. Tools support thinking, people ensure adoption, and culture sustains results. Each method has limits, so a generic methodology should balance rigor (DMAIC), boldness (BPR), and empathy (Design Thinking).
## Generic Problem-Solving Methodology
  **1. Frame the Problem (Define & Contextualize)**

  * Clarify *what is the problem* and *why it matters*.
  * Understand scope (incremental vs radical vs exploratory).
  * Gather voices: stakeholders, users, business goals.
  * Output: clear **problem statement + hypotheses**.
    → Inspired by **DMAIC’s Define**, **BPR’s Envision**, **Design Thinking’s Empathize/Define**.

  **2. Gather & Interpret Evidence (Measure & Understand)**

  * Collect relevant data:

    * **Quantitative** (metrics, KPIs, costs, performance).
    * **Qualitative** (user stories, observations, emotions).
  * Map processes / journeys / systems.
  * Output: **baseline + diagnosis of gaps**.
    → Inspired by **DMAIC’s Measure**, **BPR’s Diagnose**, **Design Thinking’s Observe/Empathize**.

  **3. Analyze Causes & Opportunities (Diagnose & Reframe)**

  * Use structured tools: fishbone, root cause analysis, benchmarking, journey maps.
  * Ask “Why?” and “What if?” to avoid solving symptoms.
  * Reframe problem if evidence suggests deeper issues.
  * Output: **validated root causes + reframed challenge statement**.
    → Inspired by **DMAIC’s Analyze**, **BPR’s Why/Ideal Way**, **Design Thinking’s Define**.

  **4. Generate & Develop Solutions (Ideate & Design)**

  * Brainstorm widely → quantity leads to quality.
  * Use creativity tools, scenario building, cross-functional workshops.
  * Shortlist ideas based on impact vs effort.
  * Output: **solution concepts or prototypes**.
    → Inspired by **DMAIC’s Improve**, **BPR’s Redesign**, **Design Thinking’s Ideate**.

  **5. Test & Validate (Prototype & Pilot)**

  * Run pilots, simulations, experiments.
  * Gather feedback from users, stakeholders, and data.
  * Refine solutions based on results.
  * Output: **validated solutions ready for scaling**.
    → Inspired by **Design Thinking’s Prototype/Test**, **DMAIC’s Improve validation**, **BPR’s Reconstruct/Evaluate**.

  **6. Implement & Embed (Control & Sustain)**

  * Deploy final solutions at scale.
  * Set up control systems: dashboards, SOPs, cultural reinforcements.
  * Ensure adoption: training, incentives, communication.
  * Output: **sustained improvement embedded into culture**.
    → Inspired by **DMAIC’s Control**, **BPR’s Reconstruct/Evaluate**, **Design Thinking’s Implementation**.
***
